Summary

“You know,” she said, tilting her head slightly, “I’m sorry about the coffee. Seriously. But maybe you should take a breath next time before jumping down someone’s throat. Could be good for your blood pressure.”
Agatha froze. Her pulse quickened, her face hardening.
“Maybe you should mind your business,” Agatha shot back, stepping closer, her eyes narrowed with a newfound sharpness. “I didn’t ask for your opinion.”
Dr Vidal didn’t flinch, though her lips curled into a knowing smirk. “Okay, then. I guess I’ll just keep walking, try not to get in your way.” She started to move past Agatha, but before she could completely step around her, she paused.
“And for the record,” she added with a mischievous glint in her eye, “if you're having a terrible day, maybe don't make it everyone else’s problem. Just a thought.”
With that, she turned on her heel and walked away, her footsteps light and confident, leaving Agatha standing there, fuming.

 

or Dr. Agatha Harkness, reeling from a messy divorce, relocates across the country with her child to start from scratch. There, she meets the infuriating yet brilliant Dr. Vidal, who challenges her in ways she never imagined.

Chapter 1

Most days, Agatha Harkness moved through the world with an unshakable certainty in her own invincibility.

She was untouchable and revered. Her life was meticulously constructed on a foundation of unyielding rules and hard won respect. No one dared to challenge her or chip away at the fortress she had built around herself through relentless hard work. 

But today, the universe seemed determined to remind her that even the strongest walls could quake.

Agatha stared at the glossy hospital sign, Mayo Clinic , as sunlight glinted off its pristine lettering from the seat of her car. This was it. Her first day as Chief Neonatal Surgeon in a new city, far from the life she’d painstakingly constructed and subsequently watched crumble.

From the comfort of her car she couldn’t help but wonder how she had ended up here. 

Agatha’s move had been less a choice and more a necessity, a bid for survival after the implosion of her carefully curated life. Her divorce had been a spectacular disaster, the kind that made even her closest friends recoil from the sheer wreckage of it all.

It started unraveling, slow and subtle. Her husband, Daniel, had always been charming; a literature professor with a disarming smile and a knack for storytelling that had captivated her years ago. But over time, his charm gave way to resentment. She worked long hours as one of the top neonatal surgeons in NYU and her ambition seemed to needle him in ways neither of them could articulate.

What began as arguments about late nights and missed dinners escalated into something darker. Daniel grew bitter, accusing her of valuing her career over their marriage, over him, over their kid. 

The final blow came when she discovered the affair. It wasn’t the affair itself, she had long suspected something, but the brazenness of it. He had brought the woman, a graduate student barely older than their marriage into their home.

The divorce proceedings were venomous, with Daniel demanding a ludicrous settlement. claiming that her relentless dedication to her career had left him a lonely man raising their son Nicholas practically on his own. The custody battle was the worst of it. Agatha had always known she wasn’t the stereotypical mother, she couldn’t make school drop offs or bake cookies for fundraisers, but she loved her son fiercely. By the time the dust settled, Agatha had lost more than her marriage. She lost the brownstone they’d renovated together, the antique furniture she’d handpicked, and the city she had called home for over two decades. Staying in New York felt untenable; the reminders of her old life were everywhere, suffocating her.

In the end, she was granted joint custody, but the arrangement was fragile. Daniel made snide comments about her being too busy or distracted whenever he handed Nicholas over. Agatha felt like a guest in her own son’s life, her time with Nicky carved out of an already overburdened schedule.

The divorce had been finalized six months ago when the offer from Mayo Clinic came, it felt like a lifeline. Minnesota, with its sprawling landscapes and unfamiliar pace, was as far removed from Manhattan as she could imagine. It was a place to rebuild, to start over, not as the betrayed wife or the workaholic surgeon but as someone new. A new life for her and Nicky, a new beginning for them both.

The hospital loomed tall and sterile before her, a stark contrast to the historic urban hospital she had left behind. It reeked of new beginnings, with a tinge of antiseptic and unfamiliarity. Agatha adjusted her white coat, already tailored to perfection, and squared her shoulders.

Inside, the halls buzzed with the typical morning rush. Residents darted between patients, and nurses exchanged hurried updates. Her shoes clicked against the tile as she strode toward the neonatal unit, eyes forward, posture impeccable.

"Dr. Harkness?"

The voice startled her only slightly, and she turned to see a young woman in scrubs standing at attention. Her badge read Jennifer Kale R.N.

"Yes?" Agatha’s voice was even, her professional mask firmly in place.

"I’m the charge nurse for the NICU," Jennifer said, her tone bright but edged with nerves. "Dr. Calderu asked me to show you around before rounds”

“And why isn’t she here to welcome me?” She said

Jennifer stiffened at Agatha's sharp tone, the smile on her face faltering. "Dr. Calderu had an emergency to attend to," she explained, her voice faltering slightly under Agatha's scrutiny. "She asked me to show you around before rounds. She’ll see you after the rounds."

Agatha’s lips thinned as she stared at Jennifer, the weight of her gaze making the nurse shift uncomfortably. "I was led to believe the chief of surgery would be here to greet me, not a charge nurse." Her words were clipped, a hint of disdain slipping through.

“I— I understand,” Jennifer stammered, trying to regain composure. “Dr. Calderu is very busy, but I’m happy to show you around in the meantime.”

Agatha’s eyes narrowed, clearly dissatisfied with the situation. "Fine. But I expect this to be rectified immediately," she said, her voice cold, and turned her attention to the bustling NICU, her impatience palpable.

Jennifer nodded quickly, trying to remain calm. "Of course, Dr. Harkness. Please, follow me."

The tension lingered as Agatha followed, her expression still tight with annoyance.

"Very well," Agatha replied. She followed her through the labyrinth of hallways, mentally cataloging every turn and department. The NICU was spacious, lined with incubators and soft beeping monitors. It smelled of clean linens and faintly of hope, a smell Agatha had always associated with her work.

As Jennifer rattled off statistics about the unit’s capacity and recent successes, Agatha’s gaze fell on a young couple huddled near an incubator, their hands pressed to the glass. Their infant, impossibly small, stretched its delicate fingers as if testing the air. Agatha’s chest tightened, not with sentimentality, but with purpose. This was why she was here. This was what she excelled at.

The charge nurse’s voice broke through her thoughts. "I hope you’ll like it here, Dr. Harkness. We’re all excited to work with you."

"I don’t need them to like me," Agatha thought, her lips curving into a faint, polite smile. "I need them to keep up."

"Thank you, Nurse Kale," she said aloud, her tone clipped but not unkind. "I’ll be ready for rounds in fifteen minutes"

Jennifer nodded and scurried off, leaving Agatha alone in the middle of her new kingdom. She adjusted her coat again, letting her fingers briefly brush the amulet hidden beneath it, a habit she hadn’t yet broken.

This wasn’t the life she had envisioned years ago, but it was the life she had now. And for all its unknowns, there was one thing she could rely on: the work. The work had never betrayed her.

Agatha stood in the quiet of the hallway, her reflection flickering faintly on the polished glass of an observation window. The NICU buzzed softly behind her, the rhythmic hum of machines a steady backdrop to her thoughts. This wasn’t just a new chapter, it was an entirely new book, and she was determined to write it with precision, with control, with success as she always did.

Her phone buzzed in her pocket, jolting her from her reverie. She pulled it out to see a photo of Nicholas, his gap-toothed grin filling the screen. He was holding up a crayon drawing of what looked like a dragon, its body a swirl of colors, with stick figures on either side. One labeled Me wore a green cape, and the other labeled Mom had impossibly tall hair and a tiny stethoscope.

Agatha couldn’t help the small smile that tugged at her lips. Nicholas was four, with a boundless imagination and an uncanny ability to make her feel both invincible and utterly vulnerable. The divorce had hit him in ways she hadn’t fully anticipated. He’d gone from being a bubbly, chatty toddler to a child who sometimes stared out windows longer than she thought any four-year-old should.

The move to Minnesota was supposed to be a balm for both of them, a way to step out of the shadows of what they’d lost. But Agatha worried about him constantly. Would he settle into the new preschool? Make friends? Did he feel like she’d uprooted his life just as much as Daniel had fractured hers?

The message attached to the photo was from his nanny, Claire.

"First masterpiece in Minnesota! He said it’s you and him fighting a snow dragon. Drop-off went great this morning. He misses you already."

Agatha’s fingers hovered over the keyboard for a moment before typing back:

"Tell him I miss him too. And that the snow dragon doesn’t stand a chance."

She slipped the phone back into her pocket, her professional mask sliding effortlessly into place as she turned toward the door to the staff lounge. She couldn’t dwell. Nicholas needed her to succeed here, to prove this move wasn’t a mistake.

Fifteen minutes later, she was in the NICU, leading her first rounds. Nurses and residents flanked her, their expressions a mixture of awe and apprehension. Agatha’s reputation preceded her, and she could feel the weight of their expectations pressing down on her like a heavy mantle.

Her gaze swept over the team, noting the subtle shifts in posture, the way some of the younger residents avoided eye contact, while others tried to mimic her calm, commanding presence. She thrived on this, on the sharpness of their gazes, the undercurrent of fear mixed with respect. It wasn’t just respect. It was awe. They feared her, and Agatha had always known how to make that fear work in her favor.

She reveled in the power it gave her, that ability to command a room, to make people see her as an unyielding force. It had taken years to build that reputation, but now, it was hers to wield. And as she moved from incubator to incubator, her eyes calculating, her voice crisp with authority, she felt a surge of satisfaction. They were watching. They were listening. And she would make sure they knew that she was the one in charge.

Agatha paused, glancing down at a small preemie in one of the incubators, her expression softening for just a moment. But it was only for a moment. She had a role to play, and that role was one of unwavering control. She straightened, turning to the resident closest to her. “Tell me about the baby’s progress,” she demanded, her voice cool and unwavering.

The young doctor stammered, fumbling through notes, but Agatha didn’t give him a second to recover. “Speak clearly. I expect more than a summary. I want details—actual data,” she snapped.

The resident quickly straightened, eyes wide. “Y-yes, Dr. Harkness. The baby’s heart rate is stable, but there’s concern about the oxygen levels—”

“Good,” Agatha cut him off, her voice already moving on to the next patient. “But it’s not enough. You’ll adjust the ventilator settings and start an arterial blood gas. Get it right.”

She moved swiftly through the rounds, her team scrambling to keep up, each word from her a razor-sharp instruction. They might fear her, but Agatha knew she was molding them into the team she needed. Every second, every decision was an opportunity to prove her expertise. And no one would question her authority, not now, not ever.

She moved from incubator to incubator, assessing charts, asking sharp, pointed questions. Occasionally, she glanced at the infants, tiny, fragile beings whose survival depended on her expertise and the team she now commanded. She felt the familiar surge of focus, the calm that came with knowing she was in her element.

As the rounds drew to a close, she could feel the shift in the room. The team had settled into a rhythm, their earlier nerves giving way to a kind of practiced obedience. Agatha’s presence was undeniable, her leadership absolute. 

She turned to the last resident of the day, her gaze piercing. “I expect a full report on each of these cases by the end of the day,” she said, her voice as cold as ever. “And don't think I won’t know if you’ve missed something.” She paused, making sure they understood the weight of her words. “Now go. And do it right.”

With that, she turned on her heel, her back straight, her eyes scanning the rest of the NICU as she moved toward the exit. She was untouchable. They would learn quickly, Agatha Harkness didn’t make mistakes. And neither should they.

Later, as she sat in her new office, surrounded by stacks of unopened files and the faint glow of her computer screen, she looked at the photo of Nicholas again. The drawing of the snow dragon felt oddly prophetic. Life here would be a battle, a fight against uncertainty, against the lingering specter of her divorce, against her own self doubt.

But she didn’t back down from battles. Not in the operating room. Not in life. 

And certainly not for her son.

Agatha straightened her desk, ensuring the papers and files were in precise order. Her first day had already been a whirlwind, and now she had her introductory meeting with the hospital's Surgical Chief, Dr. Lilia Calderu. She’d heard the name in passing during her career, an accomplished transplant surgeon, known for her bold techniques and her equal flair for hospital politics.

The sharp knock on her office door came just as she adjusted a framed photo of Nicholas on the corner of her desk.

"Come in," she called, her tone brisk but polite.

The door opened to reveal a short, broad shouldered woman with dark hair with silver at the temples. Her piercing hazel eyes took in the room as he stepped inside. She carried himself with the casual confidence of someone used to command, but there was a warmth in her expression that caught her off guard.

"Dr. Harkness," she said, extending a hand. "Lilia Calderu. Welcome to Mayo. I’m sorry I miss your arrival this morning, emergency surgery. You know how it is”

She stood and shook her hand, her grip firm. "Thank you, Dr. Calderu. It’s a pleasure to finally meet you."

"Lilia," she corrected, her smile easy. "Titles can get exhausting around here, don’t you think?"

Agatha resisted the urge to raise an eyebrow. She had spent years in the highly hierarchical world of New York medicine, where first names were reserved for close colleagues or the rare moments of camaraderie. But Minnesota seemed intent on presenting a different way of doing things.

"Lilia, then," she said, allowing herself a small, polite and controlled smile.

"Please, sit with me" she said, gesturing toward the sofa that was on the corner of the office.

Agatha left her desk and took the seat carefully, her posture still impeccable despite the casual invitation. She crossed her legs, placing her hands neatly on her lap, and scanned the office with a practiced eye. It was sleek, well organized, but had a certain warmth to it that felt somewhat foreign to her. 

 

Lilia Calderu took her seat across from her, folding her hands in front of her. “ Your reputation precedes you, and let me tell you, it’s not easy to impress the folks up in recruitment. I hope your first rounds went well,” she said with a smile, her tone warm but laced with curiosity.

Agatha didn’t allow herself to soften at the tone. Instead, she met her gaze with a level stare, her voice crisp. “They went as expected. There’s always a bit of... adjustment when stepping into a new environment. The residents are all over the place and I hope I can mold them to be better”

Lilia’s smile faltered for just a moment, but she quickly regained her composure and nodded thoughtfully. She studied Agatha, sensing the sharpness in her words, the drive that was both formidable and, perhaps, a little daunting. “I’m sure you will,” she said smoothly, her tone still warm but carrying a slight edge of caution. “The residents here are bright, but I understand the desire to shape them. Just remember, this environment thrives on collaboration, not command.”

“Of course.” Agatha’s response was clipped, the slight tension still lingering in the room. She wasn’t here for small talk, and she certainly wasn’t here to discuss her personal adjustment. But Lilia seemed content to maintain the friendly tone, her smile never wavering.

“So, how’s the team? They seem eager to have you on board,” Lilia remarked, leaning back in her chair slightly. “But I’m sure you’ve already noticed the culture shift here. Mayo has a reputation for being... collaborative.”

Agatha’s gaze flickered for a brief moment, then she straightened herself, meeting Lilia’s eyes again. “Collaborative?” she repeated, her tone carefully neutral. She had heard whispers about Mayo’s culture, about the idea of working together, sharing decisions, offering input. It was a world apart from the highly competitive, cutthroat environment she was used to.

Lilia’s smile softened slightly, as if she were aware of the challenge that this might present to Agatha. “Yes, collaborative. We believe in bringing together a diverse set of voices, pooling our expertise to ensure the best care for our patients. I’m sure it’s an adjustment, but I have no doubt you’ll thrive in this environment.”

Agatha’s fingers twitched, betraying a flicker of discomfort. She had always trusted her own instincts, her own decisions. The idea of relying on a collective group, of yielding to others in any way, made her uneasy.

“I’m used to running a team," she said, her voice steady but firm. “But I’m adaptable.”

Lilia’s smile widened just a little, sensing the undercurrent of tension. “Good. Because here, we value every opinion, whether it’s from a nurse, a resident, or a senior attending. No one is ever truly alone in their decisions.”

Agatha’s eyes narrowed ever so slightly, but she masked it quickly, nodding in acknowledgment. “Understood. I’ll do my part to integrate.”

Lilia sat up a little straighter, the conversation shifting as she took a breath. “I wanted to make sure we’re clear on expectations. I know you're here to make an immediate impact, and I want you to feel empowered to lead in your own way. But you should know that at Mayo, it's less about commanding and more about leading.”

Agatha’s lip twitched as she processed the words. Leading, not commanding. She wasn’t sure how to feel about that. She had led with precision and authority for so long, the idea of adapting to a more... nuanced style didn’t sit entirely well with her.

“I appreciate the clarification, Lilia,” she said slowly. “And I’ll keep that in mind.”

Lilia’s eyes glinted with something like understanding, though her smile remained warm. “I’m confident you will. But I also want to remind you that we’re all here for the same goal. The patients. We take pride in our ability to work as a team, no matter how different our approaches might be.”

Agatha held her gaze for a moment, considering her words. "I'll make sure to remember that," she said, her voice smooth and controlled. But in her mind, a quiet storm was already brewing. She was used to working alone, relying only on herself. The idea of balancing her control with the collective input of others was a challenge, but Agatha didn’t shy away from challenges. She’d find a way to make it work, on her terms. The she continue choosing her words carefully. "I appreciate the opportunity to lead the NICU team here. The work they’re doing is impressive, and I’m looking forward to helping elevate it even further."

Lilia leaned back in his chair, studying her for a moment. "I like that. I’ve looked over your record, your success rates, your innovations in neonatal surgical procedures. It’s impressive, no question. But I also know you’re stepping into a team that’s been running a certain way for years. Change, even when it’s necessary, can be… complicated."

"I’m not here to make unnecessary changes," Agatha said evenly. "But I do believe there’s always room for improvement, and I’ve never been afraid to make adjustments when they’re in the best interest of the patients."

"Good," Lilia said with a nod. "And fair warning: there are a few strong personalities on your team. Don’t be surprised if you meet some resistance. But from what I hear, you’re not one to back down from a challenge."

Agatha allowed herself a genuine smile this time. "Challenges tend to bring out the best in me."

Lilia chuckled, the sound deep and resonant. "I thought as much. And since we’re being candid, I’ll tell you this: I’m glad you’re here. We’ve needed someone with your drive and expertise in the NICU for a while. But if you ever feel like this place is… different from what you’re used to, don’t hesitate to let me know. Mayo has its quirks, but it grows on you."

"Thank you," she said, her tone softening slightly. "I’ll keep that in mind.”

"You know," she said, her tone lighter now, "it might be good for you to meet the rest of the surgical department sooner rather than later. We have a bit of a tradition around here, dinner with the surgeons and their teams every few months. A way to keep things collaborative and… human."

Agatha tilted her head slightly. "A dinner?"

Lilia smiled, a knowing glint in her eyes. "Yes, and before you ask, no, it’s not mandatory, and yes, it can sometimes feel like herding cats. But it’s valuable."

She let out a soft chuckle despite herself.

Agatha's expression remained unreadable, her mind running through the potential benefits, or drawbacks, of such a gathering. She wasn’t fond of unnecessary socializing, especially when it took time away from work or her kid. But she also knew how these things worked. It was a small price to pay to maintain her position and further ingratiate herself into the Mayo culture.

“A dinner," Agatha repeated, her tone flat but inquisitive, as though trying to gauge what Lilia was truly asking of her.

Lilia's smile widened, as if she sensed Agatha's hesitation. "Yes. It’s informal, but it’s an opportunity to see your colleagues in a different light, outside the OR or the office. People get to relax, share ideas, talk about things that aren’t strictly related to the hospital. It’s about fostering a sense of unity."

Agatha’s gaze flickered with something akin to mild amusement. "Unity." She let the word linger in the air, as though testing its weight. “And this... tradition, is it meant to build trust, or just to ensure no one feels too isolated?”

Lilia leaned forward slightly, folding her hands together. "Both, in a way. Trust is built over time, and this is just one way to do it. I know it might seem trivial, but these moments have a way of making the team feel more cohesive. I’ve found it to be valuable, especially for those of us who tend to be… driven, like you." She paused, watching Agatha carefully. “You don’t have to attend if you’d rather not, but it could help you with the transition.”

Agatha’s gaze narrowed ever so slightly, but she kept her tone level. “I’m here to make an impact, not to participate in feel good rituals. I don’t see how dinner will change anything.”

Lilia’s eyes softened, but there was a quiet insistence in her voice. "I understand your focus, Agatha. Trust me, I do. But sometimes, how we build those impacts, how we work with others, can make all the difference in the long run. You’re not just leading the team; you’re becoming part of it. A dinner may be a small gesture, but it could go a long way in making sure you’re not seen as an outsider."

Agatha’s fingers twitched as she considered Lilia’s words. She had never been good at navigating the nuances of workplace culture, especially when it veered into the territory of forced camaraderie. But she also understood the politics at play. Building alliances, even subtly, could prove useful in the future.

She sighed inwardly, not giving any outward sign of her reluctance. "I’ll think about it," she said, her tone measured but polite.

Lilia grinned. "I’ll take that as a tentative yes. I’ll send you the details."

Lilia stood, smoothing her suit jacket as she extended her hand again. "I won’t keep you. I’m sure you’ve got a full schedule already. Just remember, my door’s always open."

She shook her hand, noting the firm but friendly grip. As she turned to leave, she glanced back.

"And, Dr. Harkness? You’ll do great here. I’ve got a good feeling about it."

As the door clicked shut behind her, Agatha sat back in her chair, exhaling slowly. The meeting had been a test, she knew, a way for Dr Calderu to gauge what kind of leader she would be. But if this was her first glimpse of how things worked in Minnesota, she was cautiously optimistic.

With a quick glance at the clock, Agatha stood up, smoothing down her lab coat. She had a few minutes before she had to leave to pick up Nicky from the daycare. The kid had been with a sitter for the last few hours, and she was itching to get home. 

As Agatha stepped out of the hospital, the chill of the Minnesota air hit her, and she adjusted her coat against the breeze. The morning had been long, filled with meetings and patient rounds, but now she finally had a few minutes to herself. She was looking forward to a cup of coffee and a pastry for Nicky before heading to pick him up from daycare.

The familiar scent of roasting beans greeted her as she approached the small coffee shop just down the street. When she reached the entrance of the coffee shop, she paused to pull out her phone and check the time again. She didn’t have much, but a quick coffee and a pastry for Nicky wouldn’t take long.

But as she stepped back outside, coffee cup in hand, bam. 

Something, someone, collided with her. The impact sent her stumbling back, and before she could process what was happening, her coffee was splashed all over the front of her lab coat.

"What the hell?" Agatha snapped, her body tensing as she surveyed the mess.

Then she saw her: a woman standing there, wide-eyed and apologetic, her hand raised as if to steady her. But it wasn’t the woman who caught Agatha’s attention, it was the small, scruffy dog tugging on a leash, practically weaving in and out of the woman’s legs.

“Oh my God, I’m so sorry!” the woman said, voice casual, too casual for Agatha’s taste.

Her jaw clenched, her patience already running thin. She wasn’t in the mood for this. The coffee was ruined, her coat now a mess, and the last thing she needed was some random stranger acting like this was nothing.

“Are you fucking blind?” Agatha snapped, her voice cutting through the air, her eyes locking with the woman’s.

The woman blinked, clearly taken off guard by the venom in Agatha’s voice. “I— sorry, it’s just… he’s all over the place,” she said, glancing down at the dog, who was still yanking at the leash, oblivious to the situation.

Agatha wiped at her coat with a sharp huff, her irritation bubbling over. “Yeah, well, maybe you should get him under control,” she bit back, eyeing the woman now, sizing her up. She didn’t even care how the woman looked, disheveled, with dark hair falling haphazardly over her face, or the way she casually stood there like nothing had happened.

“Are you seriously blaming the dog?” the woman asked, her lips curving into a half-amused, half-annoyed smirk.

The audacity. Agatha’s patience was practically nonexistent now. “No,” she said, her voice low and cold, “I’m blaming the idiot who can’t manage their dog.” She stepped back, eyes narrowing. “It’s called being aware of your surroundings.”

The woman’s grin faltered for just a second, replaced by something else in her eyes, curiosity, maybe a little surprise. “Right. Sorry again,” she said, but there was a definite edge of condescension in her voice now.

Agatha let the silence stretch, feeling the tension hang between them like thick air. The woman’s calm indifference was infuriating, and Agatha was done tolerating it. “You know,” she said, voice sharp as a blade, “maybe next time you should actually look where you’re walking instead of just apologizing and moving on like nothing happened.”

The woman looked her up and down, that same bored expression on her face, but now there was something else in her eyes, a flicker of recognition, or perhaps amusement at how far this confrontation had escalated.

“Well, aren't we charming?” the woman said with a smirk, stepping back slightly, her dog still pulling at the leash, oblivious to the tension in the air.

Agatha’s gaze was icy, her fists clenched, fighting the urge to snap back. She wasn’t in the mood to entertain this woman’s casual disregard for basic decency. She had more important things to do than get into a pissing contest with a stranger over a spilled coffee.

Still, something about the woman’s smugness was making Agatha’s blood boil. She was about to turn on her heel, walk away, and forget about it when the woman spoke again.

“You know,” the woman said, her tone light, but there was a sharp edge creeping into her voice now, “maybe you should consider taking it down a notch. You’re the one who lost your coffee, not the end of the world.”

Agatha’s jaw tightened. “I didn’t lose my coffee, you spilled it.” The words were clipped, the bite in her voice unmistakable. She was done with this. Done with the woman’s casual attitude, her infuriating smirk, and the way she acted as if this was some small, insignificant incident when it had completely ruined Agatha’s moment of peace.

The woman’s smile flickered, but she didn’t back down. Instead, she glanced down at her dog, still happily tugging at the leash, and then back at Agatha, her expression unreadable.

“Well, I guess I’m sorry for your ruined coat, then,” she said with a half-shrug, her voice dripping with an almost sarcastic sweetness. “I didn’t realize a little coffee was such a big deal.”

Agatha could feel her blood start to boil again, but she controlled it, forcing her features to remain calm. “It’s not just coffee,” she replied, her voice cold, “It’s about being respectful of the people around you.”

The woman tilted her head, still not apologizing in any real way. Instead, she took a step back, as if the confrontation had long since lost its heat for her, her dog still tugging at the leash.

“Right,” she said, the words drawn out as if to emphasize the point. “Well, next time, I’ll make sure to step aside just for you. Wouldn't want to mess up your precious coat.”

There it was again, her tone. The casual dismissiveness that made Agatha want to explode. But she didn’t. She fought it, standing firm, glaring at the woman for a moment longer.

“You’ve got a real attitude problem,” Agatha muttered under her breath, turning on her heel and walking toward the entrance of the coffee shop.

The woman called after her, voice still too calm for Agatha’s liking. “Yeah, well, you’ve got a temper problem. Might want to work on that.”

Agatha didn’t bother responding. She didn’t need to waste another second on this woman. But as she stepped into the shop, her mind was racing, still tangled in the absurdity of the interaction. Something about that woman, her attitude, her unflappable calm, had dug under Agatha’s skin in a way that left her unsettled. But Agatha wasn’t the type to dwell on it. She had more important things to focus on. Or at least, she tried to convince herself of that as she moved to order another coffee, the memory of the woman and her irritating dog already beginning to fade, just slightly.

Chapter 2

Chapter Notes

Please keep in mind while reading this chapter that I'm not a doctor, and my limited knowledge of medical procedures and hospital life comes from an obsessive love of Grey’s Anatomy and a quick Google search. So, it might not be the most accurate. Once I finish the story, I might take some time to research more and correct any mistakes. Enjoy!

Agatha’s hands were still shaking from the encounter outside the coffee shop, but she forced herself to focus. The last thing she needed was to bring any of her frustration home. She couldn’t let herself unravel just yet—not with Nicky waiting for her.

Agatha drove to the daycare. The low hum of the car’s engine and the soft sound of the radio were the only things keeping her grounded. Her mind kept replaying the confrontation with the woman outside the shop—the woman with the dog, the woman who dared to act so indifferent. It was almost like a challenge, one that Agatha hadn’t signed up for. But she didn’t have time to dwell on it.

The moment she walked through the door, she saw Nicky. His mop of curly hair and bright eyes stood out among the other children. He was sitting at a little table, scribbling away with crayons, completely engrossed in his task. Agatha’s heart softened. No matter how tough the day had been, seeing him like this always had a way of easing her mind.

“Mama!” Nicky exclaimed when he saw her, dropping his crayon as he rushed over. He flung his arms around her, and Agatha bent down to lift him into her arms.

“How was your day, buddy?” she asked, smiling as she hugged him tightly.

“It was awesome! I made a rocket ship!” Nicky said, his eyes wide with excitement.

Agatha chuckled, taking the drawing from him as he held it out proudly. “A rocket ship, huh? Let me see.”

The picture was full of bright colors and wild shapes—nothing like a real rocket, but to Nicky, it was perfect. “It’s great,” she said, kissing the top of his head. "Let’s get you home, rocket man."

As she walked out of the daycare, the weight of the earlier confrontation melted away. Nicky’s chatter about his day filled the air as Agatha buckled him into his car seat. She focused on the drive home, the road ahead a welcome distraction. She would deal with the rest of her day later, but for now, she just wanted to be with her son, to enjoy the quiet moments before she had to go back to the hospital. 

As they arrived home, Agatha pulled into the driveway, the car crunching over the gravel as she parked in front of the house. She shifted the car into park, exhaling slowly. It had been a day filled with tension and difficult decisions, but now, with Nicky beside her, she could focus on something that mattered more than any hospital drama.

“Alright, rocket man,” Agatha said, unbuckling him from his car seat. “Time to get inside and show me that rocket ship of yours again.”

Nicky grinned, his excitement barely contained as he scrambled out of the car and ran toward the front door. Agatha followed, locking the car behind her and walking into the house, where the smell of the evening’s dinner—something simple she’d thrown together earlier—hung in the air.

She closed the door behind her, and Nicky dashed into the living room, plopping down on the couch and proudly holding out his drawing. Agatha leaned over, inspecting it again. The colors were wild and the lines all over the place, but it was exactly what she needed—something pure, something that had nothing to do with the pressures of her work or the conflicts she faced today.

“You really think this is going to make it to the moon?” she asked, a teasing smile tugging at the corner of her lips.

“It’s already there!” Nicky said, nodding seriously, his face the picture of determination.

Agatha laughed softly, sitting down beside him and pulling him into a hug. She could feel the weight of the day lifting as she focused on the moment, on the simple joys of being with her son. The world outside could wait. For now, this was her space—her home, with Nicky, where she could breathe.

After dinner and some quiet time together, Nicky was ready for bed. He yawned widely as Agatha tucked him in, pulling his favorite blanket up to his chin. She kissed the top of his head, a soft smile on her face as she whispered, “Goodnight, buddy. Sweet dreams.”

“Night, Mama,” Nicky mumbled, already half asleep.

Agatha stood for a moment, watching him drift off before gently turning the light down and closing the door behind her. She moved back into the living room, her mind returning to the work she had to do. The quiet of the house was a stark contrast to the tension of the day, and she welcomed it.

Sitting down at the kitchen table, Agatha opened her laptop and pulled up the profiles of the residents she would be working with. There was a stack of paperwork waiting for her attention, the usual administrative tasks that came with her position. She flipped through the profiles, quickly assessing each resident’s background, their strengths, and areas for improvement. She made mental notes, already formulating a plan to address any weaknesses.

Some were promising, eager to learn, and ready for the challenges she would bring. Others, Agatha noted with a sharp eye, had potential but needed a firm hand to guide them. It was a task she was used to: shaping the next generation of surgeons, honing their skills, pushing them to be better, to think faster, and to work with precision under pressure

As Agatha worked through the residents' profiles, she couldn’t help but think about how she would approach them. She wasn’t one to hold back, and she didn’t believe in coddling. These residents—whoever they were—would be held to the highest standards. She had a reputation for a reason, and she intended to make sure they knew that. She would break them down if she had to, break down the walls of complacency, ego, and laziness. 

Her eyes narrowed as she skimmed through one particularly overconfident resident’s file. This one, she thought, would be a challenge. They were cocky, probably too much so for his own good. But she could already see the cracks—the little moments where they would fail if given the chance. That’s where she would come in. She would push them, make them realize how much they still had to learn, how far they had to go to reach the level of excellence she demanded.

And the others, the ones who had potential but weren’t quite there yet? They would need patience—just enough to let them see how capable they could be if they stopped second-guessing themselves. But the pressure would be constant. Every step they took, every decision they made, would be under a microscope. If they didn’t rise to the challenge, she would break them too—metaphorically speaking, of course. She wasn’t here to babysit. 

There was no room for weakness in her world. Agatha didn’t have the luxury of softness. She had climbed her way to the top through sheer force of will, and she would make sure these residents understood that nothing less than perfection was acceptable in her eyes.

She made notes, mentally preparing herself for the next day’s rounds, strategizing how to challenge each one. She didn’t do “nice.”. And by the time they left her care, they would be far better than when they started. Or they wouldn’t last. 

Agatha didn’t waste time on failures.

Agatha closed her laptop with a soft click. The house was quiet again, Nicky tucked safely in his bed, the weight of the day finally settling over her. She stretched, feeling the tension in her shoulders and neck, and then stood up to turn off the lights in the living room.

She moved to the bathroom to wash off the remnants of the day—stress, frustration, and the remnants of coffee still staining her coat. A quick shower, then she slipped into her pajamas, the fabric soft against her skin, a small comfort amidst the chaos of her professional world.

Agatha checked on Nicky once more before heading to her own bedroom. She stood for a moment by his door, listening to the soft sound of his breathing. She allowed herself a rare moment of peace before she slipped into her own bed, the cool sheets a welcome relief from the heat of the day.

And in the silence of the night, Agatha finally allowed herself to drift off, the weight of the day lifting as she sank into rest.

The next morning, Agatha woke early, the crisp morning air slipping through the curtains as daylight crept into the room. Her first on-call day at Mayo Clinic. 

She stood and quickly moved to the bathroom, splashing cold water on her face to shake off the remnants of sleep. The day ahead would be full of challenges, and she couldn’t afford to waste a single moment. She moved with purpose, her mind already running through the list of tasks awaiting her in the NICU and OR.

Agatha pulled on her purple scrubs, the fabric smooth and familiar against her skin. They were a uniform, yes, but more than that—a symbol of the expertise and authority she carried with her. The color was bold, and it made her feel ready to face whatever the day threw at her. Her hair was pulled back in a tight ponytail, and she took a moment to double-check her appearance in the mirror, ensuring everything was in place.

After a quick breakfast, she grabbed her phone to check any messages or updates from the hospital. There was always something that needed her attention. As she scrolled through, a new message from Dr. Calderu appeared.

“Good luck on your first on-call day, Agatha. If anything comes up, I’ll be around. Don’t hesitate to reach out if you need anything.”

She glanced at the clock. Time was slipping away, and she still needed to say goodbye to Nicky before she left.

She walked down the hallway to his room, her steps quiet but purposeful. The door creaked slightly as she opened it, finding Nicky already awake, sitting up in his bed with his favorite stuffed animal clutched tightly in his arms.

"Hey, buddy," Agatha said softly, her voice betraying the tenderness she usually kept hidden behind her professional mask. Nicky looked up, his sleepy eyes brightening when he saw her.

"Mommy!" he exclaimed, reaching out his arms to her.

She smiled, walking over to his bed and sitting down beside him. She hugged him tightly, pressing a kiss to the top of his head. " I’ve got to go to work today. I won’t be home for a little while, okay?"

Nicky nodded solemnly, his small face filled with understanding. "You’ll be back soon?"

Agatha’s heart clenched at the innocent question. She swallowed, her voice steady but soft. "Yeah, I’ll be back tomorrow. You’ll be with your sitter, and I’ll be thinking about you the whole time."

He nodded again, his little face looking more serious than usual. "Okay, Mommy. Be careful."

"I will, Nicky. I promise." Agatha kissed him again, lingering for a moment as she closed her eyes. "I’ll miss you, okay?"

"I’ll miss you too," he whispered back, a small smile tugging at his lips.

Agatha stood, taking one last look at her son before walking toward the door. She wasn’t sure if it was harder leaving him behind for a shift like this or the fact that she'd be away from him for a full 24 hours.

"Love you, kiddo," she said, her voice tight.

"Love you, Mama," Nicky called back.

With a deep breath, Agatha closed the door behind her and left the house, her thoughts heavy with the knowledge that she wouldn’t see him for another full day.

The car ride was quiet, her thoughts focused and calm, but her body was already keyed up for what lay ahead. She arrived at the hospital with time to spare, walking into the building with her usual controlled demeanor.

Today, nothing would slip past her. She was ready.

She made her way to the NICU first, where the team had already gathered, ready to follow her lead. As she approached, she could feel the eyes of the residents and nurses on her—an unspoken expectation, a mix of nerves and respect, as they awaited her direction.

Agatha cleared her throat and began. "Let's get started. We have a lot to go through and I expect full attention. No distractions, no excuses." Her voice was firm, sharp, and to the point—exactly the way she had learned to command attention in her years of experience.

Jennifer Kale, the charge nurse, stepped forward. "Dr. Harkness, the babies in isolation are stable for now. No significant changes. We also have a new admission—very premature, 24 weeks."

Agatha nodded, already flipping through the patient files. The statistics, the clinical data—it was all in front of her, but the real work would come from the decisions she made. "We’ll start with the premature infant. I want full neonatal support, intubation on standby, and let’s prepare for the possibility of immediate surfactant administration."

Jennifer nodded, jotting down notes as the team moved into action. The residents exchanged quick, practiced looks, their faces impassive but focused. They had seen this kind of case before, but this time it was her call.

"Anyone have any concerns?" Agatha asked, her voice carrying across the room.

One of the residents spoke up. "Given the gestational age, we should be careful with aggressive interventions. The risks—"

"Risks?" Agatha interrupted, her eyes narrowing as she looked directly at the young resident. "The risk is not acting fast enough. Do you want to be the one explaining why we didn’t give this baby every chance to survive?"

Dr. Park swallowed, looking down. The tension in the room thickened, and Agatha could feel it, the weight of their expectations and her no-nonsense approach. It wasn’t about making friends here. It was about saving lives.

"Prepare for the surfactant, and let's get moving," Agatha continued, her tone leaving no room for further debate.

The team scrambled into action, working seamlessly together. Agatha remained calm on the outside, but internally, her mind raced, calculating the next steps, ensuring she didn’t miss a single detail.

Minutes later, the infant was stabilized, intubated, and receiving the necessary treatment. Agatha stood back, watching the team work with precision. She allowed herself a brief moment of satisfaction—this was why she was here. The baby’s life was no longer in immediate jeopardy, but the night was far from over.

She moved on to the other cases, making quick, efficient rounds. Despite the weight of the responsibility on her shoulders, Agatha felt a sense of clarity. The job wasn’t easy, but she had spent years training for this very moment. And as the night stretched on, she found herself thriving in the challenge, completely immersed in the work.

"Keep up the pace," she reminded the team. "We’ll get through this. And when the shift ends, we’ll do it all over again.”

The morning passed in a steady, predictable rhythm. Agatha moved through her rounds with practiced efficiency, her focus sharp as ever. The hospital was bustling as usual, but she didn’t get paged to the trauma yet, a rare and welcome break. She used the time to assess the resident profiles further, her mind always thinking several steps ahead. Every moment was another opportunity to refine her plans, but today there was a quiet sense of relief.

By the time noon rolled around, Agatha found herself alone in the hospital's staff cafeteria, seated at a small table near the window. The lunch crowd had already come and gone, and now there was only the hum of conversation and clinking silverware around her. She didn't mind the solitude.

Her meal was simple—just a salad and a cup of coffee—but it was enough. She ate without distraction, her mind swirling with the conversations, cases, and decisions she had already made this morning. 

Agatha’s pager buzzed, a sharp reminder of the reason she was here. The message was brief: Trauma, urgent.

Without hesitation, she grabbed her phone and sprinted toward the trauma center. The brisk pace of her walk reflected her focus—this was what she signed up for, what she thrived on. Emergencies, the unpredictable nature of it all, the constant need for swift decisions. The steady hum of the hospital around her seemed to fade as she entered the trauma bay.

Inside, the usual chaos of trauma care was underway: nurses moving swiftly, equipment clattering, doctors barking orders. Agatha’s eyes scanned the room, immediately identifying the severity of the case. A young woman, in his early twenties, was wheeled in on a stretcher, covered in blood. She’d been involved in a severe car accident—multiple broken bones, a likely head injury, and internal bleeding.

She moved to the side, gathering her thoughts, when a voice from behind her caught her attention.

"Looks like you’ve got your hands full, huh?"

Agatha turned to see a tall, confident woman in scrubs, her red hair pulled into a tight ponytail. There was something about her demeanor—focused, calm, but with an edge that suggested she had seen it all. She wore the jacket of a trauma surgeon with ease, like it had been tailor-made for her. Agatha could see the familiar weight of authority in the woman’s stance.

"Dr. Wanda Maximoff, neurosurgery" the woman said, extending her hand.

Agatha took it, shaking firmly. “Dr. Agatha Harkness. Neonatal surgeon. I’m on call today"

Wanda nodded, sizing her up with a quick, calculating glance. "Yes, me too. Well, I hope you’re ready for this. We’ve got a mess on our hands." She motioned to the young man on the stretcher. "Possible intracranial hemorrhage, and his vitals are dropping fast. We need to move quickly if we’re going to save him."

Agatha felt the sharpness of her medical mind clicking into place as she assessed the situation. There wasn’t time for hesitation. She turned to the trauma team and barked out orders. "We need to intubate immediately. Start two large-bore IVs, and get the blood products ready. Let’s get a head CT in progress as soon as possible. This guy doesn’t have long."

The team worked with practiced efficiency, but Agatha’s focus was on the patient—and on Dr. Maximoff. She noticed the way she moved, directing the team with ease, offering guidance without overstepping. She was a leader, a natural, in a way that Agatha recognized immediately.

As the team worked to stabilize the young woman, Wanda stepped closer to Agatha, her voice low. "You seem to know your stuff. This patient’s critical, but I can tell you’re not fazed. You’ve handled trauma before."

Agatha didn’t respond right away. Instead, she focused on the monitor, her eyes narrowed as she checked the vital signs. "I’ve been through worse," she finally said, her voice steady, betraying nothing of the storm that often raged inside her. "But this one’s tricky."

Wanda nodded, watching the monitors too. "I get that. It's always the ones you don’t expect that get you."

The tension in the room was palpable. Every second counted. As the team rushed to keep the young woman stable, Agatha and Wanda moved in sync, each one stepping in when needed, their expertise guiding them through the chaos.

Finally, after what felt like an eternity, the young woman’s vitals stabilized enough for a CT scan to be done. The results were critical: a large hematoma pressing against the brain, along with signs of hemorrhaging.

"We need to go to surgery," Wanda said, turning to Agatha. "We’ll do a craniotomy to relieve the pressure. Can you assist me? I know you see mostly premature babies and pregnant women but the rest of the on-call attendings are working on the other OR´s already and this is a complicated case. I dont want a resident assisting me”

Agatha nodded, her mind already switching gears. "Let’s do it” she said confidently as they started walking to the OR.

The surgery was intense, and the entire team worked in concert, with Dr Maximoff leading the charge. They had to move fast, but with precision. Every incision, every decision had to be perfect.

As they closed the patient up, the silence in the room spoke volumes. The young woman was still alive, her chances of survival increased thanks to their combined efforts.

Wanda turned to Agatha as the team finished their work. "Nice work," she said, her tone respectful, though it wasn’t overly effusive. "You’ve got the skill to handle these situations. I can see why Dr Calderu brought you in."

Agatha looked back at her, her expression cool but with a flicker of appreciation. "Same to you," she replied. "You handled that well."

"Well, that was fun," Wanda said, her smirk returning. "You might just be my new favorite surgeon to work with." she said with a wink. 

Agatha gave a small, almost imperceptible smile. "We’ll see how things go next time."

As Wanda walked off to check on the next patient, Agatha stayed for a moment longer, her mind still buzzing from the adrenaline of the trauma. She hadn’t expected to meet someone like Wanda tonight, but in this world, where egos often collided and tempers flared, it felt... refreshing.

The night dragged on, the relentless pace of the hospital leaving Agatha on the edge of exhaustion. Despite the adrenaline from the surgeries wearing off, the pressure didn’t relent. Two complex surgeries in one night had left her drained, but it wasn’t just the surgeries that were wearing her down. The residents, in their chaotic attempts to “help,” had only made everything harder. Their lack of experience and inability to follow instructions made her blood boil.

She’d had enough.

After her second surgery, she finally collapsed into the break room, trying to shut out the noise and chaos around her. Two hours of sleep had done little to recharge her, and the caffeine buzz had long worn off. She felt a simmering rage building up inside her. The residents’ incompetence had added unnecessary stress to an already overwhelming night. She wasn’t the type to let it slide, and it wasn’t going to happen now.

She slammed her coffee cup down on the counter, glaring at the clock on the wall. Her mind was racing, her temper barely contained. She needed this to stop, or she was going to break. The lack of respect, the disregard for protocol, it was all getting to her.

Agatha took a deep breath, fighting to keep her composure. She could already hear the voices outside the room—the murmurs of residents discussing cases, as if everything was business as usual. It made her want to tear the walls down.

She stood up, smoothing her scrubs, the last remnants of exhaustion mixing with the overwhelming need to assert control. This was her shift, her domain, and if she had to make it everyone's problem, then so be it.

She walked out of the break room, her eyes scanning the hallway until she spotted the group of residents huddled together near the nurses’ station, laughing about something. She didn’t care what they were laughing at. They hadn’t been doing their jobs.

As she approached, her voice cut through the air, icy and sharp. “If you all can’t get your shit together, I’ll make sure you regret it,” she said, the words calculated and lethal. “This is a trauma center, not a playground. Stop wasting my time, and start acting like you belong here.”

The residents froze, their laughter dying in an instant. Agatha could feel their nervous glances on her, but she wasn’t about to give them the satisfaction of seeing her flinch. She stood tall, unrelenting, her gaze unwavering.

She didn’t care if she’d just made enemies. They needed to understand the gravity of the work they were doing. It wasn’t just about the procedures—it was about lives.

With that, she turned on her heel and strode down the hallway, not bothering to look back.

Agatha’s feet carried her to the coffee station with a sense of purpose. She needed something strong to wake her up, something to erase the lingering frustration from the night. Her mind was still buzzing with the aftermath of the surgeries and the confrontation with the residents. 

As she approached the counter, she noticed Dr Maximoff standing near the window, talking to someone. Agatha's tired eyes barely registered the woman at first, but then Wanda spotted her and gave a quick wave.

"Agatha!" Wanda called, her voice cheerful. "Come over here"

Agatha’s brow furrowed slightly, but she walked over, more out of courtesy than curiosity after she got her coffee. As she neared, she noticed the woman Wanda was talking to—a woman she hadn’t quite seen clearly before. She had her back turned to Agatha, but there was something oddly familiar about her posture. The woman was laughing, engaged in a conversation, completely at ease.

Wanda turned to her, a smirk tugging at her lips. "Agatha, meet Dr Vidal, orthopedic surgeon”

The woman turned to face her, and the recognition hit Agatha like a punch to the gut. It was her. The same infuriating woman from the coffee shop—the one who had spilled coffee all over Agatha’s lab coat, the one who had barely apologized before brushing it off as if nothing had happened.

Agatha’s eyes narrowed as the woman looked at her with the same casual demeanor, her lips twitching into a half-smile. "Oh," the woman said, a flicker of recognition passing through her expression as she looked Agatha up and down with a playful smirk forming on her lips.

Agatha’s mouth tightened, her annoyance bubbling up again, but Wanda was already smiling between the two of them, clearly oblivious to the tension she was walking into.

Agatha gave a small nod but kept her distance, her fingers tightly gripping her coffee cup. She wasn’t sure what to say. She was still working to keep the nerves from flaring up again.

“Nice to meet you, Dr Harkness. I’m sure you’ve been quite busy,” she said, almost as if it was an inside joke.

Agatha didn’t miss the undercurrent of familiarity between the two of them, and the edges of her patience began to fray again. Still, she kept her cool. "Yeah, it’s been a hell of a shift," Agatha responded flatly. "Nice to meet you too."Agatha said stiffly, her tone flat. She didn’t make the effort to hide the sharpness that had come back into her voice.

Dr Vidal raised an eyebrow but didn’t seem bothered, giving Agatha a cool look. “Yeah I can tell” she replied, her voice holding an edge of amusement.

Agatha’s fingers tightened around her coffee cup, but she kept her posture neutral, her face a careful mask. The woman—Dr. Vidal—wasn't going to get under her skin, not here, not now. She had already dealt with far worse in the hospital than some casual confrontation, but there was something about Dr. Vidal’s presence that grated on her nerves.

"Right," Agatha said, her voice cool and measured. "Long shift, lots of cases." She let the words hang in the air, the subtle edge to them making it clear that she wasn’t thrilled to be standing in front of the woman.

Dr. Vidal smiled—calm, unbothered.  "Don’t worry, we all have our days" she said, her tone teasing, as if she was making light of Agatha's irritation, or maybe enjoying it.

Agatha met her gaze, her expression hardening. "Tell me about it," she replied dryly, before lifting her cup to her lips and taking a long, deliberate sip of her coffee. She refused to let her annoyance show any more than it already had.

"Well," Wanda said, looking between the two women, her expression gleaming with curiosity, "I’ll leave you both to it. Got a few things to wrap up before heading home" She clapped her hands together and gave a playful wink to Agatha. "Catch you later, Agatha, Rio. You two can keep each other company. 

Agatha was about to leave but the infuriating woman spoke first.  So you’re the new chief of the NICU that everyone has been talking about huh?”

Agatha ignored her and started walking, refusing to let Dr. Vidal’s words get under her skin. She could feel her pulse quicken, but she wasn’t going to let herself be baited. The nerve of this woman, acting as if she knew her, acting as if she had any right to make judgments. Who the hell does she think she is?

She gripped her coffee cup tighter, keeping her eyes fixed ahead as she passed people on her way to the exit of the cafeteria. She didn’t need to dignify Dr. Vidal’s comment with a response. 

Dr. Vidal reached her when she was about to exit the cafeteria . “You know,” she said, tilting her head slightly, “I’m sorry about the coffee. Seriously. But maybe you should take a breath next time before jumping down someone’s throat. Could be good for your blood pressure.”

Agatha froze. Her pulse quickened, her face hardening.

“Maybe you should mind your business,” Agatha shot back, stepping closer, her eyes narrowed with a newfound sharpness. “I didn’t ask for your opinion.”

Dr Vidal didn’t flinch, though her lips curled into a knowing smirk. “Okay, then. I guess I’ll just keep walking, try not to get in your way.” She started to move past Agatha, but before she could completely step around her, she paused.

“And for the record,” she added with a mischievous glint in her eye, “if you're having a terrible day, maybe don't make it everyone else’s problem. Just a thought.” 

With that, she turned on her heel and walked away, her footsteps light and confident, leaving Agatha standing there, fuming.

Agatha stared at her, feeling the simmering heat of her frustration. The woman wasn’t the least bit rattled by Agatha’s confrontation—hell, it looked like she almost enjoyed it. With a last, lingering glance, she turned and walked away,

Her hand still on the door, every muscle in her body screaming to turn around and lay it all out. Instead, she took a deep breath and let it out slowly, her anger simmering just beneath the surface. She was done with this exchange.

Without looking back, Agatha pushed open the door and stepped into the cool air outside. She wasn’t going to let someone like Dr. Vidal distract her, not now, not when there were more important things on her mind. If Dr. Vidal wanted to play games, fine. But Agatha wasn’t interested in wasting her time. Not today. Not anymore.

With each step she took, she felt the tension ease, as if putting distance between herself and the woman allowed her to reclaim her focus.

And just like that she had survived her first 24 hour shift at Mayo Clinic.




Chapter 3

Chapter Notes

See the end of the chapter for notes

Agatha finally made it home, her body heavy with exhaustion but her mind buzzing with the events of her first 24-hour shift at Mayo Clinic. She tossed her bag onto the nearest chair and exhaled deeply, savoring the quiet sanctuary of her home.

The thought of Nicky arriving in the afternoon brought a wave of warmth, a reminder of the parts of her life that grounded her. Before that, though, she needed to shake off the tension still clinging to her muscles. She got out of her scrubs and changed into workout gear and went for a run. 

The rhythmic cadence of the run and the steady pulse of her workout playlist became a much-needed escape. With each step she took, the frustrations of the morning began to melt away, replaced by a sense of accomplishment—not just from surviving the shift but from finding her footing in this new chapter.

By the time she finished, her breathing was steady, her mind calmer. She grabbed a quick shower and settled in with a light snack, anticipation bubbling as she thought of Nicky’s arrival. 

After a quick lunch and a glance at the clock, Agatha grabbed her car keys and headed out to pick him up from daycare. The drive was peaceful, a welcome change from the chaos of the hospital and the whirlwind of her thoughts earlier in the day.

When she pulled into the daycare parking lot, the familiar sight of children playing in the yard brought a smile to her face. She stepped out of the car and walked toward the building, already eager to see Nicky's excited grin.

Inside, the daycare staff greeted her warmly. As always, the sound of laughter and chatter filled the air. Moments later, Nicky appeared, his little face lighting up when he saw her.

"Mommy!" he exclaimed, running toward her with arms wide open.

Agatha crouched down to scoop him up, her heart swelling at the sheer joy in his voice. "Hey, buddy! Did you have fun today?"

Nicky nodded enthusiastically, already launching into a story about his day—complete with dramatic reenactments and a tangle of half-formed sentences only a parent could follow.

Agatha chuckled, holding him close as they walked back to the car.

The afternoon was a cozy whirlwind of flour, sugar, and giggles as Agatha and Nicky set about making cookies together. Nicky insisted on using every cookie cutter they owned—stars, hearts, dinosaurs, and even a lopsided snowman that he said looked "like Olaf."

Agatha guided his little hands as they pressed shapes into the dough, her laughter bubbling up every time he tried to sneak a piece of raw cookie dough into his mouth. By the time the cookies were in the oven, both of them were covered in a fine dusting of flour, their cheeks pink from the warmth of the kitchen and their shared laughter.

As the cookies cooled, they settled on the couch with a plate of their creations and a glass of milk for Nicky. He grabbed the remote and beamed up at her. "Can we watch Frozen, Mommy? Please?"

Agatha smiled, ruffling his hair. "Of course, buddy."

Nicky clambered onto her lap as the familiar opening notes of Frozen began to play. Together, they sang along to Let It Go, with Nicky dramatically waving his arms like Elsa casting her ice powers. Agatha couldn’t help but join in, laughing at his enthusiasm and her own slightly off-key performance.

By the time the credits rolled, Nicky was curled up against her, his eyes drooping. Agatha kissed the top of his head, savoring the quiet moment.

Agatha leaned back on the couch, her arm draped protectively around Nicky as he slept, his little chest rising and falling with each peaceful breath. She glanced at the remnants of their afternoon—a plate of half-eaten cookies, flour smudges still on the counter, and the faint echo of Let It Go lingering in her mind.

For the first time in a long while, she felt a quiet sense of accomplishment. Not as a doctor or a leader, but as a mother. The chaos of her life often made her question if she was doing enough for Nicky, if she was present enough, patient enough, good enough. But moments like these, filled with his laughter and the warmth of his trust, reminded her that she was trying—and succeeding.

Agatha stroked Nicky’s hair, her heart swelling with love and resolve. She was doing her best, and for him, that was enough.

After putting Nicky to bed and tidying up the living room, Agatha made her way to her bedroom. The day had been long but rewarding, and she was ready to unwind. As she reached for the laundry basket, her hand brushed against the fabric of her coat, now marked with a large, brown coffee stain.

She paused, staring at it. The sight of the stain brought the memory rushing back—the coffee shop, the collision, and Dr. Vidal’s maddening smirk. Agatha’s jaw tightened as her mind replayed the woman’s unapologetic demeanor and flippant remarks.

“Of course,” she muttered under her breath, yanking the coat off the chair and inspecting the stain. Her annoyance reignited as if no time had passed. Who did Dr. Vidal think she was, waltzing into Agatha’s life with that smug attitude and audacious comments?

She tossed the coat into the laundry pile with a little more force than necessary. Shaking her head, she tried to dismiss the thoughts. Not worth my time, she reminded herself, heading to the closet to find her workout gear.

But as she pulled her sneakers on the shelf, the memory of Dr. Vidal’s parting words lingered.

"Not everyone’s having a terrible day. Just saying."

Agatha sighed, rubbing her temple. There was something infuriatingly intriguing about the woman’s confidence, a challenge in her words that stuck with Agatha longer than it should.

She went into her office and turned on her laptop before she could second guess herself. 

Agatha hesitated for a moment, her fingers hovering over the keyboard. She could almost hear her own inner voice questioning her motives, but curiosity—or perhaps the need to understand her new adversary—won out.

"Just to know what I’m dealing with," she told herself, her excuse flimsy even in her own mind.

With a deliberate exhale, she typed into the search bar: Dr. Rio Vidal.

The results popped up almost immediately. Articles, profiles, awards—it was all there. Rio Vidal, orthopaedic surgeon, renowned for her groundbreaking techniques in minimally invasive heart surgery. Agatha’s brows furrowed as she skimmed the headlines.

“Johns Hopkins Surgeon Transforming Orthopedic Care.”

“Dr. Rio Vidal: Redefining Precision in Orthopedic Surgery.”

“Vidal’s Revolutionary Approach to Orthopedic Surgery Saves Lives Globally.”

Agatha’s lips pressed into a thin line. Of course. Dr. Vidal wasn’t just confident—she had the credentials to back it up. 

Agatha clicked on the Mayo Clinic website, scrolling through a picture of the woman standing confidently in scrubs, arms crossed, her smirk unmistakable even in a formal photograph.

Even in her employee photo, that same damn smirk lingered—cocky, bold. The one that had irked Agatha earlier.  She looked incredibly young to have earned these credentials. That alone seemed unusual. How old was she?

Agatha scanned the details, her brow furrowing as she read further. No Ivy League school for her undergrad; a Bachelor of Science degree in Plant Biology at North Carolina State University, interesting . But there was Johns Hopkins for med school straight out of undergrad, followed by a residency in orthopaedic surgery at the same hospital. 

“Figures,” Agatha muttered under her breath, feeling her irritation simmering again. But there was something more, something beneath the irritation—a grudging respect she wasn’t quite ready to acknowledge.

As she read on, she found herself drawn in despite herself. Vidal’s career was impressive, no doubt about it. But it didn’t erase the fact that she was infuriatingly smug and unapologetic. The memory of her smirk at the coffee shop flashed through Agatha’s mind again, reigniting her frustration.

Agatha closed the laptop with a decisive snap. She wasn’t about to let this infuriating woman live rent-free in her mind any longer tonight. Pushing back from the desk, she resolved to get some sleep. But as she lay in bed, staring at the ceiling, she couldn’t quite shake the feeling that this wouldn’t be the last time Dr. Vidal got under her skin.

While she was laying in the comfort of her bed staring at the ceiling as the events of the day looped through her mind like an unrelenting movie reel. The shift had been grueling, the daycare pick-up a much-needed balm, and the afternoon with Nicky pure joy. But as satisfying as it was to be home and savoring the calm, her thoughts kept snagging on one infuriating thread: Dr. Rio Vidal.

She couldn’t pinpoint what made her angrier—the unapologetic smirk, the casual dismissal of her frustration, or the fact that someone like Vidal had managed to unsettle her at all. Agatha prided herself on her composure, her ability to stay one step ahead, to maintain control. That control had been hard-won through years of medical school, residency, her marriage and motherhood.

Yet somehow, in the span of a single coffee-stained moment, Vidal had managed to throw her completely off balance.

With a frustrated sigh, Agatha shifted under the covers. It wasn’t just the coffee spill or the maddeningly confident attitude. No, it was the way Vidal had seemed to see her—to call out her bad day with a flippant comment that stuck like a burr.

“maybe you should take a breath next time before jumping down someone’s throat. Could be good for your blood pressure.”

Who says that to a stranger? To a colleague even? And who waltzes into a hospital with that level of swagger, acting as if the world owed them admiration? Agatha rolled onto her side, her jaw tightening. She hated people like that—people who thrive on chaos, who embraced uncertainty with open arms. It was the antithesis of everything Agatha had built her life around.

Control was her armor. Her meticulously scheduled days, her color-coded calendar, even the way she folded Nicky’s clothes into neat little squares—it was all part of her system, her fortress against the unpredictable. And now here was Vidal, crashing through that carefully constructed fortress like a hurricane, leaving coffee stains and cocky smirks in her way. 

Agatha’s mind replayed the moment she’d looked up Vidal’s resume. That smirk in her employee photo—it was almost taunting. As if Vidal knew she was difficult to pin down, impossible to predict.She found herself trying to parse every detail in the photo. Was the smirk deliberate? Was it an act of defiance? Or was Vidal genuinely one of those people who found the world endlessly amusing, and Agatha had just happened to be the punchline that day?

How had she done it? How had she made it this far, this fast? Agatha couldn’t help but wonder what sacrifices Vidal had made along the way. Or did Vidal just have it all figured out—charm, brilliance, and the kind of confidence that made people want to follow her lead? It was maddening to think about.

Her mind circled back to Vidal’s career achievements. The articles written about her, the awards she had won, the glowing praise. It all added up to someone who was clearly exceptional, a rising star in her field. Agatha hated to admit it, but Vidal’s résumé was intimidating. She wasn’t just talented—she was innovative, a trailblazer. And young, too. Younger than Agatha had expected. How old was she? That part stung more than she cared to acknowledge.

How did someone like Vidal— what, in her early thirties mid thirties at most —already become chief of a department? Agatha felt a pang of frustration. It had taken her years of grueling work, sacrifices, and navigating a labyrinth of hospital politics to get where she was. Her first time leading a department hadn’t come until she was forty-one. Now, five years later, she was in her second leadership role, proud of the role she was taking but acutely aware of the cost it had taken to get there.

Every step had been hard-earned, every achievement fought for tooth and nail. There were no shortcuts, no strokes of luck—just relentless effort. The late nights spent agonizing over cases, the holidays she’d missed, the birthdays she’d forgotten, and the friendships she’d let fade. She had poured everything into her career, giving pieces of herself she could never reclaim.

It had even cost her her marriage, for God’s sake. The thought twisted in her chest, a familiar ache she had learned to live with. Her ex-husband had once said she married her work, and maybe he wasn’t entirely wrong. Being a surgeon, a well recognized one —it wasn’t just a job. It was an identity, a calling, and she’d answered it, fully and without hesitation, the goal she knew she wanted to achieve since she was in high school. 

She stood there, looking at the ceiling if it were somehow to blame for her spiraling thoughts. Vidal made it look effortless, as if she had simply walked into her success. That smirk, that maddeningly self-assured attitude—it all screamed privilege, or at least luck. 

Agatha’s hands tightened into fists. Was it luck? Or had Vidal truly outworked everyone around her? The thought twisted in her gut. If it was the latter, it felt like a judgment, a reminder of the time Agatha might have wasted second-guessing herself, playing it safe, or trying to balance too much.

She sighed heavily, running a hand through her hair. The truth was, Vidal’s success wasn’t her failure. Was it fair to compare? Probably not. But fairness didn’t stop the sting of knowing that someone nearly a decade younger had seemingly skipped the trenches she’d had to wade through; it didn’t stop the little voice in her head from whispering that Vidal had somehow lapped her in the race, and with a cocky smirk to boot.

"Whatever," Agatha muttered, standing up abruptly. She didn’t need to think about Vidal anymore tonight. She had her own accomplishments to be proud of—accomplishments she had worked damn hard for. Let Vidal have her smirk.

But as much as Agatha hated to admit it, there was something else beneath the frustration. A flicker of curiosity. An uncomfortable, grudging respect. Vidal was everything Agatha couldn’t stand—and yet, everything she couldn’t ignore.

That thought was the most unsettling of all. Agatha didn’t like people getting under her skin, didn’t like feeling out of control. And Vidal had managed to do both in record time.

Turning onto her back, Agatha exhaled sharply. “I don’t have time for this,” she muttered aloud, as if saying the words could banish Vidal from her thoughts. She had enough on her plate without adding a maddening, overly confident surgeon to the mix.

But deep down, Agatha knew the truth. Vidal wasn’t the type to fade into the background. She was a challenge—and Agatha hated leaving a challenge unanswered.

The thought made her stomach tighten, a mix of irritation and anticipation curling in her chest. She would have to face Vidal again, and when that happened, she needed to be ready. No more letting the woman catch her off guard. Next time, Agatha would hold the upper hand.

Resolving to push the matter aside for now, she closed her eyes and focused on her breathing, willing herself to relax. But even as sleep crept in, Vidal’s smirk lingered in the corners of her mind, a sharp reminder that some storms can’t be contained—and some battles can’t be ignored.

Chapter 4

The next morning, Agatha woke up to the faint sound of Nicky stirring in his room. For a moment, she lingered in bed, savoring the stillness before the day began. But even as she mentally outlined her schedule—rounds, a patient consultation, an administration of the NICU meeting—the thought of Vidal intruded, as sharp and unwelcome as the coffee stain on her coat.

She sighed, rolling out of bed and pulling on a sweatshirt. It was too early for this. Yet, as she padded into the kitchen to start breakfast, her mind picked up the thread from the night before. Vidal wasn’t just infuriating; she was a wildcard. And Agatha didn’t do well with wildcards.

By the time Nicky bounded into the kitchen, babbling about dinosaurs and asking for pancakes, Agatha had managed to push most of her irritation aside. She focused on flipping pancakes and helping him decide between syrup or fruit. These moments grounded her. They reminded her of why she worked so hard, why she pushed herself to be better every day. Nicky deserved stability, love, and a mother who was present.

Still, as she dropped Nicky off at daycare and drove toward the hospital, the tightness in her chest returned. She didn’t want to admit it, but Vidal had sparked something in her—an unease, yes, but also a challenge. And if there was one thing Agatha couldn’t resist, it was the need to prove herself.

As she entered the hospital, the familiar rhythm of her day began to take over. The sterile smell of antiseptic, the faint hum of monitors, and the steady stream of nurses and residents greeting her brought a sense of order. This was her domain, the place where she thrived. She didn’t have time for distractions here.

But fate, it seemed, had other plans.

After the rounds had ended Agatha found herself adjusting a plan for one of her little patients of the NICU.
