“Dr. Harkness,” nurse Kale called as Agatha reviewed a patient chart near the nurses’ station. “We’ve got a call. There’s someone asking for you in the consultation room.”

Agatha glanced up, frowning. “Who is it?”

“She didn’t say. Just mentioned it was urgent.”

Agatha’s pulse quickened. Urgent? That could mean anything—a sudden consult, a patient’s worried family member, or worse. She closed the chart and handed it off, her mind already cycling through possibilities as she headed down the corridor.

As she pushed the door open, her stomach sank.

There she was. Dr. Vidal, leaning casually against the center table, arms crossed, and of course that same maddening smirk playing on her lips. 

“Morning, Harkness,” Vidal said, her voice smooth and infuriatingly self-assured.

Agatha felt her spine stiffen, her grip tightening on the doorframe. “Dr. Vidal,” she said, her tone sharp, “I don’t have time for this, so let’s get to the point.”

Vidal’s smirk widened, her eyes gleaming with mischief. “Good to see you too,” she replied, unbothered by Agatha’s curt greeting. Vidal shrugged, her expression almost playful. “I thought it was time we had a proper introduction. Considering how well our first encounter went.”

Agatha’s jaw clenched. “You spilled coffee on me.”

“Technically, you walked into me,” Vidal countered, her tone light. “But I’ll let you have this one.”

Agatha closed the door behind her, crossing her arms. “If you have something to say, get to the point. I’m busy.”

Vidal chuckled, her gaze steady. “I’m sure you are. But being busy doesn’t mean we can’t coexist, does it? We’re colleagues now, after all.”

Colleagues. The word landed like a challenge. Agatha fought to keep her composure, refusing to rise to the bait. “Is that why you’re here? To discuss ‘coexisting’?”

“Partly,” Vidal admitted, her smirk softening into something more genuine—or maybe Agatha was imagining it. “I’ve heard about you, Dr Harkness. Everyone here talks about how sharp, driven, and focused you are. I wanted to see for myself.”

Agatha narrowed her eyes, searching Vidal’s face for any hint of insincerity. But Vidal’s expression remained open, almost curious. It threw Agatha off balance.

“And?” she asked, her voice cooler than she intended.

Vidal’s smirk returned, sharper this time. “You’re exactly as intense as they said you’d be”

Agatha blinked, unsure how to respond. She wasn’t used to compliments — especially not from people like Vidal.

“I don’t need your approval,” she said finally, her voice firm.

Vidal straightened, nodding as if she’d expected that answer. “I’m sure you don’t. But I thought we should clear the air. We’re going to be working together, after all. Might as well figure out how to make it work.”

Agatha pushed back, crossing her arms tighter as if creating a physical barrier between them. “I don’t see the point of this conversation, Dr. Vidal,” she said curtly. “We’re professionals. We’ll work together when necessary and that’s that. No need for personal theatrics.”

Vidal tilted her head, an amused glint in her eye. “No theatrics, huh?” she echoed. “Well, I suppose that depends on how you define it. I just thought it might be helpful to establish some common ground.”

“Common ground?” Agatha raised an eyebrow, her voice dripping with skepticism. “I wasn’t aware we had any. You work on bones I see mostly babies and pregnant women”

Vidal laughed softly, the sound as smooth and maddening as her smirk. “We’re both here to do our jobs as leaders of our departments, aren’t we? That seems like a good enough start.”

Agatha wasn’t buying it. Vidal’s nonchalance felt too calculated, her charm too deliberate. “If you think flattery or casual chit-chat is going to make me forget the way you waltzed in here as if you own the place, think again,” she said, her tone sharp.

Vidal didn’t flinch. Instead, her smirk softened into something closer to genuine amusement. “Noted,” she said simply. “But for the record, I don’t ‘waltz’—I stride. Confidently” she paused, a smirk forming in her lips “and if i recall correctly one of us is new here" she paused "and its not me” she said slowly, savoring the worlds

Agatha felt her cheeks warm despite herself, irritation and something she couldn’t quite name twisting in her chest. “Is there anything else, or can I get back to work?”

Vidal took a step back, hands raised in mock surrender. “I’ll leave you to it, Dr Harkness. For now.”

“For now?” Agatha echoed, her eyes narrowing.

Vidal’s smirk returned, full force. “We’re bound to cross paths again. Something tells me it’ll be… interesting.”

Agatha bristled, but before she could respond, Vidal was already halfway out the door, her laugh echoing behind her.

As the door clicked shut, Agatha let out a breath she hadn’t realized she was holding. Vidal was going to be a problem—a challenge she couldn’t avoid.

And yet, there was something about Vidal that stuck with her, like a splinter under the skin. It wasn’t just the smirk or the confidence. It was the way she carried herself, as if the world couldn’t touch her. As if she knew something Agatha didn’t.

Agatha shook her head, trying to refocus. She had a full day ahead—a patient recovering from surgery, an administrative report due by noon, and a meeting with the department heads in the afternoon. Vidal didn’t fit anywhere in her schedule.

But, as much as Agatha tried to will her out of her mind, Vidal lingered.

Her thoughts churned as she pulled up the day’s patient list on her tablet. Driven. Intense. Focused. Vidal’s words echoed in her mind. Agatha didn’t know whether to feel flattered or patronized. Probably both. She wasn’t used to being analyzed like that, not outside of her performance reviews. And certainly not by someone she’d just met.

Why does she care what I’m like? Agatha thought. Why is she pushing my buttons?

The idea that Vidal might see her as some kind of challenge made Agatha’s stomach tighten. She hated being underestimated—or worse, being studied like she was some sort of puzzle to be solved.

But even as her irritation grew, so did her curiosity. Who was Dr. Rio Vidal, really? The articles had painted her as a rising star, but they didn’t explain the person behind the accolades. What drove her? What was it like to operate with such confidence?

The thought gnawed at Agatha throughout the morning, resurfacing during rounds and even as she delivered a post-op update to a patient’s family. Her usually precise focus felt unsteady, her mind darting back to Vidal’s grin, her challenge, her maddening presence.

By lunchtime, Agatha had had enough. She grabbed her tray from the cafeteria line and found a quiet corner to sit, determined to regain control over her thoughts. Control was what she excelled at, after all. Control was what kept her sane in the chaos of her life.

But as she picked at her salad, her phone buzzed with a new email. She glanced down, expecting another reminder from administration, but froze when she saw the sender.

Dr. Rio Vidal.

Agatha’s breath caught. She hesitated for a moment, debating whether to open it. But curiosity won out, as it always did.

Subject: Thought You Might Like This

Message:

“Dr Harkness,

I noticed you’re on the committee for procedural innovation grants. I recently published a case study on minimally invasive fracture repairs in high-risk OBGYN patients that might interest you. Let me know if you’d like to discuss.

Best,

Dr Vidal”

Attached was a PDF. Short, professional, and completely without the cocky tone Agatha had come to associate with her.

Agatha stared at the email, her mind racing. Was this a genuine professional gesture? Or another way for Vidal to insert herself into Agatha’s life?

She opened the attachment, scanning the document quickly. It was impressive, she had to admit—meticulously detailed and thoroughly researched. But that only made her more suspicious.

Was this Vidal’s way of asserting dominance? Of showing off her brilliance? Or was it something else entirely?

Agatha’s appetite vanished as she reread the email. She didn’t know what Vidal’s game was, but she was determined to find out.

Fine, she thought, closing her laptop with a snap. Two can play this game.

The rest of the day passed in a blur, Agatha’s thoughts half on her patients and half on her growing determination to understand Vidal. By the time her department meeting rolled around, she felt more composed, more in control.

That is, until she walked into the conference room and saw Vidal sitting at the table, chatting casually with the other surgeons.

Vidal looked up as Agatha entered, her smirk returning as their eyes met.

Agatha squared her shoulders and lifted her chin, refusing to let Vidal see even a flicker of the unease brewing beneath her composed exterior. She greeted the room with a curt nod, her voice steady as she said, “Good afternoon, everyone.”

Sliding into her seat, she avoided Vidal’s gaze and began organizing her notes. The other surgeons exchanged pleasantries, but Agatha stayed focused on the papers in front of her. She wasn’t about to indulge Vidal with attention—or worse, let her see how rattled she was.

“Dr. Harkness,” Vidal’s voice cut through the low hum of conversation, smooth as ever. “Dr. Calderu  was just telling me about the recent developments in procedural integration. Your work on optimizing team efficiency at NYU has really set a standard.”

Agatha froze for half a second, her pen hovering above the page. Was that... a compliment? She looked up, her expression guarded. Vidal’s face was unreadable, though her eyes still carried that spark of mischief.

“Thank you,” Agatha replied coolly, her tone professional. 

“Modest,” Vidal said, leaning back in her chair with an infuriatingly relaxed posture. “But effective. It’s no wonder your department ran so smoothly. Or thats what the streets are saying anyways”

Agatha felt the eyes of her colleagues shift between the two of them, the air in the room charged with an undercurrent of curiosity. Was this some kind of power play? A public acknowledgment meant to keep her off balance?

“Well,” Agatha said, forcing a small smile, “I strive for excellence here. I’m sure I’ll bring the same level of success to the NICU here.”

Vidal’s lips twitched, her smirk softening into something that almost resembled genuine amusement. “That’s the goal,” she replied, her voice warm but edged with something Agatha couldn’t quite place.

The meeting began, and for a while, Agatha managed to immerse herself in the agenda. Discussions of resource allocation, upcoming procedures, and departmental goals filled the room. But every so often, she felt Vidal’s gaze on her—a steady, measured presence that was impossible to ignore.

By the time the meeting ended, Agatha’s head was spinning. She gathered her things quickly, determined to leave before Vidal could corner her again.

But as she stepped into the hallway, a voice called out behind her.
“Harkness, wait up.”

She froze for half a beat before turning, her expression carefully blank. Rio Vidal was strolling toward her, the deliberate ease of her steps setting Agatha’s teeth on edge. That small, almost disarming smile was plastered across her face, but something sharper gleamed in her eyes.

“What is it, Dr. Vidal?” Agatha asked, her tone clipped and cold.

Vidal stopped a few steps away, tilting her head as though appraising a puzzle she hadn’t quite solved. “Relax,” she said, her voice tinged with amusement. “I’m not here to start a fight.”

“Good,” Agatha replied curtly, crossing her arms. “Because if you were, you wouldn’t win.”

That earned a low chuckle. Vidal’s smile deepened, but there was an edge to it. “God, you’re fun when you’re defensive.” She lingered, her gaze sweeping over Agatha in a way that felt just shy of insolent. “But no, really. I meant what I said earlier. Impressive work.”

Agatha’s eyes narrowed. “If you’re trying to butter me up, Vidal, don’t bother. Compliments are cheap, and I don’t need your approval.”

Vidal’s smile didn’t falter, but a flicker of something—annoyance or respect—flashed behind her eyes. She stepped closer, invading Agatha’s space just enough to make her bristle.

“Approval?” Vidal echoed, her tone sharp but controlled. “Don’t flatter yourself, Harkness. I’m just giving credit where it’s due. Doesn’t mean I’m lining up to be your cheerleader.”

Agatha’s glare hardened. Compliments from Vidal always felt like a setup, a smooth path with an unseen drop-off. “And you’re telling me this because…?”

Vidal shrugged, her casual tone undercut by a teasing glint in her eyes. “I don’t know. Maybe I thought it’d be nice to acknowledge we’re both damn good at what we do. Or maybe I just wanted to see how long you’d keep glaring at me like I stole your favorite pen.”

Agatha scoffed, shifting her weight but holding her ground. “You’re assuming I have a favorite pen.”

“Oh, you definitely do,” Vidal said slowly, savoring each word, a smirk tugging at her lips. “Probably something expensive and unnecessarily intimidating. Just like you.” 

Agatha held her breath. The jab hit closer to home than she cared to admit, but she kept her expression carefully neutral. “Do you have a point, or are you just enjoying wasting my time?”

“I enjoy lots of things,” Vidal said lightly, taking one last calculated step closer. “But no, I’m serious. That paper I sent you earlier—it’s good. Let me know if you ever want to discuss it. We both know collaboration isn’t exactly your strong suit, but it could be… entertaining to try.”

Agatha stared at her, suspicion and irritation warring with something else she refused to name. Vidal’s smile softened—just enough to feel dangerous.

“What do you think?” Vidal added, her voice low, playful.

Agatha’s tone was icy. “I think I’d rather staple my hand to my desk.”

Vidal laughed, the sound genuine and annoyingly warm, sending a flutter of irritation—or something else—through Agatha’s chest.

“You’re impossible,” Vidal said, shaking her head as she turned to leave. “Goodnight Dr Harkness”

Agatha watched Vidal walk away, her shoulders set with the kind of confidence that made Agatha’s blood simmer. That laugh—light, infuriatingly genuine—echoed in her head like a song she couldn’t shake.

Smooth bastard. 

Imposible. The word clung to her, both a dismissal and a challenge.

She turned sharply on her heel, jaw clenched, fuming inside. Her steps echoed down the corridor as she headed toward her office, determined to put the interaction out of her mind. But Vidal’s words lingered like a thorn under her skin. We both know collaboration isn’t your strong suit.

The nerve of her.

Slamming her office door shut behind her, Agatha threw her coat over the back of her chair and leaned against the desk, exhaling sharply. Her fingers gripped the edge, knuckles whitening as she replayed the conversation.

She didn’t need to justify herself to Vidal, of all people. Yet the way Vidal had looked at her, all sharp edges wrapped in disarming charm, made her chest tighten with something she couldn’t quite name.

Collaboration. Agatha almost laughed aloud at the thought. If Vidal thought she could waltz into her life with smirks and half-hearted compliments, she had another thing coming.

She gathered her belongings with practiced efficiency and headed to her car, the weight of the day pressing heavily on her shoulders. Sliding into the driver’s seat, she exhaled sharply, gripping the steering wheel for a moment before reaching for the ignition.

Just as she was about to start the engine, her phone buzzed. The screen lit up with a notification— Message from Dr. Calderu.

“ Good afternoon Agatha. The dinner of the heads of the surgery departments is this Friday at 8:00. Ill send you the details. hope to see you there”

Agatha sighed as she read the message, her thumb hovering over the screen. A dinner with the heads of surgery departments? While she understood the importance of networking and maintaining collegial relationships, she wasn’t in the mood for socializing—especially after the day she’d had.

Still, ignoring Dr. Calderu’s invitation wasn’t an option. These events carried weight, a chance to solidify her position and demonstrate her commitment to the team. Not to mention, skipping it might be seen as rude or uncooperative.

Before she could second guess herself her fingers moved to type a response.

“Thank you for letting me know, Dr. Calderu. I’ll be there.”

She hit send, then leaned back against the headrest of her car, letting out a breath.

Dinner with department heads. Great. Just what she needed—a night of polite small talk, half-hearted laughs, and the underlying tension of professional politics.

Agatha’s mind drifted to the inevitable interactions she’d face. Would Vidal be there? The thought immediately irritated her. Of course Vidal would be there. She seemed like the kind of person who thrived in those environments, effortlessly charming everyone while leaving Agatha feeling off-balance.

Shaking her head, Agatha started the car and pulled out of the hospital parking lot. She told herself she’d deal with Friday when it came. Right now, she needed a long shower, a quiet evening, and a moment to clear her head from Vidal’s maddening presence.



Chapter 5

Chapter Notes

Quick reminder: I'm not a doctor and only have a general understanding of medical concepts. This chapter is a bit graphic, but it's essential for the next chapters to make sense, and it gives a better understanding of Agatha and Rio's dynamic.
I’ve written the next 10 chapters and am really pleased with the pacing of the story so far. Let me know what you think—I'd love to hear your feedback. Enjoy!

As Agatha reached for the wheel, ready to go home to Nicky, the phone on her bag buzzed again—this time, an emergency alert. Her eyes flicked to the screen, and her pulse quickened slightly as she read the message.

"Dr. Harkness, we have a trauma case involving a pregnant woman coming in; a car accident with an apparent partial amputation of one of her legs. They need you for immediate evaluation and surgery."

Her first instinct was to curse under her breath. Nickys nanny would have to put him to bed. Technically she wasn't on call today but she had the knowledge and the skill needed on this case.  She had no choice but to answer the call. 

With a tight, controlled exhale, Agatha grabbed her phone, shot a quick text to the sitter, and then moved swiftly into the hospital. Her heels clicked decisively against the polished floors as she made her way into the trauma bay, the weight of her responsibility settling onto her shoulders. Every second counted in neonatal surgery, and there was no room for hesitation.

The pregnant woman, unconscious and pale, was being wheeled in, a team of ER nurses and doctors surrounding her. The crash had been severe, and Agatha could already see signs of the woman’s injuries. She was in critical condition—bruising on her abdomen, dangerously low blood pressure, and her right leg… Agatha’s breath hitched for a second as she took it in.

The leg was a disaster. The bone of her femur had punched through the skin, jagged and gleaming white under the harsh fluorescent lights, surrounded by a halo of torn flesh and pooling blood. The exposed bone looked stark against the mottled bruising and shredded muscle, and bits of dirt and debris clung to the open wound, likely from the impact. Blood was dripping steadily onto the stretcher, pooling in uneven streaks across the sterile white sheet beneath her.

The sight made Agatha’s stomach tighten—not from queasiness, but from the urgency of the situation. 

“Dr. Harkness,” the attending physician called as she entered the room. He was mid-setup for a central line insertion, his face taut with concern. “This is Mary Connors, 32 weeks pregnant. Severe blunt trauma from a car accident. The fetal heart rate is dropping, and we’re losing maternal vitals fast.”

Agatha moved quickly to the bedside, assessing the woman’s condition in an instant. Her vitals were low, and there was a palpable sense of urgency in the room. She placed her hand on the woman’s abdomen, gently pressing. There was a slight shift in the fetal heartbeat monitor—a slow, irregular beat.

She snapped into action.

“Get an ultrasound, stat. We need to confirm fetal positioning and any signs of placental abruption,” Agatha ordered, her voice calm but authoritative. “Start two large-bore IVs, and prepare for a possible emergency C-section depending on what we find.”

The team responded immediately, the nurses already in motion, and the ultrasound technician rushed in with the portable machine. Agatha’s eyes remained on the monitor as she observed the fetal heart rate pattern. The baby’s distress was evident, and it was clear they were running out of time. But the mother’s instability added another layer of complexity to the situation.

The ultrasound revealed a significant placental abruption, with signs of hemorrhaging that threatened both the woman’s life and the baby’s.

“Prepare for immediate surgery,” Agatha commanded. “We’re going to need an emergency C-section. Both of them are in jeopardy. Let’s move.”

As the team worked to get the mother prepped for surgery, Agatha’s mind was already in overdrive, calculating the risks. She needed to act fast, but with precision. There was no room for error when both lives were on the line.

"I understand the fetal distress, but we need to be careful here. This woman is in critical condition. Her BP is dropping rapidly. If we proceed with the C-section too quickly, we risk hemorrhaging her out before we even get to the baby. we need to stabilize the leg first” Agtatha stiffened. Of course they had called her.

Dr Vidal approached the patient. She was already reviewing the medical chart in her hands, her brow furrowed.

Agatha’s jaw tightened. "We don’t have the luxury of time," she replied, her voice cool but firm. "This is a case of placental abruption, and the longer we wait, the more we risk both of them. The baby’s heart rate is already dropping. If we don’t act now, we could lose both mother and child."

Dr. Vidal met Agatha’s gaze with a cool, measured look. There was no doubt in her eyes—she wasn’t backing down, but she wasn’t pushing further either. Agatha could feel the subtle challenge hanging between them. Vidal, the young rising star, already positioning herself as an equal in the room. But Agatha knew this was no time for professional posturing.

“The hemorrhaging from the leg injury is significant,” Vidal said, her voice low but steady. “If we don’t stabilize the leg first, she’ll bleed out before we get to the baby. You know that as well as I do, Dr. Harkness. We need to prioritize.”

Agatha took a sharp breath, her mind working quickly. She wanted to trust her instincts—take the baby first, save the child before it was too late—but Vidal had a point. If they didn’t stem the blood loss from the leg, the mother might not survive long enough for the C-section.

Her fingers tightened into fists at her sides, but she didn’t hesitate. “You’re right. Stabilize the leg first, but we move fast. We don’t wait around.”

Vidal nodded, a glint of something like respect—or maybe just a shared understanding—flashing across her face. "We’ll need a full blood transfusion set up as well. I'll call for it now."

As the team shifted into action, Agatha kept a sharp eye on the monitors. There was no room for hesitation in a case like this, not with two lives at stake. While Dr  Vidal coordinated the stabilization of the woman’s leg, she kept one eye on the fetal heart rate. It was dangerously low, a rhythmic pattern that spoke of deep distress.

Minutes felt like hours. Vidal’s hands were steady, her expertise undeniable as she worked on the leg injury, but Agatha couldn’t shake the feeling of being caught in a race against time.

“Prep the OR,” Agatha barked, her voice commanding. “We’re not wasting a second after this.”

The team moved with precision, their movements synchronized under Agatha’s leadership. Vidal didn’t falter, though Agatha could sense the tension in the air. They were both calculating, both trying to find a way to save both mother and child. But Agatha’s mind stayed focused on the baby’s heart rate, dropping dangerously low by the second.

“We’re almost there,” Vidal said, her voice calm amidst the chaos. “Get the mother ready for transport, and we’ll move as soon as we have the leg stabilized.”

Agatha gave a sharp nod, her gaze never leaving the monitor. “Get her to the OR. I’ll make the final call on the C-section.”

The decision was made. In that moment, both of their expertise blended—Vidal’s precision with trauma and Agatha’s unwavering focus on neonatal care. They’d work together, for now, but Agatha knew that this was her room, her call. There was no room for compromise when lives were on the line.

As the patient was prepped and moved into the operating room, Agatha’s heart raced, but her hands were steady. This was what she did best—this was where she thrived. The trauma, the pressure, the stakes—it all came down to this moment.

Agatha rushed into the changing room, tossing her clothes aside as she quickly donned the hospital scrubs. Then, she hurried to scrub into the OR. Dr. Vidal was already there, washing her hands.

“What are you doing here?” Agatha said sharply. “You already stabilized the leg on the trauma bay. I'll get someone to page you when i'm done with the c section”

Dr. Vidal didn’t flinch. “I need to install the external fixators on her femur before you do that or she’ll bleed to death before you even get the chance to deliver the baby. Rushing into a C-section without stabilizing could cause more harm than good. We need to stabilize her first, get her blood pressure up, and manage the bleeding before making any decisions about the baby.”

Agatha didn’t take her eyes off her. “We’re beyond stabilization, Vidal. Every minute we delay, we lose precious time for that baby. I’m not about to sit here and wait for her to bleed out while we ‘stabilize.’ We do both, and we do it now.”

The tension between them was palpable, the room momentarily still as their differing opinions clashed. Agatha had seen what could happen when you hesitated in situations like this—she wasn’t going to let that happen today.

Rio’s eyes narrowed, her voice lowering but still sharp. “I’m not saying we wait forever. I’m saying we don’t throw caution to the wind and jeopardize the mother’s survival for the sake of a hasty decision. We need a controlled, careful approach.”

Agatha took a step closer to her, her stance unwavering. “A controlled approach is a luxury we don’t have. The baby is already in distress. If we don’t deliver now, we’re playing a dangerous game.”

The silence stretched, both surgeons unwilling to back down. For a brief moment, the team hovered, unsure whether to follow Agatha’s directive for immediate action or Rio’s cautious approach. But Agatha’s authority was undeniable, and she knew that if they didn’t follow her lead, they would all lose precious time.

She turned to the anesthesiologist, who had been waiting for directions. “Get the patient intubated, stat. We’re going in.” Her gaze flicked back to Dr. Vidal. “We’re doing this my way. I’ll take full responsibility for the outcome.”

Rio looked at her for a long moment, her jaw clenched. Then, with a sharp nod, she stepped aside. “Fine,” she said, her tone still tense. “But you’re on the hook for this, Agatha.”

Agatha met her eyes without hesitation. “I’m not stupid. I know the risks. But I’m not willing to lose a mother and child because we second-guess ourselves. I’ll deliver the baby, and then you’ll handle the leg.

With the final decision made, the tension in the room remained thick, but the team quickly rallied into action. Agatha didn’t have the time or the patience to question her choices now. She was focused—utterly committed to the task at hand. As the team prepared for surgery, she placed her hands on the woman’s abdomen, steadying herself for the high-stakes operation that would determine the lives of both mother and baby.

There was no room for error, no room for hesitation. And she was determined to make sure that, no matter the outcome, she wouldn’t back down.

Agatha’s thoughts flickered briefly to Nicky. But there was no time to dwell on it. This was her job—her responsibility. She couldn’t afford distractions now. In the operating room, it would be all hands on deck, and Agatha had to ensure both the mother and child survived.

“Let’s go,” Agatha said, her voice sharp as she scrubbed in. The room went quiet except for the hum of machines and the soft shuffle of her team as they prepared for the delicate, high-stakes procedure. The pressure was immense, but Agatha was used to it. She thrived in these moments. And right now, every second counted.

The team moved quickly into action, each person seamlessly stepping into their role, the choreography of the OR honed by countless hours of training and instinct. The anesthesiologist focused on maintaining the patient’s fragile vitals, calling out updates while the surgical assistants worked efficiently to prepare for the emergency C-section. The atmosphere was thick with urgency, the hum of the monitors a constant reminder of how precarious the situation was.

Agatha’s mind was razor-sharp, her focus unyielding. She couldn’t afford a single misstep. Every second counted. The patient’s vitals were dropping—a precarious mix of tachycardia and plummeting blood pressure. Agatha’s gloved hands moved with practiced precision as she made the first incision, her scalpel cutting through layers of skin, subcutaneous fat, and fascia. The bleeding was heavier than expected, but she worked quickly, adjusting her technique to minimize further loss.

“Retractor,” Agatha barked, her voice steady despite the tension crackling in the air. The surgical assistant handed her the tool, and she positioned it with precision, exposing the uterine wall.

Dr. Vidal stood just outside the sterile field, arms crossed, her eyes sharp and assessing. Agatha could feel the weight of her gaze but didn’t acknowledge it. This wasn’t the time for egos or friction. This was about the mother and the baby, and nothing else mattered.

The baby’s heart rate flickered on the monitor—slow and erratic. Agatha felt her own heartbeat sync with the fragile rhythm. Her next incision was deliberate, cutting through the uterus and exposing the amniotic sac. A gush of fluid spilled out, and for a split second, time seemed to freeze.

“Come on,” Agatha muttered under her breath, her hands reaching into the uterus. She felt the baby’s small, fragile body—a startling contrast to the clinical intensity around her. She cradled the infant’s slippery form in her hands, lifting it out with practiced care.

“Baby’s out!” she called, her voice ringing with authority. The neonatal team was already at her side, and she handed the baby over, watching as they sprang into action. The room seemed to collectively hold its breath as the team worked to resuscitate the newborn.

Agatha’s focus snapped back to the mother. The uterine wall was bleeding profusely, and the patient’s blood pressure was dropping even further. “Suction,” she demanded, and the nurse responded immediately. She worked quickly to control the hemorrhage, applying pressure and suturing with relentless precision.

Dr. Vidal moved in as Agatha stabilized the uterus, ready to address the open fracture in the patient’s right leg. The bone was exposed, jagged and white against the bruised and swollen flesh. Vidal’s hands were steady as she prepared the external fixators. For the first time since their earlier clash, their movements aligned, their shared professionalism overriding any lingering tension.

“Vitals holding steady,” the anesthesiologist reported, her voice strained but steady.

“Good,” Agatha said, her eyes flicking over to Vidal, who was already applying the external fixators. The tension between them had simmered down to a cold professional respect, though neither one had forgotten the earlier standoff.

The neonatal team’s soft cheer broke through the tension. The baby had let out a faint, fragile cry—a sound that sent a ripple of relief through the room. Agatha didn’t let herself pause. There was still work to be done.

“Let’s close her up,” she said firmly, her voice cutting through the quiet hum of the monitors. The surgical team moved in perfect harmony, each step calculated and efficient. Agatha worked quickly, suturing the final layers of tissue and ensuring no further bleeding threatened the patient’s stability.

As the team worked to finish the procedure, she took a moment to breathe, her muscles aching from the intensity of the surgery.

When the procedure was finally complete, Agatha stepped back, her hands trembling slightly as the adrenaline began to fade. The patient’s vitals were stabilizing, the baby was alive, and they had done everything they could. 

She exhaled deeply, the tension slowly beginning to ease from her shoulders.

As the team moved to prepare for the next steps, Agatha felt a strange mixture of exhaustion and satisfaction. The weight of the day was heavy, but in that moment, she knew she had done what was necessary.

Dr. Vidal looked up from her work, her expression unreadable but calm. “Good” she said, her tone devoid of its earlier edge.

Agatha nodded curtly, too exhausted to do more than acknowledge the sentiment. The tension between them hadn’t dissipated entirely, but for now, they had fought the same battle and won. As the team began to clean up and prepare for the transfer to the ICU, Agatha allowed herself one deep breath of relief. The hardest part was over—at least for now.

The door to the OR swung open, and a nurse entered with an update. “The baby’s stable, Dr. Harkness. He’s in the NICU now, and the mother’s blood pressure is starting to stabilize.”

Agatha’s heart gave a quiet leap. She didn’t allow herself to show it, but there was relief in her chest. The hardest part was over.

As Agatha and Dr. Vidal exited the OR, the harsh fluorescent lights flickered above them, casting long shadows on the sterile hospital floor. The tension between them was suffocating, the air thick with unresolved anger. The surgery was over, but the battle for control had only just begun. Agatha could feel Vidal’s presence like a weight behind her, her every step a challenge. Agatha refused to turn around. She wasn’t in the mood to deal with this.

The smell of antiseptic still clung to Agatha’s senses as they walked down the corridor, the adrenaline of the OR slowly giving way to the weight of exhaustion. Her mind was already on the next steps in the case, but Vidal wasn’t going to let her focus shift just yet.

“What the hell was that?” Vidal’s voice was a sharp, cutting whisper. Agatha stopped walking but didn’t turn to face her. She knew exactly what this was about. And she didn’t have the energy to entertain it.

“What was what, Dr. Vidal?” Agatha’s voice came out flat, brittle from the pressure, but controlled. Just barely.

Vidal stood just behind her, arms crossed, her eyes seething with barely contained fury. “I understand your urgency in the OR, but we can’t just rush through these decisions. The mother’s still in critical condition, and you didn’t listen when I suggested stabilizing her leg first. If you’d waited—just for a few minutes—we could’ve avoided all that unnecessary risk. You don’t get to call the shots on your own here.”

Agatha spun on her heel, fury igniting in her chest. “You think I don’t know that?” she snapped, taking a step forward, her voice hardening like a blade. “Every second in that OR was a decision, and I chose the baby. I’m not going to let a child die while we sit around and waste time debating the best sequence of events. This was about life or death, not playing it safe.”

Vidal’s jaw clenched, her nostrils flaring as her anger bubbled to the surface. “You’re reckless. You acted out of desperation. That was a gamble, Dr Harkness—one you weren’t prepared for.” Her voice dropped, cold and cutting. “And I won’t let you pretend that your decision was some perfect, well-thought-out plan. You didn’t listen. You ignored my recommendation, and we both know it.”

The words hit Agatha like a slap, but she wasn’t about to let Vidal get the better of her. She took a step closer, her voice low and dangerous. “You weren’t the one who had to make that call. I didn’t ‘gamble,’ I made a choice—and I stand by it. If you want to throw stones, fine. But don’t mistake me for someone who second-guesses their decisions when lives are on the line.”

For a long moment, Vidal said nothing. The silence between them was tense, raw, and neither one seemed willing to back down. Agatha met Vidal’s glare, unflinching, her heart hammering but her resolve unwavering. Then, Vidal spoke again, her voice quieter, but still carrying an undeniable bite.

“You think you’re the only one who cares about saving lives? That you’re the only one who’s willing to do whatever it takes?” Vidal’s eyes darkened. “I didn’t sign up for this to be sidelined by someone too proud to listen to the people around them.”

Agatha’s pulse quickened, and her chest tightened. She wasn’t going to let Vidal turn this into a personal attack. She bit back the urge to snap back and instead pressed her lips into a thin line. “This isn’t about pride, Vidal. This is about doing what needs to be done in the moment, not sitting on our hands because someone wants to play it safe. If you want to lecture me on caution, do it with someone who has time to entertain it. Today, there was no time. Not with that baby’s heart rate dropping. Not with that mother in the state she was in.”

Vidal didn’t respond immediately. She inhaled deeply, a controlled breath, like she was steadying herself for something. The silence between them stretched taut, the weight of their words hanging in the air like smoke. Finally, Vidal spoke again, her voice quieter but no less hard-edged.

“Fine,” she said, her eyes flashing with something fierce. She stepped closer to Agatha, invading her personal space, and Agatha could feel the heat of Vidal’s body radiating toward her, unable to move. “You’ve got this one. But don’t get comfortable, Harkness. I won’t fall in line just because you gave yourself a higher rank today. I won’t let you dismiss me—or anyone else—just because you think you have all the answers. This is a team effort. And you’d better remember that.”

Agatha stood her ground, every muscle in her body stiff with tension. She didn’t flinch. She wasn’t backing down. Not now. Not ever. “You think I don’t know what it means to be part of a team? I didn’t make that decision alone, Vidal. But I’m the one who called the shots. And I’ll continue to do what needs to be done—whether you like it or not.”

The room was cold, the air between them crackling with hostility, and for a split second, it seemed like they might go on fighting, endlessly, until someone gave in. But then, Agatha turned away, her shoulders tight, and started walking again, every step a silent refusal to engage further. The last thing she needed was to fight over every decision she made.

As she walked through the hospital, Agatha’s mind began shifting back to the case, back to the next steps. She had done what was necessary to save the mother and baby. Everything else—Vidal’s challenge, the power struggle—was irrelevant. The patient, the baby, her own decisions—that was all that mattered. Vidal could stew over her pride for all Agatha cared.

Vidal was right about one thing: they would have to find a way to work together. But Agatha would always be the one making the tough calls. Always.

Chapter 6

Agatha paced the dimly lit corridor outside the ICU, her footsteps echoing softly against the sterile tiles. The weight of the day pressed down on her, but it wasn’t the exhaustion in her muscles or the ache in her lower back that gnawed at her. It was the guilt—a creeping, insidious thing that clung to her chest and whispered doubts into her mind.

She did what needed to be done. That was what she told herself. That was what she had to believe. But as the minutes went by, her confidence wavered. Had she truly made the right call? Would stabilizing the mother’s leg first have changed the outcome? Would it have spared the patient some of the trauma her body had endured?

Agatha stopped walking and leaned against the wall, pinching the bridge of her nose. She could still feel Vidal’s sharp words echoing in her ears, and could still see the accusation in her dark eyes.

"Reckless."

The word stung more than it should have. She had always prided herself on her composure, her ability to make the tough calls under pressure. But today, she had acted on instinct, and that instinct had saved a life. Two lives, she corrected herself. She had delivered the baby, stabilized the mother, and stopped the bleeding. That was what mattered. That had to be enough.

But what if it wasn’t?

Agatha pushed herself off the wall and walked toward the nurses’ station. Her steps felt heavier now, each one weighed down by the what-ifs swirling in her mind. A tired nurse glanced up from the computer, her eyes sympathetic.

"Dr. Harkness," the nurse said softly. "You should go home. It’s late, and we’ve got things under control here."

Agatha shook her head. "I’m not leaving until we find someone from her family," she said, her voice hoarse from hours of use. "Any updates on that?"

The nurse sighed, glancing at the file. "Not yet. We’ve called the emergency contacts listed, but no one’s picked up. Social services is working on it."

"Let me know the moment you hear anything," Agatha replied. She turned away before the nurse could protest further, heading for the small on-call room down the hall.

Inside, the room was dark and quiet, the faint hum of the overhead light a constant presence. Agatha sank onto the thin cot, her body folding forward as she rested her elbows on her knees. She stared at the floor, her mind replaying the surgery in relentless detail. Every incision, every suture, every decision she had made flashed through her mind like a film reel stuck on repeat.

The baby’s weak cry echoed in her memory, a sound that had momentarily pierced through her exhaustion and doubt. That cry had been a victory, a fragile reminder of why she had fought so hard. But the mother was still in critical condition, her body battered and fragile. The machines were doing most of the work to keep her alive, and Agatha knew the road ahead would be long and uncertain.

Her phone buzzed on the metal table beside the cot, pulling her from her thoughts. She picked it up and glanced at the screen: a message from the night-shift resident.

"No updates on the family yet. Patient stable for now. Neonatal team says the baby is improving."

Agatha exhaled slowly, the tension in her chest loosening just a fraction. The baby was improving. That was something. She set the phone down and leaned back against the wall, closing her eyes.

The guilt still lingered, heavy and persistent, but she pushed it aside. There was no room for it right now. She needed to be ready—for the mother, for the baby, for the inevitable questions that would come in the morning.

Agatha stared at the clock, the numbers staring back at her like a cruel reminder of everything she was missing. It was 12:06 AM. She wasn’t even on call, but here she was anyways—still at the hospital, still tethered to this sterile world of beeping monitors and urgent decisions. Nicky was at home, likely asleep, and she wouldn’t see him until morning. Guilt wormed its way into her chest, a slow, insidious thing that tightened around her heart. Was Daniel right? Had she really let her work consume her?

She hated this feeling—this gnawing sense of inadequacy. She was supposed to be a mother first, wasn’t she? But here she was, choosing to stay behind while her son slept alone, as if the world outside the OR could wait. But the truth was, it couldn’t. There was always another life to save, always another crisis, and she couldn’t be in two places at once.

Maybe she was too absorbed in the work, too focused on the lives that hung in the balance in these cold, clinical rooms. She was married to her job, and it felt like everything else—Nicky, her family, even herself—was a distant second.

The thought of going home felt impossible. She would do one last check—on the baby, on the mother—and then she’d leave. But even as she thought it, she knew it wasn’t that simple. Leaving meant leaving a part of herself behind, a part of the commitment that drove her to this profession in the first place. Her shift had ended hours ago, but duty didn’t let her off the hook so easily.

Seven hours. That’s all she had before she had to be back at the hospital for rounds. Seven hours to sleep, to attempt some fleeting semblance of normalcy. But sleep felt like a distant luxury. The weight of the decisions made today, the lives hanging in the balance, would follow her home, haunting her even in the quiet moments of rest.

She wanted to leave. She needed to leave . But every fiber of her being, every instinct honed by years of hard-earned experience, told her that the work wasn’t done yet. With those thoughts on her mind she made her way into the NICU.

The sterile smell of the neonatal unit hit Agatha the moment she walked in. The soft beeping of the monitors, the hum of the ventilator—everything in this room was designed to keep life alive, to hold it together when the world outside felt like it was falling apart. Agatha paused by the incubator where the tiny baby lay, a fragile, miraculous bundle of life surrounded by wires and tubes. The baby’s chest rose and fell with a steady rhythm, the heartbeat strong and sure now. Agatha reached out, her fingers brushing the glass, a silent gesture of connection.

The neonate’s skin was still so thin, almost translucent in places, but the baby was breathing on its own now. It had made it through the worst, and for that, Agatha allowed herself a moment of relief. A small, weak cry from the infant sent a flutter through her chest—nothing like the gut-wrenching fear of its earlier distress, but a sign of life, of progress.

The pediatric team had done their job, and now all she could do was wait, just as they were. But for now, the baby was stable. And that was something to hold onto.

She turned and left the neonatal unit, her steps dragging as she made her way to the ICU. The mother was still critical, and Agatha’s mind could not let go of her—she had made the decision, fought for both lives, but now the weight of it all was heavier than ever. The mother’s chances were still uncertain. She wasn’t out of the woods, and there was no guarantee she’d make it through the night.

The ICU was quiet, except for the constant beep of monitors and the soft shuffle of nurses moving between beds. Agatha stood at the door to the woman's room for a long moment, taking in the sight of the pale, unconscious woman, the blood pressure cuff still inflated, the IV lines running into her veins.

Agatha walked to her side, pulling up the chair and sitting quietly next to the bed. She didn’t speak. There was nothing to say, really. Just the soft whoosh of the ventilator and the rhythmic beeping of the monitors filled the room, the air heavy with an unspoken tension.

Her eyes drifted to the woman’s pale face, the bruising on her abdomen and the signs of trauma visible even under the dim hospital lighting. Agatha had done what she could, but the feeling of powerlessness lingered. She reached out, lightly touching her hand, her fingers briefly closing around the limp fingers, as if holding on to some tiny shred of hope.

The exhaustion finally caught up with Agatha, her body heavy with the strain of the long hours. She leaned her head against the bed’s edge, her eyes closing just for a moment. But that moment stretched into something longer. The events of the day—of the surgery, of the choices made—blended together, the adrenaline fading into a dull ache in her bones.

She didn’t even notice when sleep took over completely.

----------------------------

The soft click of the door opening the next morning stirred her awake. Agatha blinked, disoriented for a moment as she straightened in her seat. Her neck ached, and she realized she must have dozed off sitting at the mother’s bedside. The hospital room was still quiet, but now there was another presence in the room.

Dr. Vidal stood in the doorway, her eyes flicking over to Agatha before moving to the woman in the bed. Vidal’s expression was unreadable, but there was something else there — something beyond professional concern. Agatha pushed herself up from the chair, feeling the weight of the night in her every movement.

Agatha blinked, the remnants of sleep still clouding her vision. Her neck throbbed from the awkward position she had been in, but the dull ache was nothing compared to the heaviness in her chest. As the light from the hallway filtered into the room. What time is it? She had promised herself she’d leave before the next shift began, but here she was, still in the hospital, still tethered to this place.

Her gaze flicked back to Vidal, who was standing in the doorway, looking as composed as ever. For a moment, Agatha felt exposed, as if Vidal could see all the cracks she was desperately trying to keep hidden beneath her professional exterior.

“You stayed,” Vidal said, her voice low but surprisingly soft. It wasn’t an accusation. Just a statement.

Agatha hesitated, the words she’d been rehearsing in her mind since the moment she’d sat down slipping away in the face of Vidal’s quiet question. She glanced back at the mother, still unconscious, but stable for now, before she answered.

She sighed and stood, her legs unsteady beneath her. “What time is it?” she asked, her voice quieter now.

“Six-fifteen,” Vidal replied. “Have you been here all night?”

Agatha felt a pang of guilt. 

Agatha’s mind reeled as the words sank in.

Six-fifteen AM.

The reality hit her like a ton of bricks. The hours had slipped away unnoticed, lost in the blur of the night’s chaos and the weight of decisions she hadn’t even fully processed. Six hours had passed since she decided to leave. And not once in that time had she thought of anything but the patient in the bed and the baby struggling to survive.

But Nicky…she hadn’t seen him. She hadn’t heard his voice, hadn’t put him to bed. The guilt settled deep in her stomach, a cold knot that wouldn’t loosen. What kind of mother does that?

Her heart clenched painfully as the realization sank deeper. Her son was probably waking up alone, only accompanied by his nanny. The thought of him in the morning light, confused and maybe even a little scared, gnawed at her.

She forced herself to swallow the sharp taste of guilt, but it didn’t go away. She could feel the weight of Vidal’s gaze on her, though she tried her best to keep it together. Don’t let her see you like this, Agatha thought desperately. Don’t let her see how badly you’re failing.

Vidal was watching her with an unreadable expression, the same steady gaze she always wore. Yet Agatha could see the slightest flicker of understanding in her eyes. She knows, Agatha thought, she can see it in me.

Agatha’s hands trembled as she reached for the chair to steady herself. She had to leave. She had to get to Nicky. But the weight of the past hours—of everything she’d done, of everything she hadn’t done—was pressing down on her. And she felt like she was suffocating in it.

“I... I need to go,” Agatha said, her voice strained. She turned away quickly, trying to hide the turmoil from Vidal, but she knew it was pointless. She wasn’t fooling anyone, least of all herself.

She made her way toward the door, the exhaustion in her every step. But as she reached the threshold, she heard Vidal’s voice behind her, softer than before.

“I’ll finish here,” Vidal said, her voice pulling Agatha from her spiraling thoughts. 

Agatha turned her head slightly to acknowledge Vidal, giving her a brief, silent thanks. She couldn’t bring herself to say it aloud, not when the weight of the night still pressed so heavily on her. But Vidal’s presence—steady, calm—had somehow made the situation a little easier to bear. Even if Agatha didn’t know how to forgive herself just yet, at least someone else believed she was doing her best.

With a final, deep breath, Agatha opened the door and stepped out into the hallway, the cool air of the early morning a stark contrast to the heat of the room she’d just left. Her footsteps were slow, each one heavy, but determined. Nicky needed her. And for the first time that night, Agatha allowed herself to believe she could still make it home to him.

—--------------------------

Agatha’s foot pressed harder on the accelerator as she sped down the quiet, empty streets. The rising sun had begun to paint the sky in shades of orange and pink, but she barely noticed. Her mind was fixed on one thing—getting home. She couldn’t afford to lose another precious moment with Nicky.

Her pulse quickened, but not from the adrenaline of driving recklessly. No, it was the thought of seeing her son’s face again, the reassurance she’d been missing all night. She couldn’t help the guilt that gnawed at her, but the thought of him still asleep, probably missing her warmth, gave her enough of a push to keep moving.

She parked her car in the driveway, barely giving herself a moment to breathe before slamming the door behind her and rushing inside. The familiar scent of the house—clean, slightly floral, and safe—did little to calm her nerves. The house was silent, but she knew he was here, somewhere. Her footsteps echoed as she moved quickly through the hall, passing his room where the door was slightly ajar.

She peeked inside. There he was, still curled up under his covers, his soft breathing rising and falling in gentle rhythm. He was so small, so innocent, and the sight of him made the tight knot in her chest loosen, if only a little. She stood there for a moment, taking in the peaceful image, trying to let the moment of calm wash over her before reality set in again.

She leaned in and gently nudged him awake, her voice soft but insistent. “Nicky,” she whispered, brushing a lock of his hair from his face. “Time to wake up, sweetheart.”

His eyes fluttered open slowly, and a sleepy smile stretched across his face as he blinked up at her. “Mommy?” he mumbled, rubbing his eyes. “You’re home?”

Agatha’s heart swelled, and for a moment, the weight of everything else disappeared. She reached down, smoothing his blankets and brushing his hair away from his forehead. “Yes, I’m home,” she whispered, her voice thick with emotion. “I’m sorry I wasn’t here when you went to sleep.”

“ I missed you." His arms stretched out toward her for a hug, and Agatha didn’t hesitate. He reached up for her, his small arms wrapping around her neck as she lifted him into her arms, holding him close. She kissed the top of his head, whispering sweet nothings, anything to make him feel like the most important thing in the world, because he was. In that moment, nothing else mattered.

But the clock was ticking. The time was slipping away, and she had already wasted too much of it. Agatha pulled herself together, setting Nicky back on his feet. “I have to take a quick shower, baby,” she said softly, smoothing his hair back once more. “You stay here and Karina will help you get dressed, okay?”

He nodded, though he looked at her with his usual trusting, wide-eyed gaze.

Agatha gave him one last kiss on the forehead before hurrying to the bathroom to take a quick shower. Her mind raced as she stripped off her clothes, the warmth of the shower engulfing her for just a few moments. She had no time to relax, no time to let the hot water soak away the exhaustion of the night. It was quick and efficient—just enough to make her feel human again, just enough to refresh her.

The moment she stepped out, she glanced at the clock. Time was slipping by too fast. She had twenty minutes before she needed to be at the hospital for rounds.

Agatha rushed to get dressed, pulling on a pair of black slacks, a white blouse, and a purple  blazer. She didn’t have time to perfect her appearance, so she focused on the essentials: pulling her hair back in a ponytail, making sure her badge was clipped to her chest, and giving her face a quick swipe of makeup to mask the almost sleepless night.

She found Nicky sitting on the couch, dressed in his favorite little outfit, his small shoes already on his feet, his face bright. He held up his stuffed bear and grinned. “Mommy, look! Teddy’s ready to go!”

Agatha smiled, a warm, genuine smile. “I see, Teddy’s ready to go. You’re ready to go, too, huh?”

Nicky nodded eagerly, standing up with a little bounce in his step. “I want to go to the park later, okay?”

She couldn’t help the bittersweet smile that tugged at her lips. “I promise, baby. We’ll go to the park. But right now, Mommy has to go to work, okay?”

He nodded solemnly but didn’t complain, and Agatha felt her heart squeeze in her chest. She wished she could stay with him longer, but the pull of duty was stronger.

She gave him one last kiss on the cheek before grabbing her bag, throwing it over her shoulder, and heading for the door. “I’ll be back soon, I promise. You and Teddy take care of each other.”

“Okay, Mommy,” Nicky said with a grin, waving as she stepped out the door.

Agatha paused in the hallway, taking one last look at her little boy, standing there in the doorway, his tiny hand waving goodbye. 

With that final thought, she turned and rushed out the door, already late for rounds, but her heart lightened knowing she’d seen her son before she stepped back into the chaos of the hospital.

Agatha’s steps were brisk as she navigated the hospital hallways, her mind a mix of exhaustion and determination. She had only a few minutes to pull herself together before she had to face the residents and start rounds. She hated this part of the day—the endless back-and-forth of checking charts, correcting mistakes, and reviewing cases with doctors who were still learning the ropes. But it was part of the job, and she was good at it, too good sometimes.

When she entered the round room, the residents immediately fell silent, as if they knew to brace for whatever was coming. Agatha’s eyes scanned the group, noting who was prepared and who was not. Dr. Larson, the third-year resident, was looking down at his shoes, clearly dreading her scrutiny. Dr. Patel was fiddling with a pen, avoiding eye contact. Perfect.

“Alright,” Agatha started, her voice low and controlled, though anyone who knew her could sense the tension simmering beneath the surface. “Let’s start with Mrs. Timmons, the trauma patient from last night. Who’s on her case?”

Dr. Larson shifted uncomfortably, looking to Patel for some kind of rescue. Neither of them was quick to respond, but Agatha had already anticipated this.

“Larson?” she said, her tone sharp.

He hesitated but finally spoke. “Uh, yes, Dr. Harkness. Mrs. Timmons is stable. We’ve managed the bleeding and fixed the fracture with external fixation. Her vitals have improved overnight.”

“Stable?” Agatha’s voice dropped a notch, as if the word itself left a sour taste in her mouth. “This is the woman who almost bled out on the table. Stable is not a word I use lightly. Did you adjust her fluids, increase the pressors? Did anyone check her for compartment syndrome after we stabilized her leg?”

Larson's eyes widened, his face flushed as he started to stutter through the answer. “Uh, we... we made adjustments to her IV fluids, Dr. Harkness. But we didn’t check for compartment syndrome. Should we... should we do that now?”

“God, yes,” Agatha snapped, cutting him off. “She’s in critical condition, and you’re talking about stable ? This isn’t a textbook case, Larson. She’s a living, breathing person, and she could deteriorate in an instant if you don’t stay ahead of the curve. Get your head in the game.”

The other residents shifted uneasily, and Agatha could practically feel their collective anxiety. She didn’t care. They had to learn, and sometimes the lessons weren’t gentle. It wasn’t enough to just pass through medical school or residency. They had to think. They had to anticipate .

She turned to Dr. Patel next. “How are we doing with the baby? The newborn?”

Patel cleared her throat and flipped through her notes. “The infant is stable, Dr. Harkness. The neonatologists managed to stabilize her respiratory function overnight. We’re monitoring for any complications, but as of now, she’s responding well to treatment.”

Agatha nodded, but the weight of the baby’s fragile condition was never far from her mind. She had pulled the child out of the womb herself, and despite the team’s best efforts, the tiny form was always in a precarious state. She didn’t let her relief show, though. Not here. Not in front of the residents.

“Make sure the neonatologists keep a close eye on her,” Agatha continued, her voice lowering as she added a note of warning. “If there are any changes in the baby's status, I want to be informed immediately. This isn't over yet.”

As Agatha’s eyes swept across the group, she saw a few more uncertain glances. The room felt too small, the air too thick. She hated the silence, and she could feel the tension seeping into her skin. They needed to learn how to be confident—how to think critically under pressure. That was what she had tried to instill in them since she’d started overseeing rounds.

“Now, moving on,” Agatha said, her voice still carrying that sharp edge. “Who’s on Mrs. Timmons' pain management? Have we started her on a post-operative analgesia plan?”

Dr. Lee, the senior resident, spoke up. “We’ve started her on a PCA pump for now, Dr. Harkness. We’ve also set up a protocol for switching to oral pain management once she's more stable.”

Agatha raised an eyebrow, scanning Lee’s face. “Is that the most appropriate choice, given her blood pressure? I’d expect you to consider her comorbidities, the risk of opioid-induced respiratory depression, and monitor accordingly.”

Lee nodded quickly, taking notes. “Yes, Dr. Harkness. We’ll adjust the dose and monitor more closely.”

“Good. And remember,” Agatha added, voice softening slightly, “it’s not about what’s easiest. It’s about what’s safest. Always.”

She turned to the final case of the morning, running through the last few charts and providing a final assessment of each case. She was hard on the residents, but the truth was, she expected nothing less than perfection. She had no room for mistakes—especially not after the events of the night before.

Finally, the rounds ended, and Agatha stood by the door, her mind already racing to the day ahead of her. 

As Agatha stepped out of the ward, she was greeted by the familiar click of heels approaching her. Turning, she found Dr. Calderu, her sharp eyes and warm smile cutting through the haze of Agatha’s exhaustion. Calderu was impeccably dressed as always, her tailored blazer giving her an air of effortless authority.

“Agatha,” Dr Calderu greeted, her tone friendly but with that ever-present undertone of expectation. “Just the person I wanted to see.”

Agatha adjusted her grip on the clipboard, forcing herself to match Calderu’s smile. “Morning, Dr Calderu. Everything alright?”

“Of course. And remember darling you can call me Lilia. Just wanted to check about tonight,” Calderu said, tilting her head slightly. 

Tonight? Her mind started racing. What was happening tonight? 

And then it hit her.

Dinner .

Agatha’s stomach sank. She had forgotten. Completely. Her schedule was already packed, and now she was supposed to attend a semi-formal dinner with her peers. 

Her hesitation must have shown on her face because Calderu’s smile shifted into something more knowing. “You forgot,” she said, not unkindly. “Don’t worry, it happens to all of us. It’s at eight, at Della Casa. You’ll be there, right?”

Agatha nodded quickly, forcing a reassuring tone. “Of course I will be there. I already confirmed my attendance, remember? I Won't miss it.”

Calderu raised an eyebrow, her gaze lingering on Agatha’s face. “ We are looking forward to that. Everyone is really excited to get to know you” she paused for a second “ You look tired. You should grab some rest before then—or at least a strong coffee.”

Agatha gave a faint chuckle. “I’ll take that under advisement.”

As Dr Calderu walked away, the weight of the evening ahead settled on Agatha’s shoulders. Another obligation, another role to play. She exhaled, mentally rearranging her day to fit it all in. 

Her phone buzzed in her pocket, and she instinctively reached for it, hoping it wasn’t another emergency. Instead, it was a picture from Nicky’s nanny—a photo of him eating breakfast, his cheeks sticky with syrup and his bright eyes lighting up the screen.

Agatha’s heart twisted. She’d made it home to wake him up, but her time with him had been a blur. Now, as she stared at his face, she felt a pang of guilt that even the dinner with the department heads couldn’t match.

This afternoon, before dinner, she would make it up to him, she thought.

It wasn’t optional. She had to— she must.

Chapter 7

Before Agatha left the hospital, she took a detour back to the ICU to check on the mother and baby. She’d told herself she could trust the team to handle things, but her mind wouldn’t let it rest. The long corridors were quieter now, the buzz of the day winding down to a low hum.

She stepped into the mother’s room, her footsteps soft as though afraid to disturb the stillness. The woman lay as she had that morning—unconscious, pale, but stable. Machines beeped rhythmically, a reassuring sign that her body was holding on. Agatha scanned the monitors, her practiced eyes taking in the data in an instant. No major changes, no signs of deterioration.

Still no visitors, no family. The ache in Agatha’s chest deepened. How could someone endure so much alone? She stood there for a moment longer, her hand brushing the edge of the bed. The faint sound of the ventilator filled the silence, and Agatha silently promised herself she wouldn’t let this woman fade unnoticed. 

She hesitated outside the NICU next. The baby—so tiny, so fragile—had been occupying a corner of her mind all day. The sight of those little fingers and translucent skin still haunted her, even in the flurry of rounds. Peering through the glass, she saw the incubator, surrounded by attentive nurses and the steady hum of monitors. She wanted to go in, to look closer, but she held herself back. The neonatologists were on it; she needed to trust them.

Her phone buzzed in her pocket. She pulled it out and saw an email from Dr. Vidal.

Subject: Update on trauma patients
Both mother and baby are stable. No significant changes since morning. Neonatology will update if anything shifts. Get some rest, Dr Harkness. You’ve done enough today.

Dr Vidal

Agatha exhaled, her shoulders loosening slightly. Vidal’s brief message carried its usual calm precision, but Agatha sensed the unspoken care beneath it. She closed her phone and slipped it back into her pocket. She didn’t need to go looking for Vidal now. Her job was done—for today.

As she exited the hospital, her thoughts lingered on the ICU. The mother and baby were stable, but stability was fragile. Still, she’d done everything she could. For now, it would have to be enough.

By the time Agatha finally left the hospital, the winter sun hung low in the sky, painting the city in golden hues. Her steps quickened as she approached her car, her mind already shifting from the sterile, controlled chaos of the hospital to the warm, messy joy of home.

Nicky was already home waiting for her. She knew it from the picture the nanny had sent earlier: Nicky standing by the front window, his tiny hands pressed against the glass, watching for her. The thought brought a pang of guilt mixed with determination. She had promised herself she’d make time for him today, no matter what.

The drive home felt longer than usual, her fingers tapping against the steering wheel as she navigated through the slow crawl of evening traffic. When she finally pulled into the driveway, she saw Nicky’s small frame behind the curtains, the fabric shifting as he jumped up and down, waving excitedly.

The moment she stepped through the door, Nicky launched himself at her, his arms wrapping tightly around her legs. “Mommy!” he cried, his voice filled with delight.

Agatha dropped her bag and scooped him up, holding him close. “Hey, kiddo,” she said softly, pressing a kiss to his cheek. “Did you have a good day?”

“Uh-huh!” Nicky nodded enthusiastically. “Can we go to the park now? Please, Mommy? Please!”

Agatha glanced at the clock. Dinner with the department heads was looming, but there was still time—time for just the two of them. “Let’s do it,” she said, setting him down. “Go grab your shoes and coat. I’ll be ready in a second.”

Nicky darted off, and Agatha quickly swapped her heels for sneakers and grabbed a warmer jacket. Within minutes, they were out the door, Nicky skipping ahead as they made their way to the neighborhood park.

The park was alive with the sounds of children laughing, the rhythmic creak of swings, and the occasional bark of a dog. Nicky ran straight for the slide, his small legs pumping as he climbed to the top. Agatha settled onto a bench nearby, her eyes never leaving him as he waved at her from the platform.

“Watch me, Mommy!” he called before sliding down with a squeal of pure joy.

“I’m watching!” she called back, smiling despite the ache of exhaustion still lingering in her body. For the first time that day, the weight on her chest felt a little lighter.

As the minutes stretched into an hour, Agatha chased Nicky around the playground, helped him on the monkey bars, and laughed as he insisted on burying her shoes in the sandbox. She was fully present, her mind nowhere but here, with him.

By the time they headed home, the sky was streaked with the deep purples and oranges of dusk. Nicky’s hand was warm in hers, his steps slowing as the excitement of the day began to catch up with him.

“You’re the best, Mommy,” he said sleepily, leaning against her side.

Agatha squeezed his hand, her heart swelling. “And you’re my favorite little guy.”

The dinner still loomed ahead, but for now, this moment was hers and Nicky’s—simple, fleeting, and perfect.

Agatha returned home from the park with Nicky, her heart lighter from the sound of his laughter and the feel of his small hand in hers. He had insisted on showing her every flower, every leaf, and every bug he found, his excitement contagious. She had let herself be fully present in those moments, his joy washing away some of the day's heaviness.

Once inside, Nicky darted to the living room, babbling about his adventures to the nanny. Agatha smiled, but her mind had already shifted to the evening ahead. She kissed the top of Nicky's head, promising she’d read him a story before bed, and headed upstairs.

The bathroom filled with steam as Agatha stepped under the hot shower. She closed her eyes, letting the water cascade over her, washing away the sweat and strain of the day. The tension in her shoulders eased as she scrubbed away the remnants of the hospital—the smell of antiseptic, the fatigue clinging to her like a second skin.

After drying off, she stood before her closet, her hand brushing past casual blouses and trousers until she reached the dress she’d mentally reserved for evenings like this. It was a black dress, sleek and tailored, the fabric hugging her figure in all the right places without feeling overt. The neckline dipped just enough to hint at her collarbones, and the hemline ended modestly above the knee, balancing sophistication with allure. The subtle cinch at the waist highlighted her curves, but the design remained elegant, understated.

She slipped on the dress, smoothing the fabric over her hips, and glanced in the mirror. Her reflection looked polished, almost unrecognizable from the woman in scrubs from a few hours earlier.

From her jewelry box, she selected a pair of earrings—a stunning set of purple diamond drops that caught the light with every movement. As she fastened them, the cold metal brushing her skin, she felt a flicker of confidence return.

Agatha completed the look with her LV heels, the black leather sleek and glossy, their design minimal yet undeniably expensive. She stood a little taller as she stepped into them, the familiar click of the soles against the floor a reminder of the power she carried when she walked into a room.

A light spritz of perfume, subtle and warm, lingered in the air as she grabbed her clutch. Glancing one last time at her reflection, she allowed herself a small smile. The day had been long, but tonight she was ready to face the world again—polished, poised, and undeniably Agatha.

As Agatha descended the stairs, the click of her heels resonating softly against the hardwood, her thoughts churned. The prospect of this dinner sat uneasily in her stomach, an unwelcome obligation she couldn’t quite dodge. It wasn’t the event itself that bothered her—she’d attended countless formal dinners over the years. It was what this one symbolized.

The “family-style” dynamic Dr. Calderu had so enthusiastically described during her first day at Mayo Clinic made her skin crawl. The idea of a surgical team treating each other like siblings or cousins over shared plates and wine felt absurd to her. Surgery was discipline. It was precision. It was life and death. Not a casual dinner where people laughed too loudly and passed breadbaskets across the table.

Agatha knew she wasn’t built for that kind of camaraderie. She didn’t trust it. She didn’t like the blurred lines or the way it demanded a vulnerability she wasn’t willing to give. And she certainly didn’t want anyone pretending they knew her beyond her scalpel and her decisions in the OR.

She shifted her clutch to her other hand, her nails digging slightly into the leather as her irritation mounted. Maybe it was her fault for accepting Dr. Calderu’s invitation in the first place, but it had felt like there was no real option. It was her first time attending one of these dinners at her new position, and skipping it would send the wrong message. She didn’t want to be branded as standoffish—at least not more than she already was.

And yet, the whole thing left a bitter taste in her mouth.

Her mind darted to Dr. Vidal, the one person she dreaded and anticipated seeing tonight. Agatha hadn’t missed the glances Vidal had thrown her way earlier, nor the quiet tension that always seemed to linger between them. It was aggravating and oddly magnetic at the same time. Agatha didn’t know what irritated her more—the possibility that Vidal might pry into her life tonight under the guise of casual conversation, or that Vidal might pretend to care about this ridiculous “family dinner” as much as Calderu did.

Agatha's fingers tightened on the strap of her clutch as her thoughts drifted back to this morning, to the moment she had woken up in the hospital room and found Dr. Vidal standing in the doorway.

She hated being caught off guard, and Vidal had a way of doing just that—unearthing pieces of Agatha she preferred to keep buried. The memory of her steady gaze as she quietly took in the scene made Agatha’s skin prickle with discomfort. She’d spent the night slumped in a chair, vulnerable, exhausted, and painfully human. She hated that she had seen her like that, stripped of the armor she wore so carefully.

She hadn’t said much, but the weight of her presence had been enough. There had been no judgment in her voice when she said, “You stayed.” If anything, there had been something else—understanding? Respect? Agatha wasn’t sure, and that uncertainty gnawed at her. She didn’t want Vidal’s approval, and she certainly didn't need it. Yet the idea of what Vidal might think of her in that moment lingered, sharper than she cared to admit.

And then there was the fight in the OR.

Agatha’s jaw clenched as she recalled the sharp words exchanged during the emergency surgery. Vidal had challenged her—publicly, decisively—and for a split second, Agatha had been furious. It wasn’t the challenge itself that had stung; she could handle pushback. It was the fact that Vidal had been right.

The baby’s case had been a precarious balancing act, and Agatha’s instincts had screamed at her to proceed cautiously. But Vidal had pushed, advocating for a bolder approach, one that teetered on the edge of recklessness but offered a better outcome if it worked. In the heat of the moment, Agatha had shut Vidal down, unwilling to take the risk.

Looking back, she could still feel the heat of Vidal’s glare through her surgical mask, and could hear the quiet steel in her voice as she argued her point. It had taken everything in Agatha not to snap, not to let the tension crack her focus.

And yet, as much as she hated to admit it, Vidal’s insistence had lingered in her mind long after the surgery ended. She couldn’t shake the nagging thought that maybe, just maybe, she had seen something Agatha hadn’t—or hadn’t wanted to see.

It infuriated her. Not because she thought Vidal was wrong, but because Vidal’s presence had a way of forcing Agatha to confront things she’d rather ignore: her exhaustion, her mistakes, her humanity.

Agatha exhaled sharply, pushing the thoughts aside. She couldn’t afford to dwell on Vidal, not tonight. The last thing she needed was to waste energy speculating about Vidal’s intentions. 

Agatha entered the living room, where Nicky was sitting on the couch, a bright smile spreading across his face when he saw her. He was playing with his toy cars, but his eyes immediately flicked up to meet hers. She paused for a moment, the tiredness still heavy on her shoulders, but the warmth of his gaze eased some of that weight.

“Mommy, you look so beautiful,” he said, his little voice filled with such genuine sweetness that it stopped Agatha in her tracks.

She blinked, taken aback by the simplicity of the compliment, her breath catching for a moment. She hadn’t expected it, not from a four-year-old with a toy car in his hand, his face still smeared with traces of lunch. Yet, in his innocent, untainted way, he had somehow cut through the tired facade she’d put on for the day.

A smile tugged at the corner of her lips, soft and sincere. She knelt down in front of him, brushing a few stray locks of hair out of his face.

“Thank you, baby,” she murmured, her voice thick with emotion. She knew he didn’t understand the depth of her exhaustion, or the layers of guilt and pressure that had been weighing on her. But in that moment, she didn’t need him to. The simple act of him noticing her—seeing her, even in her worn state—felt like a balm for all the parts of herself she had neglected.

Nicky beamed at her, his little hands reaching up to give her a quick hug. Agatha squeezed him tightly, the rush of love filling her chest like a quiet promise. This was the reminder she needed: this was why she did it all. For him.

She kissed the top of his head, pulling back to look at him with a tender smile, letting the moment linger, a rare, quiet treasure in the midst of the chaos of her life.
