Agatha stood up slowly, feeling the familiar pang of guilt wash over her, even as Nicky's innocent smile lingered in her mind. She was about to leave again—duty called, and she had no choice but to answer. But there was a part of her that wished she could stay.

She took a deep breath and looked down at him. "Okay, sweetheart," she said, her voice soft but steady. "Mommy's gotta go back to work now. I’ll be back soon, I promise."

Nicky looked up at her with wide, trusting eyes, his small hands clutching his toy car tightly. “Be careful, Mommy,” he said, a little wrinkle of concern creasing his forehead.

Agatha's heart ached. She bent down to kiss him on the forehead again, her lips lingering a moment longer than usual. "I will, Nicky. I’ll be careful. And I’ll be home soon."

As she straightened up, she gave him one last smile, forcing herself to make it bright, despite the heaviness of her heart. She needed him to feel secure, even if she didn’t quite feel it herself.

“I love you,” she whispered, a promise more than just words.

"I love you too, Mommy!" Nicky replied, his voice filled with the innocent certainty of a child who believed in the simplicity of love.

With one last lingering glance at her son, Agatha walked toward the door, each step heavier than the last. She grabbed her purse, adjusted her coat, and with a quick glance over her shoulder, she said, "I’ll see you in the morning, okay?"

Nicky waved enthusiastically from his spot on the couch. "Okay! Bye, Mommy!"

Agatha stepped out into the cold night, the door closing softly behind her. As the cool air hit her face, she drew a sharp breath. It was time to put on the mask again—time to leave her home life behind and return to the chaos of the hospital.

But somewhere in her heart, the warmth of Nicky’s love lingered, a small reminder of why she pushed herself so hard, even when everything else felt like too much.

Still, as she stepped into the foyer, she couldn’t suppress the tension building in her chest. It wasn’t just the dinner, she realized. It was what the dinner represented—a chipping away at the walls she had so carefully built. Walls she wasn’t ready to let down for anyone, least of all Dr Vidal.

Tonight, she’d go, make an appearance, and leave as soon as it was polite to do so. No bonding, no stories about herself. And if anyone tried to drag her into that nonsense, she’d have a polite excuse at the ready.

The Uber pulled up in front of the restaurant, its sleek exterior glinting under the soft golden glow of the ornate lanterns lining the entrance. Agatha stepped out carefully, the glossy patent leather of her Louis Vuitton heels hitting the cobblestone driveway with a sharp click. The air smelled faintly of jasmine and citrus, mingling with the scent of fresh rain on stone.

The restaurant was undoubtedly upscale, its grand double doors flanked by manicured hedges and a red velvet rope that seemed more ceremonial than functional. Inside, through the towering glass windows, Agatha could see dim lighting, chandeliers glistening like constellations, and waitstaff in crisp black-and-white uniforms moving with practiced elegance.

Agatha straightened her black dress, smoothing the fabric that hugged her curves just enough to highlight her form while remaining classically elegant. The weight of her diamond earrings grounded her momentarily as she adjusted the strap of her clutch and walked up the short flight of stairs, her confidence feigned but convincing.

As she stepped inside, the hum of conversation and the clinking of glassware hit her ears. A hostess greeted her with a warm smile and led her toward the private dining area. The room she was ushered into had an air of exclusivity—polished oak floors, a long table set with fine china, and plush chairs that suggested they’d been selected for hours of indulgent conversation.

Lilia Calderu was the first to greet her, rising from her seat near the head of the table. She was dressed in an emerald-green silk blouse that matched the confidence in her sharp smile. “Agatha! You made it,” Lilia said, her voice as smooth as the wine being poured into glasses around the table.

Agatha nodded politely, forcing a smile as her eyes scanned the room. Wanda was seated a few chairs down, her vivid red hair catching the candlelight. She raised a glass in a silent toast when their eyes met. But the rest of the faces were unfamiliar—a cluster of department heads and senior staff, each looking far too comfortable with one another, laughing and leaning into the warmth of shared camaraderie.

Agatha’s unease prickled under her skin. She had always hated gatherings like this. The casual intimacy felt forced, the laughter too loud, the sense of camaraderie so foreign it felt alien. Lilia had called it a “family-style” dinner, and now Agatha understood why. The entire scene was like an unsettling echo of a real family gathering—complete with inside jokes and stories that felt designed to highlight her as the outsider.

“Come, sit,” Lilia gestured to the chair beside her, pulling Agatha further into the group. Agatha obliged, sliding into the seat and crossing her legs in a way that she hoped read as poised rather than uncomfortable.

Someone—a broad-shouldered man whose name she didn’t catch—leaned over to ask her a question about a recent case, and the conversation turned to work. Agatha gave clipped, professional answers, offering nothing more than what was necessary. Her glass of wine sat untouched.

The group’s laughter rang out around her, warm and unrestrained. Agatha found herself tightening her grip on the arm of her chair. She hated this kind of vulnerability, this shared comfort that she had neither the time nor the inclination to cultivate. Her discomfort wasn’t from not knowing these people—it was from realizing she didn’t want to know them.

She thought of Nicky for a brief moment, his small voice saying she looked beautiful, and wished she was back home with him.

Wanda caught her eye from across the table, raising an eyebrow as if to say, Surviving yet? Agatha managed a faint smile, but it didn’t reach her eyes. As much as she disliked this dynamic, she had no choice but to endure it. After all, in her line of work, perception mattered nearly as much as skill.

Agatha’s fingers traced the stem of her wineglass absently, her gaze drifting around the room. The noise of the group—a chorus of laughter and overlapping conversations—faded into the background as a strange realization settled in.

No signs of Vidal.

For the first time that evening, she felt a flicker of ease, like an invisible tension unwinding from her spine. Maybe Vidal wasn’t coming. That would mean one less judgmental gaze dissecting her every word, one less person who knew just how much she hated being here.

She exhaled softly and allowed herself to lean back slightly in her chair. The tightness in her chest loosened, and for a moment, she even thought she might be able to enjoy the evening—or at least endure it without gritting her teeth.

But then, as the seconds stretched on, her thoughts turned sharp again. She should be here. The absence that had initially calmed her now stirred something closer to anger. After all, hadn’t Vidal practically demanded perfection from everyone? Hadn’t she carved herself into Agatha’s life with a relentless, cutting precision? And yet, here she was missing in action, leaving Agatha to suffer through this performative dinner alone.

Her jaw tightened, the irritation bubbling just beneath her composed exterior. She sipped her wine, more forcefully than necessary, and tried to bury the simmering annoyance.

“Agatha,” Lilia’s voice broke through her thoughts, drawing her attention back to the table. Lilia had that smile on again—the one that felt just a little too knowing. “I’ve been meaning to ask… how are you settling in? New city, new hospital. That must be a big adjustment.”

Agatha straightened, her expression smoothing into polite neutrality. “It’s been... busy,” she said, her voice clipped but polite. “Plenty to keep me occupied.”

“Do you have family here? Or are you on your own?” Lilis intervened again

Agatha’s shoulders stiffened slightly, though she kept her expression neutral. “Just me and my son,” she replied smoothly, taking a measured sip of her wine. She hoped the brief answer would satisfy the question, but Lilia wasn’t done.

“Oh, you have a son!” Wanda chimed in, leaning forward with a warm smile. “How old is he?”

“Four,” Agatha replied, her tone polite but clipped. She didn’t want to delve into this, especially not here, in a room full of near-strangers.

“That’s such a sweet age,” Wanda continued, her tone almost too earnest. “It must be challenging, though, balancing work and raising a child. I know that. I have two of my own. Billy and Tommy, my set of twins. They are six and right now are a handful, not going to lie. My husband and I managed barely to be able to keep”

Agatha forced a tight smile. “It’s a challenge” she asked, hoping for a change of topic that didn't involve her sharing her personal life.

Lilia tilted her head, her eyes narrowing slightly as if searching for more. “What about his dad? Is he in the picture?”

The question hit like a sharp jab, and Agatha’s fingers tightened around the stem of her wine glass. She kept her voice steady, though a touch of frost crept in. “No. Just me.”

Lilia hesitated, as if gauging whether to press further, but Wanda jumped in before she could. “You must be so busy,” she said, her tone sympathetic. “A single mom and a surgeon—how do you do it? Do you ever have time for yourself?”

Agatha let out a low, humorless chuckle, shaking her head. “Time for myself? Not much of that these days.” She paused, then added dryly, “I suppose that’s why I ended up here tonight. It’s about as close to a break as I get.”

The table laughed lightly, and Agatha seized the opportunity to redirect the focus. “What about all of you? This whole ‘family dinner’ tradition of yours—it’s quite the dynamic. How long have you been doing this?”

Lilia and Wanda exchanged a glance, and the conversation shifted back to their camaraderie, leaving Agatha momentarily off the hook. But even as the spotlight moved, she could still feel the weight of their questions, lingering like an unwelcome shadow.

Agatha hated talking about herself—especially when it came to her personal life. Sharing even the smallest details about Nicky, about the life she had carved out for them, felt like giving up control. And control was something she had clung to desperately, especially after everything that had unraveled in her past.

When she answered questions about her son or her status as a single mother, it was like cracking open a door she kept firmly bolted. She didn’t want their sympathy, their curiosity, or their judgments. They didn’t need to know how she balanced midnight surgeries with kindergarten pickups, or how she’d rebuilt her life after Daniel’s betrayal. Those were her struggles, her victories—hers to carry alone.

Here, at this table, surrounded by a dynamic she couldn’t relate to, Agatha felt the weight of their attention pressing down on her. Every question chipped away at her carefully maintained façade, and she hated it. The vulnerability of exposing even a sliver of her private world made her skin crawl. It was as if she was losing control of the narrative, allowing others to fill in the blanks with their assumptions.

And yet, refusing to answer outright wasn’t an option either. It would make her stand out even more, make her seem cold or aloof—traits that already followed her like shadows. So she played along, giving just enough to satisfy their curiosity but not enough to truly reveal anything of substance.

Still, every word felt like a betrayal of the boundary she had drawn between herself and the rest of the world. Agatha wasn’t just protecting herself by keeping her life private; she was protecting Nicky, their routine, their fragile sanctuary. This wasn’t the kind of vulnerability she was willing to indulge—not here, not with these people.

And yet, as the conversation moved on, she felt the tension simmering beneath her polished exterior. Sharing these details, however small, felt like a loss. A crack in the armor. And Agatha hated feeling anything less than entirely in control.

The sharp clatter of a fork against a glass broke through Agatha’s spiraling thoughts, yanking her back to the present. The hum of conversation at the table faltered, and Agatha instinctively glanced toward the entrance. Her heart gave a small, involuntary jolt.

It wasn’t something that interrupted her thoughts. It was someone.

Rio Vidal.

She strode into the room with effortless confidence, drawing every pair of eyes like a magnet. The restaurant’s golden lights seemed to catch every angle of her perfectly tailored green suit. The deep emerald fabric was rich, almost luminous, and it clung to her frame in a way that was both sharp and undeniably sensual. The jacket was cut just low enough to hint at the curves beneath, her collarbone a delicate line above the subtle dip of the neckline. Her trousers skimmed her long legs, the kind of fit that spoke of custom tailoring and impeccable taste.

Her dark hair was pulled back just enough to highlight her striking features—high cheekbones, a jawline that could cut glass, and that damned smile. It wasn’t even a full smile, just a faint curve at the corners of her mouth, but it was enough to unnerve Agatha.

The air seemed to shift as Rio entered, as though she brought her own gravity. Even Lilia, who had been holding court at the head of the table with her usual boisterous charm, faltered for a moment, raising a hand in a half-wave.

Agatha couldn’t tear her eyes away. She hated herself for it, but there was no denying Rio’s presence. She was arresting. Like a storm rolling in— unpredictable, and impossible to ignore.

For a fleeting moment, Agatha thought she saw Rio’s gaze sweep the room and land on her. That faint curve of her mouth deepened just slightly.

Agatha’s grip tightened on her wine glass. Any sense of ease she’d felt moments ago was gone, replaced by a roiling mixture of emotions she couldn’t quite name.

Damn her.

Chapter 8

Chapter Notes

I wasn’t planning on posting this today—I wanted to save it for next week—but I just couldn’t help myself. I guess Christmas came early!

Also, I had Devil I Know by Allie X on loop while writing this—it feels like the perfect reading soundtrack for it. Do with that information what you will (wink wink).

Anyways, enjoy this double-day update!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Rio stepped into the room with the ease of someone who knew exactly how to make an impression, while the faintest smirk tugged at her lips as she surveyed the table. She exuded an effortless magnetism, the kind that could command attention without a single word. Agatha hated that it worked so well.

“Apologies for being late,” Rio said, her voice smooth and unhurried as if she weren’t the last one to arrive. “Something came up last minute—” her voice was calm but carrying just enough weight to silence the murmurs. She smiled, her teeth white against the dark red of her lips. It wasn’t an excuse; it was a brush-off. 

Dr. Calderu looked up from her seat at the head of the table, her brow furrowing slightly. “Everything alright, Rio?”

“Oh, nothing dramatic,” Rio replied with a casual wave of her hand, sidestepping any real explanation. “Handled and forgotten.” she said dismissively, clearly uninterested in elaborating. “All good now. Let’s not waste time on me.” She offered a warm smile that somehow felt more like a shield than an invitation. The tension that her tardiness might have created dissolved quickly, her charm disarming everyone as effortlessly as she entered. 

” Now, where should I sit?” Agatha’s heart sank as she scanned the table. Every seat was filled. She was just beginning to hope Rio might be shuffled to another table entirely by herself when Calderu spoke.

“Well, we seem to have run out of chairs,” Calderu said, almost amused. “Someone, grab another for Dr. Vidal. She can join us here.” She gestured to the space next to Agatha.

The waitstaff moved quickly, bringing in a spare chair and sliding it into the narrow gap beside her. Agatha tensed, her posture stiff as Rio strolled over, her eyes gleaming with something far too close to mischief.

Agatha didn’t move, her face carefully blank as Rio slid into the chair beside her, close enough that their shoulders almost brushed.

“Looks like I’ll be your neighbor for the evening,” Rio said as she lowered herself into the chair, the scent of her cologne—a mix of cedar and something citrusy—brushing over Agatha’s senses. She sat closer than necessary, the arm of her chair just grazing Agatha’s.

Agatha forced a tight smile, her fingers gripping the stem of her wineglass a little too tightly. “Lucky me,” she muttered under her breath, low enough that only Rio could hear.

“Oh, don’t sound so thrilled,” Rio whispered back, her tone teasing. “We’re supposed to be fostering camaraderie, aren’t we?”

Agatha turned her gaze toward the table, gripping her wineglass like it was the only thing keeping her grounded. “I didn’t realize camaraderie involved invading personal space.”

Rio leaned in slightly, her voice dipping conspiratorially. “You didn’t seem like the type to mind a little invasion.”

Agatha’s pulse quickened, a reaction she couldn’t fully control. She took a slow sip of her wine, willing herself to remain calm. “You’re late,” she said flatly, her tone carrying a subtle edge. “Didn’t think punctuality was a skill surgeons were allowed to neglect. Or did you miss that memo?”

“Fashionably late,” Rio corrected with a smirk. “It’s an art. Besides, it’s not like I missed anything earth-shattering.”

“Just your chance to make a good impression,” Agatha shot back, her voice sharper now.

Rio tilted her head, her dark eyes scanning Agatha’s face. “I think I’ve already made one. Whether it’s good or bad, well... I’ll let you decide.” She chuckled softly, leaning just slightly closer, her voice dropping to a conspiratorial murmur. “I make up for it in other ways.”

Heat prickled at the back of Agatha’s neck, and she couldn’t tell if it was anger or something more insidious that made her skin flush. She hated the way Rio spoke—as though every word was a double-edged sword, cutting just enough to leave a mark. She hated the ease with which Rio had inserted herself into the room, the way everyone seemed instantly charmed.

“You look tense,” Rio continued, her gaze flicking to Agatha’s rigid posture. “Big night for you I guess?”

“Stop talking,” Agatha hissed, her tone sharp enough to cut. But instead of retreating, Rio smirked and leaned back in her chair, slowly lifting an eyebrow, thoroughly entertained by Agatha’s barely concealed annoyance.

The conversation around the table carried on, oblivious to the undercurrent between them. Agatha kept her eyes forward, trying to focus on Calderu’s anecdotes about department milestones and upcoming surgeries. But every time she shifted in her chair, she was acutely aware of Rio beside her—the warmth of her presence, the occasional brush of their sleeves. It was maddening.

“Nice dress, by the way,” Rio said suddenly, her voice soft but deliberate. “Black suits you. Very... commanding. Executionary style, like a modern-day Darth Vader.”

What the hell?

Agatha’s eyes snapped to Rio, but her expression gave nothing away except the faintest trace of amusement. “Shouldn’t you be catching up on the department gossip instead of commenting on my wardrobe?” she retorted.

“I’m multitasking,” Rio replied with a shrug, her smirk deepening.

Agatha glanced at her, her expression hard and unreadable. She didn’t respond, though her pulse betrayed her annoyance—or was it something else? She couldn’t quite decide, and the ambiguity of it all only made her more frustrated. She hated how easily Rio got under her skin, how the teasing somehow managed to feel personal and impersonal all at once. It was like a game Rio had mastered, and Agatha despised being the unwitting participant.

She tightened her grip on her glass, silently vowing to endure the rest of the evening with as much dignity as she could muster. But when Rio leaned in again, her voice low enough to feel intimate, Agatha knew it was going to be a long night.

“I bet you rehearsed that in the mirror before you left,” Rio teased, her voice dropping just enough to ensure only Agatha could hear.

Agatha’s grip tightened on her glass, her knuckles whitening as she forced herself to take a slow sip. “Do you always have to talk this much?” she muttered through clenched teeth.

“Only when I’m entertained,” Rio shot back, her grin widening. “And right now? You’re the highlight of my night.”

Agatha exhaled sharply through her nose, her jaw tense. She tightened her grip on her glass, silently vowing to endure the rest of the evening with as much dignity as she could muster. She directed her focus toward Calderu, who was recounting a particularly dramatic case, but the words barely registered. Every time Agatha adjusted her position, she was acutely aware of Rio beside her—the warmth of her presence, the occasional brush of fabric against her arm. It was maddening.

But when Rio leaned in again, her voice low enough to feel intimate, Agatha knew it was going to be a long night.

“You’re no fun when you’re wound this tight,” Rio murmured, her breath brushing lightly against Agatha’s ear. “Relax. The world isn’t watching.”

Agatha shot her a glare, the kind that would have made anyone else retreat. But Rio’s lips twitched into another infuriating smile, and she didn’t push further. Instead, she simply raised her glass in an unspoken truce, leaving Agatha to stew in her complicated tangle of irritation, intrigue, and the smallest flicker of something she refused to name.

The moments stretched on, and despite her best efforts, Agatha’s mind remained fixed on Rio. The teasing, the casual proximity, the way Rio seemed to draw everyone’s attention without even trying—it was maddening. She hated how easily Rio slipped under her skin, how every word felt like a challenge, every glance like an unanswered question.

As the evening wore on, Calderu called for a toast, pulling Agatha’s attention back to the group. She raised her glass mechanically, the sound of clinking crystal filling the air. But even then, she could feel Rio’s presence beside her, as constant and vexing as ever.

And when Rio leaned in one last time, her voice soft but insistent, it sent a ripple of frustration—and something else—through Agatha. “Relax,” Rio said, her tone unusually sincere. “You’re among colleagues, not enemies. No one’s going to bite.”

Agatha glanced at her, her expression as cold as she could manage. “Pity. I could’ve used the distraction.”

Rio’s laugh was quiet, but it carried just enough weight to send Agatha’s pulse into overdrive.

Wanda, lounging back in her chair with the ease of someone who had thoroughly enjoyed herself, turned to the group.

“So, what’s everyone doing after this?” she asked, her voice casual but her eyes sharp, scanning the faces at the table.

“Heading home,” Dr. Calderu said firmly, dabbing at her lips with her napkin. “It’s late, and I have an early morning tomorrow.” Several others murmured similar sentiments, the rigors of surgical life evident in their tired faces.

“Not me,” Wanda declared, a sly grin creeping onto her lips. “Mariah and I are hitting a bar downtown. Someplace with good music and even better whiskey.” She leaned forward slightly, her gaze landing on Agatha. “You should come.”

Agatha stiffened, caught off guard by the sudden attention. “I don’t think so,” she started, her voice hesitant.

“Oh, come on,” Wanda pressed, her grin widening. “You’re new here. You need to loosen up, get a feel for the city’s nightlife. Besides, I won’t take no for an answer.”

Mariah Hill, sitting a few seats away, nodded in agreement. “It’ll be fun. And it’s not every night we get a break like this.”

Agatha hesitated, her grip tightening around her glass. The idea of spending more time with anyone tonight felt exhausting, but at the same time, there was something freeing about stepping into the unfamiliar. She had nothing to lose, really. Still, she wasn’t ready to commit.

“I don’t know,” Agatha said cautiously. “I—”

“You should go,” Rio interjected, cutting her off with a teasing lilt in her voice. “You might actually enjoy yourself for once. It’s been a while since you’ve done something that’s not, oh, I don’t know—terribly important.”

Agatha shot her a glare, but Rio’s smirk only deepened. She leaned in slightly, her voice low and playful, though loud enough for the others to hear. “Unless you’re worried you can’t keep up?”

“Agatha? Can’t keep up?” Wanda cut in, her tone dripping with mock disbelief. “Impossible. She’s clearly got ‘party animal’ written all over her.”

Laughter rippled through the table, and Agatha felt heat rise to her cheeks, though she managed to maintain her usual composed expression.

“I don’t need advice from you,” Agatha snapped under her breath, her voice a low warning.

“Oh, I’m not advising,” Rio replied, leaning back with an infuriating air of nonchalance. “I’m just saying—life’s short. Might as well make a night of it. Besides, I’m curious how you’d handle something that’s, you know, actually fun.”

Wanda chuckled, clearly enjoying the exchange. “You’re right, Rio. Agatha, don’t tell me you’re all work and no play?”

Agatha’s jaw tightened, her mind bristling with irritation. Rio’s ability to needle her was almost an art form.

“Are you coming with us then, Rio?” Wanda asked, her tone light but her curiosity evident.

“Not tonight,” Rio said with a dismissive wave of her hand. “But you should absolutely drag Agatha along.” Her grin turned wicked as she added, “She could use a little fun.”

Mariah smirked, raising an eyebrow at Agatha. “No offense, but you don’t exactly strike me as a whiskey-and-music type.”

“Oh, I can surprise you,” Agatha replied coolly, the sharpness in her tone earning a chuckle from Wanda.

“She’s full of surprises,” Rio added, her teasing relentless. “Though I bet she orders something boring, like a vodka soda, and nurses it all night.”

Agatha resisted the urge to roll her eyes, her frustration bubbling just beneath the surface. But as she sat there, surrounded by the chatter and the warmth of the room, a part of her softened. Maybe Wanda was right. Maybe she did need to loosen up, to embrace the unknown instead of retreating into herself.

“Fine,” Agatha said finally, her voice firmer than she felt. She set her glass down with a decisive clink. “I’ll go.”

Wanda beamed, and Mariah raised her glass in approval. “That’s the spirit,” Wanda said, leaning back with satisfaction.

Rio let out a low whistle, her grin smug. “Guess I underestimated you. Maybe tonight’s the night you finally surprise me, Harkness.”

Agatha met her gaze with a cold stare, refusing to let her irritation show further. She avoided looking at Rio after that, but she could feel her eyes lingering—a weight that seemed to both irritate and intrigue her in equal measure.

As the group began to disperse, Agatha stood, smoothing her dress and steeling herself for whatever came next. She didn’t know if this was a mistake, but for tonight, she was willing to take the risk.

While they gathered their belongings and began to drift toward the exit, Rio lingered near Agatha, her green suit sharp under the soft lighting of the restaurant. Agatha tried to keep her focus on her clutch, on her phone, on anything but the woman who had spent the evening alternately charming and infuriating her.

“Enjoy your night,” Rio said casually, her voice like silk, as she adjusted her cufflink. Her smirk was just a touch too knowing, her tone just a shade too amused. “Try not to overthink it, Harkness.”

Agatha’s jaw clenched. “Goodbye, Dr. Vidal,” she said curtly, her tone clipped. She didn’t look back, but she could feel Rio’s eyes on her as she followed Wanda and Mariah out to the street. What was she doing?

Still, a part of her couldn’t shake the lingering question: Why wasn’t Rio coming with them? The question surfaced unbidden, accompanied by an unexpected pang of disappointment. Rio’s playful teasing had been a thorn in her side all evening, but somehow, it had anchored her too. Her absence now felt oddly unmooring.

She hated herself for caring. What did it matter why Rio wasn’t going? Agatha had made it perfectly clear she didn’t need—or want—Rio’s input on her choices. But the thought nagged at her, persistent as an itch she couldn’t scratch. Maybe it was the ambiguity of Rio’s reasons, the casual way she’d brushed off the invitation. Or maybe it was something deeper, something about Rio’s infuriating ability to rattle her composure and then retreat as if she were above it all.

Agatha huffed softly under her breath, trying to shake off the irritation. This was supposed to be her night, her choice. Yet, the thought of Rio’s absence lingered like a shadow, affecting her in ways she refused to examine too closely.

As the cool night air hit her skin, Agatha refocused on Wanda and Mariah, who were chatting easily beside her. She climbed into the backseat of the Uber, settling into the leather upholstery with a mix of apprehension and resolve.

She didn’t have to understand why Rio wasn’t there or why it bothered her. She just had to move forward. For tonight, at least, she’d let herself step outside the confines of her carefully constructed world. Whatever came next, she’d face it head-on—without Rio Vidal’s infuriating smirk lingering in the back of her mind.

The Uber pulled up to the curb, and Wanda immediately slid into the middle seat, waving Agatha to follow. “Come on, Agatha” Wanda teased, patting the seat next to her. “This is going to be fun. Or at least funny.”

Agatha sighed and climbed in, settling into the seat as Mariah took the other side. The car was filled with a faint smell of leather and citrus air freshener. Wanda, ever the extrovert, immediately began chatting with the driver.

Agatha leaned back, the cool leather pressing against her bare shoulders. She could feel the tension from the evening beginning to dissipate, but her mind still churned. What possessed her to agree to this? 

Wanda seemed to sense her unease. “Lighten up, Agatha,” she said, her tone conspiratorial. “We’re not going to make you sing karaoke or anything.” She paused, then grinned. “Unless you want to. In which case, Mariah’s buying the first round.”

Mariah groaned but smiled. “Don’t listen to her. She’s been trying to get me to do karaoke for years.”

Agatha couldn’t help the faint smile that tugged at her lips. Wanda’s energy was infectious, her jokes a welcome distraction from her own spiraling thoughts.

The Uber hit a bump, and Wanda exaggeratedly clutched at Agatha’s arm. “See? Already an adventure,” she said with mock drama. “I can tell this night is going to be legendary.”

“You have an interesting definition of legendary,” Agatha said dryly, but her voice had softened, her tone less sharp. For the first time that evening, she felt the faintest flicker of anticipation. Maybe this wouldn’t be such a disaster after all.

As the car turned down the glowing streets of downtown, Agatha glanced out the window, the city lights blurring past. She wasn’t sure what she was chasing tonight—escape, connection, or just a moment to forget. But for now, she let herself be carried along.

The Uber came to a smooth stop outside a sleek, dimly lit bar with large glass windows. The place exuded a perfect balance of elegance and casual chic, its polished wood and soft golden lighting inviting but not overwhelming. The crowd inside was a mix of well-dressed patrons, but the atmosphere was warm, not pretentious. A jazz band played softly in the corner, blending into the hum of conversation.

“Here we are,” Wanda said, stepping out of the car first. She was already grinning, clearly in her element. “Time to relax.”

Agatha followed, trying to shake off the last remnants of tension from the dinner. She had been hyper-aware of every conversation, every glance, but now she was starting to feel like she could breathe again. The cool night air helped clear her head as they walked through the door.

Inside, the bar was lively, but not overcrowded. The host greeted them with a smile, and after a quick word, they were escorted to a private booth in the back. It was an intimate space, with plush seating that curved around a small, elegant table, a candle flickering gently in the middle. A large window behind them provided a view of the city streets, and the ambient lighting was low but comforting.

Wanda slid into the booth first, making herself at home. Mariah followed, settling in beside her. Agatha hesitated for a moment before sitting, finally sinking into the softness of the seat. She could feel her shoulders drop just a little bit, her body beginning to relax now that the pressure of the evening had shifted.

A waiter arrived almost immediately with a small tray of drinks and a menu, his presence smooth and efficient.

“We’re going to start with something simple to welcome Agatha,” Wanda declared with a grin, “A round of cocktails for the newbie.” She glanced at the waiter. “We’ll have three Negronis, please. Make it a double.”

The waiter nodded, taking the order and leaving them to chat.

Agatha folded her arms loosely on the table, feeling a little out of place but also surprisingly comfortable. It was strange—she’d spent so many years in hospitals and offices, in environments that demanded precision and control. But here, in this relaxed, almost luxurious setting, she found herself trying to navigate the lines between professionalism and something far more... casual.

“So, Agatha,” Wanda started, her eyes sparkling with curiosity, “tell us about yourself. Do you have a partner? A dog, maybe? What's your kid's name?”

Mariah leaned forward, her expression warm but equally inquisitive. “Yeah, we’re all dying to know more. You’ve been pretty quiet all night, and we need some answers.”

Agatha’s eyes flicked between the two women. She hadn’t expected this—didn’t know what to expect, really. But the questions felt invasive, like they were peeling back layers she wasn’t ready to expose. Her stomach clenched slightly.

“His name is Nicholas, no husband,” Agatha said smoothly, keeping her tone even. “And as for pets...” She paused, eyes flicking to the side before she added, “Not at the moment.”

The silence that followed felt too thick. It wasn’t a bad silence, just... pregnant with unasked questions. Agatha shifted uncomfortably, feeling the weight of the scrutiny on her.

Wanda raised an eyebrow. “Really? No pets? You seem like a cat person.”

Agatha offered a tight smile, deflecting. “I used to have a dog. He didn’t quite work out.” Her voice was casual, though the memory tugged at her in ways she couldn’t quite control. Her eyes darted to the window, watching the city bustle outside.

Mariah laughed softly, leaning back. “So, what do you do when you’re not being a surgeon?” she asked, her voice more relaxed now.

Agatha considered the question, eyes narrowing slightly. How much of herself did she really want to give them? This dinner, this conversation, was supposed to be a break—an escape from the weight of work, of responsibility. But it was hard to leave behind the parts of her that felt too exposed, too vulnerable.

Just as Agatha was about to elude the question, the waiter returned with their drinks, setting them down on the table with a practiced flourish.

“Here you go—Negronis for everyone,” he said with a polite smile, before retreating.

Wanda picked up her glass, toasting it gently in the air. “To Agatha,” she said, a twinkle in her eye. “Welcome to the team. May the rest of your evening be as eventful as our first.”

Agatha clinked her glass against theirs, the cold surface of the glass steadying her hands as she took a sip. The bitter-sweetness of the Negroni filled her senses, and for a moment, the weight of the conversation, of the questions, seemed to lift. The night was just beginning.

Agatha took a moment, swirling her Negroni thoughtfully, then decided to turn the tables. She needed something to focus on other than her own discomfort, and who better to interrogate than the two women who had been so relentlessly curious about her?

“So, Maria,” Agatha started, her voice smooth, “you’re the pediatric surgery chief, right? That’s impressive.” She leaned back slightly, trying to keep her tone casual. “How do you manage such a high-stress job? Must be hard to keep everything balanced.”

Maria chuckled, taking a sip of her drink. "Oh, it's not easy, that's for sure. You can’t really ‘balance’ anything. It’s a constant juggling act. But it’s rewarding, especially when you get to save kids.” She smiled softly, a proud glint in her eyes. “And yes, I’m single so that helps a lot. Got a cat though—his name is Alfred.”

“A cat?” Agatha asked, raising an eyebrow. “What’s that like? A little companionship?”

Maria smirked. “He’s great. A bit of a diva, but he’s the only living thing in my apartment that doesn’t ask for anything from me. No constant demands, no needy behaviors—just the occasional cuddle when he wants to nap.”

Wanda, who had been quiet for a moment, suddenly piped up with a teasing grin. “I think that’s the perfect relationship. No emotional baggage to carry around.”

Agatha chuckled, the banter easing the tension in her chest a little. She then turned her attention to Wanda, who was sipping her drink casually.

“What about you, Wanda?” Agatha asked.

Wanda grinned broadly, the question clearly one she enjoyed answering. “I've got my twins and I live with my partner. His name is Vision. He’s my fiancé.” She paused, her eyes glinting with humor. “He’s actually a stay-at-home dad right now. It’s an arrangement that works for us. Someone’s got to raise the little one while I’m out here saving lives.”

Agatha raised an eyebrow, feigning surprise. “Wait, he’s a stay-at-home dad? That’s... interesting.”

“Oh, don’t get me started,” Maria chimed in, her tone playful. “We like to mock Wanda for this dynamic. But hey, it works for them, so whatever.” She gave Wanda a pointed look. “Right, Wanda?”

Wanda shrugged, clearly unbothered. “Yeah, it works. Vision is great with the kids. He’s got his hands full, but I’m killing it in the OR, so it’s all good.”

Agatha’s lips twitched into a smile. “Well, that’s a new take on things. When it's the big  day happening?

“Actually I really don't know. We got engaged in my second year as an attending and a month after we found out that we were pregnant with the twins. We postponed it because of work and the kids so I'm not sure but it's definitely happening. It works for us right now. We've been together for 15 years so waiting a little more doesn't bother us” she paused

“And you?” Wanda asked with a sly grin, taking another sip. “Why are you single? I mean, you’re successful, you have your life together. Most people would have settled down by now.”

Agatha blinked, not expecting the question to come so directly. The tone in Wanda’s voice was casual, but it hit a nerve. 

"I—" Agatha started, pausing to think. "I’ve just never felt like I had the time for that sort of thing. Between work and... everything else, it wasn’t really a priority for me."

Wanda leaned in a bit, narrowing her eyes playfully. “You’re saying that like you've never had anyone in your life. You’re telling me a woman like you—smart, driven, with the whole world at her feet—has never had someone to settle down with?”

Agatha’s eyes flickered to the side for a moment. She didn’t want to get into specifics, not here, not now. But the thought of the past—of Daniel—slipped into her mind, uninvited. She felt the weight of that old pain start to creep in, but she swallowed it down quickly.

She took a deep breath, her fingers tightening around her glass as Wanda’s question hung in the air. She hadn’t been asked about her personal life in a while, and the thought of opening up, even just a little, felt strange. But she realized they weren’t going to let it go, and she didn’t really care to keep pretending anymore.

“I was married,” she said, her voice even, but there was a slight hesitation before the words left her mouth. “But not anymore.”

She didn’t elaborate, didn’t explain the details of the messy divorce, or the heartbreak, or Daniel. That part of her life was long behind her. But the weight of those words hung between them, a quiet, unspoken history.

Maria gave her a nod, sensing the unspoken depth behind the response, but Wanda’s expression softened with curiosity. “I’m sorry to hear that,” she said gently, though her eyes were still inquisitive. “Do you think you’ll ever... try again?”

Agatha shrugged, her face carefully neutral. “I don’t know. It’s not really something I’ve thought about much. Right now, I’m focused on my kid and on my work, on what I do best.”

Maria nodded, seeming to understand that there was more to the story, but Agatha’s calmness in the face of the question was enough to convince her not to press further. Wanda, on the other hand, wasn’t as easily deterred.

“I get it,” Wanda said, giving her a playful grin, clearly trying to lighten the mood. “No pressure. But hey, I can’t help but think you’d make someone very happy. You seem like the kind of woman who knows what she wants.”

Agatha smirked, the words hitting their mark just right, but she didn’t let herself get drawn in. Instead, she took a sip of her drink, letting the conversation move in another direction.

Maria, sensing the shift, leaned back and smiled. “It’s interesting, though, how so many people think that success in one area of life should translate into success in every other area. Like... if you’re good at your job, you should automatically be good at everything else.”

Agatha gave a small, tight smile. “It’s a strange thing, isn’t it? The expectation that we all have to fit into this perfect mold.”

Wanda’s gaze softened slightly, a knowing look in her eyes. “I get it. It’s not always as easy as people think it is.” She took a deep breath, then looked at Agatha seriously. “But hey, don’t let anyone rush you. You do you.”

Agatha looked at both women, feeling the warmth of their understanding. There was a certain comfort in knowing that they didn’t expect her to fit into their mold. She could relax a little, let her guard down just a fraction.

Maria looked up from her drink, glancing at Wanda with a raised brow. "Speaking of, why wasn't Rio here tonight? She’s usually around, especially for something like this."

Wanda, ever the conversationalist, leaned back in her chair, her lips curling into a smile. Agatha shifted slightly in her seat, intrigued despite herself. She hadn’t realized she was still so... aware of Rio’s absence.

"I think Rio had some prior engagement," Wanda said casually, a playful glint in her eyes as she toyed with her drink. "Something about needing 'alone time,' which, you know what that means. Rio being Rio," Wanda replied with a wink, her voice carrying a hint of finality.

Mariah nodded, accepting the explanation without further question. Agatha, however, couldn't shake the feeling that there was more to the story. She felt a slight flush creep up her neck. She wasn’t sure why, but the thought of Rio deliberately avoiding this gathering struck a nerve. She hated how much it bothered her, but there it was.

Agatha wanted to use this as an opportunity to know more about Rio, that fucking infuriating person . She had spent the better part of the evening being teased and goaded by her, only for Rio to vanish and leave her in this unfamiliar crowd. It was maddening.

What was it about Rio that made her so impossible to ignore? Agatha had been trying to dismiss her since the moment they met, but it was as though Rio left behind an invisible tether, one that yanked at her when she least expected it. And now, sitting here, listening to Wanda’s lighthearted dismissal, Agatha’s curiosity burned brighter than her irritation.

"She really keeps people guessing, doesn’t she?" Agatha said, her voice deliberately neutral, though she could feel the weight of her own interest betraying her.

Wanda turned her gaze to Agatha, her expression mischievous. “Oh, absolutely. Rio’s like that stray cat you leave milk out for. She’ll come and go as she pleases, and just when you think she’s gone for good, there she is—stealing your seat and charming your guests.”

The analogy hit uncomfortably close to home, and Agatha forced a tight smile. She could picture it all too vividly: Rio strolling into any room as though she owned it, leaving chaos in her wake and never staying long enough to answer for it.

“Well, some of us prefer a little more consistency,” Agatha said dryly, lifting her glass to take a measured sip.

Mariah chuckled softly, but Wanda’s grin only deepened. “Consistency’s boring,” Wanda quipped. “And Rio’s never boring.”

Agatha didn’t respond, choosing instead to let the conversation move on. But as Mariah and Wanda began discussing their plans for the week, Agatha’s thoughts lingered.

She hated how Rio had taken up so much space in her mind without even being there. She hated how much she wanted to unravel the mystery of her absence, as though knowing more about Rio would somehow settle the uneasy pull she felt.

But most of all, she hated the nagging realization that she didn’t just want to understand Rio. She wanted Rio to understand her—and that was the most infuriating thought of all.

As the evening wore on, Agatha found herself glancing toward the door occasionally, half-expecting Rio to walk in. But she never did. She left a subtle void in the group, a space that Agatha couldn't help but feel drawn to.

Agatha’s fingers tightened around her glass. It wasn’t just about Rio’s absence—it was the unspoken things left unsaid. Why was Rio so unpredictable? Why did she pull away when Agatha least expected it? And why, despite her frustration, was Agatha so fixated on it?

The evening wound down. As much as Agatha tried to deflect, there were still lingering questions she had skillfully avoided, and she’d managed to keep her personal life just vague enough to leave them unsatisfied.

"Good night, Agatha," Mariah said, squeezing her shoulder gently before moving toward the door. "It was great to finally meet you. You should join us more often. We try to go out at least once every two weeks. It's mostly us and Rio. Other people tag along sometimes. You definitely should"

Wanda was already halfway out the door, waving back with a mischievous smile. "We’ll hold you to that, Agatha!

Once the last of them left, Agatha lingered by the bar for a few more minutes, her thoughts unsettled. She waited for her Uber to arrive, the minutes stretching longer than they should have. Finally, the familiar ping of her ride app let her know it was outside.

As she stepped out into the cool night air, the question that had been sitting heavily in her chest all evening resurfaced with renewed intensity: Why did Rio being Rio mean that? What exactly was it about Rio’s unpredictable nature that kept her at the forefront of Agatha's mind? Agatha couldn’t put it into words, but it felt like there was a deeper layer to Rio’s behavior that Agatha couldn’t quite unravel. It was frustrating, maddening, and yet… still somehow magnetic.

The Uber ride back to her apartment was quiet, the rhythmic hum of the tires on asphalt offering little solace. Agatha rested her head against the window, watching the city lights flicker by. She had tried not to let the evening get under her skin, to simply be a participant, not an observer of the dynamics between everyone else, but there it was, the unshakeable feeling that she was a piece in a puzzle she wasn’t sure she wanted to be a part of.

When she finally arrived home, the apartment felt strangely empty. Nicky’s toys were scattered across the living room, a familiar chaos that reminded her of the bright little whirlwind who’d return in the morning. She was grateful for the quiet, though. Tonight had been more draining than she’d expected.

Kicking off her shoes, she made her way to the bathroom, craving the soothing embrace of a hot shower. Steam soon filled the small space, and she stepped under the scalding water, hoping it would wash away the remnants of the night’s lingering tension.

It hadn’t been a terrible idea to go out with Wanda and Mariah, she admitted to herself. She’d actually enjoyed the easy camaraderie and the lively atmosphere of the bar. It had been refreshing, a small break from the isolating cocoon she often wrapped around herself. And, surprisingly, she’d managed to hold her own without giving away too much about her personal life.

Still, it would have been better if she hadn’t revealed anything at all. Agatha grimaced, the thought needling at her as she rinsed off. She was usually so careful, so deliberate in keeping her walls up. Tonight, though, those walls had felt just a little thinner.

Stepping out of the shower, she dried off quickly, her thoughts still swirling. She glanced at her phone, the screen lighting up to reveal a familiar blank slate. No new messages. Awesome.

With a sigh, she flicked off the bathroom light and padded to her bedroom. The sheets were cool against her skin as she slid into bed, her mind racing even as exhaustion began to catch up with her.

She had enjoyed herself—more than she expected. But that nagging unease refused to let go. The evening had felt like a tightrope walk, balancing between connection and guardedness, between feeling seen and staying hidden.

Her last thought before sleep took her was of Rio. Not her teasing smile or her green suit, but the maddening ambiguity of her absence—and the unshakable pull she still had on Agatha’s mind.

She turned off the lights and climbed into bed, her mind racing as the exhaustion from the day finally started to catch up with her.

Chapter End Notes

This chapter was so much fun to write, and I’m really happy with how it turned out. I hope you enjoyed it as much as I did! Also, shoutout to the person who commented on Rio being fashionable late—I just had to use that phrase. It was too Rio not to!

The next few chapters are already written—I just need to edit them, and I’ll post them as they’re ready. Have a wonderful rest of your Sunday!

Chapter 9

Agatha groaned as a persistent tug on her arm pulled her from the depths of sleep. The room was too bright, the air too cold, and her head throbbed like a drum being struck in rhythm. She squinted against the sunlight streaming through the window, the shape of her son gradually coming into focus. 

“Mom! Wake up! Pancakes!!” Nicky’s voice was loud and insistent, his small hands shaking her shoulder with the determination only a child could muster.

Agatha groaned again, this time in protest, her brain struggling to catch up. “Nicky, it’s... what time is it?” Her voice was hoarse, her throat dry.

“It’s pancake time!” he declared triumphantly, as if that was an actual time on the clock.

“Pancake time,” she repeated, her voice flat. She pushed herself up on one elbow, immediately regretting it as the motion sent a wave of nausea through her. “Nicky, honey, can we—just this once—skip pancakes?”

His eyes widened, horrified. “No!” he exclaimed, crossing his arms in defiance. “It’s Pancake Saturday. Dad always used to make pancakes for us, remember?.”

Agatha winced, partly from the pounding in her head and partly from the unintended guilt in his words. Pancake Saturday had always been their tradition—Daniel’s tradition, really. Agatha had never been the pancake expert, not even close.

“All right, all right,” she muttered, rubbing her temples. “Pancakes. But I make no promises about the quality. You might end up eating a pancake-shaped brick.”

Nicky laughed, climbing off the bed with an excited bounce. “You can’t mess up pancakes, Mom. You just put them in the pan and flip them!”

“Spoken like someone who’s never cooked a day in his life,” she grumbled, swinging her legs over the side of the bed. The floor felt colder than she expected, and her joints protested as she stood. A glance in the mirror revealed a disheveled, tired woman with dark circles under her eyes and hair resembling an unkempt bird’s nest.

“Give me five minutes,” she said, waving Nicky toward the door. “Go get your pancake-eating face ready.”

He ran off, his laughter echoing down the hallway, leaving Agatha alone with her thoughts and her pounding head. She sighed deeply, bracing herself on the dresser.

“Never again,” she muttered, her voice hoarse but firm. “Never drinking again... or at least not before fucking pancake day .”

She pulled on a robe and shuffled toward the kitchen, where the daunting task of recreating a tradition she hadn’t mastered loomed. The smell of syrup and the sound of Nicky singing a pancake song he’d made up filled the air as she reached for the mixing bowl. It wasn’t going to be pretty, but she’d make it work—somehow.

Agatha stared at the pancake batter, her skepticism growing with every slow stir of the whisk. It was too lumpy. Or maybe it wasn’t lumpy enough? She couldn’t tell. Cooking wasn’t her thing. Cutting people open? Sure. Flipping pancakes? Not so much.

Nicky, perched on a stool beside her, watched with wide eyes and an air of barely contained impatience. “Mom, are you sure you know how to make pancakes?”

“Define ‘know,’” Agatha muttered, eyeing the batter suspiciously. She flicked on the stove and poured a dollop into the pan, the batter spreading unevenly like a badly planned crime scene.

Nicky leaned closer, squinting at the sizzling mess. “It’s not supposed to look like that.”

“Are you a pancake expert now?” Agatha shot back, grabbing a spatula and poking at the blob. “Because if you are, feel free to take over.”

He giggled, but his giggles turned to gasps when she attempted to flip the pancake. Instead of a graceful arc, it flopped halfway, folding over itself like a deflated balloon.

“Mom! You made a taco!” Nicky exclaimed, laughing so hard he nearly fell off the stool.

Agatha stared at the sad excuse for a pancake and sighed. “It’s a... pancake innovation. A taco-cake. Trendy chefs would kill for this idea.”

“Sure, Mom,” Nicky said between giggles. “Just make sure the next one isn’t so... um, floppy.”

“Noted,” she said dryly, scooping the pancake-taco onto a plate and pouring another dollop of batter into the pan. This time, it spread more evenly, and she felt a tiny flicker of hope. Deciding to change the subject, she turned to him with a smile.

“Alright, future birthday boy. What do you want to do for your birthday this year?” It was still two weeks away, but she needed to start planning now.

Nicky’s laughter faded, and he sat up straighter, his little face suddenly serious. “I want Dad to come,” he said quietly, looking at her with those big, earnest eyes that always made her heart twist.

Agatha froze, the spatula hovering mid-air. She forced a smile, though it felt like a tightrope act. “We’ll see, sweetheart,” she said gently, turning back to the pan. “Anything else?”

Nicky seemed to think for a moment, his brow furrowed in concentration. Then his face lit up. “I want Auntie Nat to come, too!”

Agatha blinked, caught off guard. “Nat? Auntie Nat?”

“Yeah!” Nicky grinned. “She’s the coolest. She tells funny stories and knows all the cool moves.” He jumped off the stool and struck what she assumed was a karate pose, complete with exaggerated sound effects.

Agatha couldn’t help but laugh, even as a pang of nostalgia hit her. Nat, her best friend from college, hadn’t been around much since... well, since everything. But Nicky adored her, and the thought of Nat being there for his birthday didn’t feel as impossible as the other request.

“I’ll call Auntie Nat,” Agatha said, flipping the pancake—successfully this time—and sliding it onto a plate. “But I’m not making any promises about Dad, okay?”

“Okay,” Nicky said, accepting the plate and immediately drowning the pancake in syrup.

As she watched him dig in, her heart ached just a little. Nicky’s wishes were simple, but fulfilling them? That was another story entirely.

Agatha stared at Nicky as he happily drowned his pancake in an absurd amount of syrup. The sight was both endearing and mildly horrifying, but it gave her a moment to think—or at least try to think past the slight throb of her hangover.

Daniel. It had been months since she’d spoken to him directly—without lawyers acting as intermediaries. The divorce had been a war zone of accusations, negotiations, and compromises neither of them truly wanted to make. Split custody was the only thing they agreed on, but even that was a logistical headache. Nicky will spend all his vacations with his dad and the rest of the year with Agatha in Minnesota. The thought of broaching the subject of a birthday gathering made her stomach twist.

She absently flipped another pancake onto a plate, the golden-brown surface a rare victory in her culinary experiment. “Nicky, honey,” she said, her voice soft. “You know your dad is going to see you during your break from preschool in a few weeks. Are you sure you want him to come here for your birthday too?”

Nicky looked up, his face a mix of syrup and sincerity. “Yeah, Mom. Birthdays are special, right? I want everyone to be here. You, Dad, and Auntie Nat. All my favorite people.” he said with a smile. 

Agatha sighed, brushing a strand of damp hair from her face. “I know, sweetie. I’ll try, okay? But I really can’t make any promises about your dad. You know how grown-up things can be... tricky.”

He nodded solemnly, his little face more understanding than she felt he should be at his age. “Okay, but Auntie Nat has to come!”

A small smile tugged at her lips. If there was one person who could distract Nicky from the complexities of grown-up life, it was Natasha Romanoff.

Nat was a force of nature—a neurosurgeon with the sharpest wit and a heart of gold buried under a layer of sarcasm. They had met on the first day of pre-med, bonding over a shared disdain for the professor’s outdated PowerPoint slides. From then on, they were inseparable, navigating med school, the many sleepless nights, and life’s endless curveballs together. By some stroke of luck, they’d even matched into the same hospital for residency, cementing their status as each other’s ride-or-die.

Despite their demanding schedules and living in different cities—Nat still firmly rooted in New York while Agatha had fled to the quiet coast like a drama queen in exile—one thing had always been true: Nat was there for her. Through breakups, career meltdowns, bad haircuts, existential crises, and now, the chaotic aftermath of Agatha's divorce, Nat was her constant. And Agatha, well… had gone completely MIA since she quit her job at NYU two months ago, making her harder to pin down than a sock in the dryer.

“She’ll come,” Agatha said confidently, despite the tiny flicker of guilt gnawing at the back of her mind. Nat was definitely going to be pissed when she got the call—assuming Agatha didn’t chicken out and text instead. Honestly, texting felt safer. Nat’s rant over the phone would be epic. "Are you kidding me, Agatha? Have you lost your damn mind? I swear to God—" Yeah, better to just text. Maybe throw in a cute emoji for good measure.

“Auntie Nat wouldn’t miss your big day for the world,” Agatha reassured her son with a smile, trying to sound convincing while silently preparing herself for Nat’s inevitable wrath. There would be cursing. A lot of cursing. Nat was probably going to call her every name under the sun, maybe even invent a few new ones. And she’d deserve it. But hey, Nat always showed up in the end, even if it was with a dramatic sigh and a fresh set of insults. It was just part of her charm.

Nicky grinned, clearly satisfied, and returned to his pancake massacre.

Agatha, meanwhile, leaned against the counter, her coffee now lukewarm but somehow more palatable than the thought of calling Daniel. How would that even go? Could they manage a civil conversation about their son’s birthday without dredging up all the baggage that still hung between them?

The idea of talking to him directly felt like navigating a minefield, but she couldn’t deny Nicky this small, hopeful request. She’d figure it out. She always did—somehow.

“Mom,” Nicky said around a mouthful of syrup-soaked pancake. “Can I have a blue cake this year? Like, really blue. Like the ocean.”

Agatha chuckled despite herself, ruffling his messy hair. “A blue cake, huh? Consider it done. Just don’t ask me to bake it.”

Nicky giggled, and for a moment, the weight of everything else faded into the background. Whatever hurdles lay ahead—whether they involved ex-husbands or brightly colored cakes—she’d face them. Because if there was one thing she knew how to do, it was survive. And this? This was just another challenge to overcome.

The rest of the day unfolded at a blessedly slow pace, a rarity in Agatha’s usually chaotic life. After breakfast, Nicky insisted on building an elaborate blanket fort in the living room. Agatha joined him, her hangover steadily easing as she laughed at his enthusiastic “engineering.” They spent hours stacking cushions and draping sheets, transforming the space into a fortress worthy of a fairytale. Once the masterpiece was complete, Nicky promptly declared it his “headquarters” and crawled inside with his favorite book and a flashlight.

Agatha took the opportunity to curl up on the couch with her laptop, making a half-hearted attempt at outlining her next research. Her fingers hovered over the keyboard, but her mind wandered. The simplicity of the day was soothing, a balm to her frayed nerves. She let herself enjoy the quiet moments—the sound of Nicky’s muffled giggles, the golden afternoon light spilling through the windows, the faint hum of the coffee maker in the background.

They had a late lunch of grilled cheese sandwiches and tomato soup, Nicky dunking his sandwich with unrestrained enthusiasm. Afterward, they played a board game that quickly devolved into chaos when Nicky decided the rules were “too boring” and made up his own. Agatha let him win, his triumphant cheer making it worth her feigned frustration.

By the time evening rolled around, the blanket fort had become a cozy movie theater. Nicky picked Finding Nemo, and they huddled together under a pile of blankets, munching on popcorn as the characters danced across the screen. Agatha found herself dozing off halfway through, her body finally succumbing to the much-needed rest she hadn’t allowed herself in weeks.

As the credits rolled, Nicky yawned dramatically and declared it was bedtime. Agatha helped him brush his teeth and tucked him in, kissing his forehead as he mumbled about blue cakes and Auntie Nat.

Just as Agatha was settling into her own bed with a book, her phone buzzed. She glanced at the screen to see Wanda’s name flashing.

“Hey,” Agatha answered, leaning back against her pillows.

“Hey yourself,” Wanda’s voice came through, bright and cheerful despite the late hour. “sorry for the time but i wanted to ask you something

“Go ahead” she said calmly

“So, Vision and I are taking the twins to Disney on Ice on Monday, and we thought you and Nicky might want to join us.”

Agatha blinked, surprised. “Disney on Ice?”

“Yeah, it’s supposed to be amazing. The twins are obsessed, and I figured Nicky might enjoy it too. Besides, we could all use a little fun, right?” Wanda’s tone was inviting, but there was a knowing edge to it, as if she’d already guessed Agatha’s initial reluctance.

“Monday?” Agatha hedged, mentally running through her schedule. “I’ve got a shift on Sunday, and so do you. Won’t we both be dead on our feet by then?”

“Probably,” Wanda admitted with a laugh. “But that’s what coffee is for. Come on, Agatha. It’ll be good for Nicky—and for you. Plus, you’ll get to see Vision trying to wrangle two sugar-high six-year-olds while singing along to Let It Go. ”

Agatha couldn’t help but smile at the mental image. “I’ll think about it,” she said finally, her tone noncommittal but not dismissive.

“You better,” Wanda teased. “Let me know so I can get the tickets”

Agatha chuckled. “I’ll let you know.”

They said their goodbyes, and Agatha set her phone down, staring at the ceiling. Disney on Ice wasn’t exactly her scene, but the thought of spending time with Nicky was tempting.

She closed her eyes, letting the quiet of the night envelop her. Maybe, she thought, Monday wouldn’t be so bad after all.

Sunday morning came too soon, the shrill beeping of Agatha’s alarm cutting through the stillness of her bedroom. She groaned, dragging herself out of bed and into the bathroom. As the hot water from the shower pounded against her skin, she allowed herself a few minutes to wake up fully.

Dressed in her signature purple scrubs, her ID badge clipped to the neckline, Agatha tied her hair back into a neat ponytail and grabbed a protein bar from the kitchen. She checked on Nicky, still peacefully asleep in his bed, before double-checking the note she’d left for his babysitter.

She arrived at the hospital with time to spare, the quiet of the early morning a sharp contrast to the chaos she knew would soon erupt. The air outside was crisp, the city just starting to stir. As Agatha swiped her badge at the entrance, her phone buzzed in her pocket.

Wanda: Coffee before shift? Meet me at the café in ten?

Agatha smiled, her steps quickening. She found Wanda at a small table near the café window, already sipping a latte and scrolling through her phone.

“Morning,” Agatha greeted, dropping into the seat across from her.

“Morning,” Wanda replied, her smile bright despite the early hour. She pushed a second cup across the table. “I figured you’d need this.”

“Thank you” Agatha said, taking the cup and inhaling the rich aroma.

They chatted briefly about their weekends, Wanda recounting a comical story about her twins’ attempt at “helping” Vision make homemade pasta. Agatha shared a sanitized version of her lazy Saturday with Nicky, omitting the more emotionally charged moments.

“Well rockstar ” Wanda said, glancing at her watch. “Are you ready to deliver some babies?”

Agatha snorted. “Let’s hope it’s not too crazy today.”

But by mid-morning, it was clear that hope had been futile. The labor and delivery floor was a madhouse. One after another, expectant mothers arrived, their contractions timed perfectly—or so it seemed—to create a non-stop stream of activity.

Agatha barely had time to catch her breath between cases. A first-time mom needed extra reassurance; a veteran with her fourth child delivered so quickly they nearly missed it; and a high-risk patient required careful monitoring and a sudden rush to the operating room for an emergency C-section.

She crossed paths with Wanda several times in the bustling hallways, both of them too busy to do more than exchange quick nods or the occasional grimace of shared exhaustion.

By early evening, Agatha was leaning against the nurse’s station, sipping a lukewarm cup of water when Wanda approached, her hair slightly disheveled but her expression triumphant.

“Five deliveries and counting” Wanda said, collapsing onto the chair beside her. “And it’s not even the full moon. I don't know how you managed ”

Agatha smirked, her shoulders slumping as the adrenaline of the day started to wear off. “I’ve lost track of how many times I’ve washed my hands today.”

“I stopped counting after twenty,” Wanda replied, then glanced at her phone. “Still thinking about tomorrow?”

Agatha chuckled. “I haven’t decided yet. Ask me again after I’ve slept for fourteen hours.”

“Deal,” Wanda said with a laugh, standing to head back to her next patient.

As the shift wore on, Agatha found herself falling into the familiar rhythm of the chaos. It was exhausting, yes, but also oddly grounding. By the time the last baby of her shift was born—a healthy little girl with a surprisingly strong set of lungs—Agatha felt a sense of accomplishment that almost made the aching in her feet worth it.

When the clock finally signaled the end of her shift, Agatha peeled off her gloves and sighed, glancing at the nearby window where the sky had long since gone dark. It had been a long day. And as she headed toward the locker room to change, she couldn’t help but think that maybe tomorrow, a little bit of magic—Disney on Ice and all—wouldn’t be such a bad idea.

As she dragged herself into the locker room at the end of the shift, she found Wanda already there, sitting on the bench and unlacing her shoes with a huff. Her usually composed expression was gone, replaced by an exhausted grimace that screamed What a day.

“Hey,” Agatha said, collapsing onto the bench across from her. She started pulling off her own sneakers, her feet throbbing in protest. “Rough one?”

Wanda let out a sharp, bitter laugh, tossing her boots aside with more force than necessary. “Rough doesn’t begin to cover it,” she muttered, leaning forward to rest her elbows on her knees. “Two motorcycle accidents within an hour of each other. One DOA, the other coded twice in the OR. Oh, and a stabbing because some genius decided to argue about parking spaces. Parking spaces , Agatha. People are insane.”

Agatha raised an eyebrow, pausing mid-motion as she peeled off her socks. “Sounds like a delightful day in trauma land.”

“Delightful doesn’t even cover it,” Wanda deadpanned, rubbing her temples. “And you know what the cherry on top was? The guy who brought in his friend who got stabbed—he thought it was a good time to flirt with me. Flirt! While I was literally trying to clamp an artery right next to him”

Agatha couldn’t help it—she laughed, the absurdity of it cutting through her exhaustion. “Well, at least someone appreciates your charm, Maximoff.”

Wanda rolled her eyes but allowed herself a small, wry smile. “Yeah, great. Next time I’m elbow-deep in someone’s arm, I’ll remember that.”

They sat in companionable silence for a moment, the buzz of the fluorescent lights above them the only sound. Agatha could feel the weight of the day pressing down on her shoulders, but it was oddly comforting to share the space with someone who understood.

“Are you still thinking about Disney on Ice tomorrow?” Wanda asked, breaking the silence.

Agatha leaned back, her head hitting the cool metal of the lockers. “I don’t know. I mean, Nicky would love it, but I’ve got to figure out how to survive the thought of spending my day off surrounded by screaming kids.”

Wanda snorted. “Please, you’re a seasoned mom. You can handle it. Besides, my twins are the loudest of the bunch, and they’re basically pros at this sort of thing. You won’t even have to do anything. Just sit there and pretend you’re having fun.”

Agatha tilted her head to look at her. “You’re really selling it.”

“I’m just saying, it might be nice to do something normal for once,” Wanda said, her tone softening. “I know today was insane, but tomorrow doesn’t have to be. Plus, it’ll make Nicky happy, and that’s worth a few hours of icy theatrics, right?”

Agatha sighed, closing her eyes briefly. “Yeah, you’re probably right.”

“I’m always right,” Wanda quipped, standing and stretching. “Now go home and get some sleep, Agatha. You look like you’ve been run over by a truck.”

“Thanks for the vote of confidence,” Agatha said dryly, but she smiled as she finished changing.

As they left the locker room together, Wanda paused, nudging Agatha with her elbow. “By the way, next time you complain about delivering a dozen babies in a day, remember this: at least none of them tried to flirt with you while you were doing it.”

Agatha laughed, the sound echoing in the quiet corridor. “Fair point, Maximoff. Fair point.”

Before going into the parking lot Agatha made her way down the quiet hallway to the NICU. She wasn’t technically on duty anymore, but the events from a few days ago still weighed on her mind. The baby—the one who had survived that horrific car accident—was occupying more space in her thoughts than she cared to admit.

She paused outside the viewing window, glancing in at the rows of tiny, fragile lives tucked into incubators. When she spotted the baby in question, her heart gave a strange, involuntary tug.

The nurses had done a stellar job keeping her updated. The baby was stable for now, but the road ahead was uncertain. Prematurity compounded by trauma wasn’t an easy hurdle to overcome. Still, Agatha found herself oddly hopeful. Maybe it was because the baby had already beaten impossible odds by surviving the crash, or maybe it was because she needed something to root for after everything else this week.

She entered the NICU, sanitizing her hands and greeting the nurse stationed nearby. “How’s our little fighter doing?” she asked, her voice soft.

The nurse smiled faintly. “Holding steady. We managed to reduce the ventilator settings a bit earlier today. Small steps, but good ones.”

Agatha approached the incubator, peering down at the tiny baby swaddled in blankets, tubes and wires surrounding its fragile body. The sight was heartbreaking, but she forced herself to focus on the positives.

“You’re a stubborn one, aren’t you?” she murmured, her voice so low it was almost a whisper. “Keep fighting, little one. You’ve got a world waiting for you.”

After a few minutes, she left the NICU and took the elevator up to the ICU. Her steps slowed as she approached the room where the baby’s mother lay. The woman was still in a coma, her vitals stable but unchanging. Agatha stood in the doorway for a moment, hesitating before stepping inside.

The room was eerily quiet, save for the steady beep of the monitors and the soft whoosh of the ventilator. Agatha’s hands found her pockets as she took a seat by the bedside.

“You’ve got a little fighter down in the NICU,” she said softly, her eyes on the woman’s still form. “They’re hanging in there. I thought you should know.”

There was no response, of course. The machines continued their rhythmic work, indifferent to Agatha’s words. She sat there for a few more moments, the weight of the day pressing down on her. Then she rose, her chair scraping softly against the floor as she pushed it back. Still, she felt compelled to speak.

“Rest up,” she said quietly. “Your baby’s waiting for you.”

As she walked out of the room, she couldn’t shake the thought that this woman and her baby were somehow tied to her now, their stories tangled up with hers in a way she hadn’t anticipated. It wasn’t her case anymore—not officially—but she knew she wouldn’t stop checking in. Not until she had answers.

Agatha slid into her car, the engine humming to life as she buckled in. Before heading home, she quickly typed out a text to Wanda.

“Alright, count us in for Disney on Ice. Let’s do it.”

She hit send, a small smile tugging at her lips. It felt good to give in to something fun for Nicky

Wanda’s reply came quickly.

“Yay! We’re gonna have so much fun. I’ll send you the details when i get home”

Agatha read the message with a slight chuckle. 

“Alright, I’ll send the money to you later today!”

With a deep breath, she started the drive, the familiar route home ahead of her. It was going to be a long weekend, but at least there was something to look forward to.

As Agatha drove through the quiet streets, her mind wandered to the days since her move to Minnesota. The chill in the air, the unfamiliar roads, and even the people felt like a new chapter she wasn’t entirely sure she was ready for. But as she thought about the night with Wanda and Mariah at the bar, the unexpected invitation to Disney on Ice for her and Nicky, and the simple act of settling into something unfamiliar, a small flicker of hope sparked. 

Maybe, just maybe, moving here wasn’t such a bad idea. It wasn’t home yet, not completely, but it was a fresh start. And for the first time in a long while, Agatha felt a faint possibility that things could, perhaps, get better.



Chapter 10

Chapter Notes

The devil works hard, but my Christmas break editing works harder. Enjoy!

See the end of the chapter for more notes

Monday morning was crisp as Agatha bundled up Nicky in his warm jacket before walking him out to the daycare garden. His small hand was tucked into hers as he eagerly chattered about his plans for the day. Agatha smiled softly, watching him run ahead toward his friends, already lost in the world of the playground. She gave him a quick wave, then turned to head back to the house.

The quiet of the house felt oddly comforting. Agatha moved through the kitchen, making a quick cup of coffee before starting on the small chores she hadn’t gotten to earlier in the weekend. Though she had a housekeeper who cleaned and cooked for her, Agatha couldn’t quite shake the habit of doing little things around the house when she had the time—putting away dishes, wiping down counters, making sure the house was in order. It helped her feel grounded, even if it was just for a short time.

With everything in place, she glanced at the clock and mentally prepared herself for the next task—picking up Nicky. She had a surprise planned for him, something she hadn't told him about yet. A small part of her enjoyed the secrecy, watching him squirm with curiosity when she mentioned it. It was a spontaneous plan, but after the chaos of the last few weeks, Agatha knew they both needed something fun, something light.

After a few final touches, Agatha grabbed her keys and headed for the door, her mind already on the excitement of picking up Nicky. She couldn’t wait to see his reaction. She could already picture his face lighting up when she told him they were going to Disney on Ice.

Agatha arrived at the daycare and parked just outside the building. She could see Nicky through the window, his face pressed against the glass as he spotted her car pulling up. A wide grin spread across his face, and he was already racing toward the door before it even fully opened.

"Mom!" he shouted, practically bouncing on his feet as he burst outside, his little arms stretched wide for a hug.

Agatha smiled as she opened the door, crouching down to catch him. "Hey, buddy. How was your day?"

"Good! I painted a zebra today!" Nicky's excitement was contagious, and Agatha felt a surge of pride. He had such a bright spark in his eyes that it made everything feel worth it.

"I’m glad to hear that," Agatha replied, standing up and taking his hand. "Now, I’ve got something special for you."

Nicky’s eyes widened, curiosity sparking immediately. "A surprise?"

Agatha gave him a mysterious smile. "Yep. But you have to be patient and not ask too many questions."

He huffed, dramatically crossing his arms. "Is it something cool? Like a toy or ice cream?"

Agatha shook her head, trying to hide her smile. "You’ll see when we get there. It’s better than all of that."

"Better than ice cream?" Nicky’s voice went up a notch in disbelief.

"Way better," she teased, ruffling his hair.

They climbed into the car, and Agatha started the engine, glancing back at him through the rearview mirror. "So, what do you think the surprise could be?"

Nicky stared out the window for a moment, thinking. "Hmm... a trip to the zoo?" He looked back at her, eyes sparkling with hope.

"Maybe," Agatha said with a playful wink, "but not today."
