Chapter 13

Chapter Notes

Um... yeah, probably not what you were expecting, but enjoy, I guess! (it will make sense later trust :) )

See the end of the chapter for more notes

She forced herself to move, her feet carrying her out of the bathroom and back into the bar. She didn’t stop to say goodbye to Mariah or Alice or anyone else. The need to escape, to get out of that suffocating space, overwhelmed everything else.

Her legs felt shaky as she stepped outside, the cool night air hitting her like a slap. She gulped it down, trying to calm the storm raging inside her, but her thoughts were relentless. Rio, with some random man, in the bar they had just been drinking in. The audacity, the carelessness of it—it made her want to scream.

When Stephen followed her out of the bar, his presence felt like a grounding force, but it didn’t quite settle her. Not when she couldn’t get the sound of her moaning out of her head. He stood beside her, quiet, understanding, as if he knew she needed space. He didn’t try to kiss her or make any moves, just waited in silence as she scrolled through her phone, barely noticing when she ordered an Uber.

Stephen’s voice startled her out of her spiraling thoughts. “Agatha?” he said gently, his concern evident.

She looked at him, her vision blurring slightly from the alcohol and the sheer force of her emotions. “I’m fine,” she muttered, the words bitter on her tongue. “Just tired. I had too much to drink.”

Stephen didn’t push, his gaze steady and understanding. He stayed beside her as she fumbled with her phone.

“Are you sure you're okay?” Stephen asked quietly, his voice low, cautious. Agatha looked at him, not sure how to respond, not sure if she was okay or if she even knew what that meant anymore.

Agatha managed a weak smile, though it didn’t reach her eyes. “I’ll live,” she said, the words more for herself than for him.

When the Uber pulled up, Stephen waited until she climbed in before stepping back. She didn’t look at him as the car drove away, her thoughts too loud and chaotic to focus on anything else.

By the time she reached her house, the lights of the city were a dizzying blur, matching the swirling mess of emotions inside her. She thanked the driver distractedly, barely registering his response as she stumbled up the steps to her door.

Once inside, the silence felt deafening. She leaned against the door, her forehead pressed to the cool wood, her breathing uneven.

The anger still burned hot, but beneath it was something far worse—a dull, aching emptiness she couldn’t shake. She clenched her fists, willing herself to let it go, but she felt the weight of it all crash down on her. All she felt was the dull, aching realization that she was alone again. 

Alone, and haunted by the sound of Rio’s voice.

—---------

Agatha felt the warmth first, like sunlight filtering through a canopy of trees, caressing her skin in slow, deliberate waves. It wasn’t just a sensation; it was a presence, heavy and comforting yet charged with an undercurrent of tension. The air around her seemed thicker, humming faintly with an invisible energy, drawing her in, holding her captive. 

Then came the touch. Soft, deliberate, like silk brushing against her bare shoulders, but with an intensity that made her shudder. It moved with purpose, tracing patterns that ignited sparks wherever it landed, as if each stroke of unseen fingers was an invocation of something primal. Her breath hitched, her body alive in a way that felt raw and untamed, every nerve lit with a tantalizing awareness. She couldn’t see anything, but the sensation was too vivid, too immediate to question.

She stood there, weightless, her senses heightened and every sound muted except for the rhythmic pulse of her own heartbeat. A faint warmth prickled at her skin, growing hotter, almost unbearable, as if the darkness itself was alive and reaching for her. It moved over her like a tide, sweeping through her, leaving her trembling and breathless. She wasn’t afraid, not exactly, but a strange, electric anticipation coiled in her chest, drawing her deeper into the void without resistance. It was as if the shadows whispered her name, pulling her closer to something unseen, something that made her skin hum with awareness and longing.

She was surrounded by shadow, her body tingling with a heat she couldn’t escape. Gentle hands moved over her skin, their touch maddeningly light at first, teasing the barest edges of sensation before pressing with firmer intent, tracing lines that sent shivers through her, the touch both delicate and insistent, like a painter sculpting her out of smoke and fire. They explored her, tracing the curve of her shoulders, the line of her spine, and the dip of her waist. Each motion felt deliberate, as though she were being mapped, memorized. The hands lingered, tracing the lines of her collarbone, then down the curve of her arms, sparking shivers that cascaded down to her fingertips.

Her breath came in shallow gasps as the hands slid lower, their touch featherlight but unrelenting, tracing the curve of her hip, the tender flesh of her thighs, before moving back up in slow, deliberate strokes. Every nerve in her body was alight, raw and exposed, begging for more even as she felt herself unraveling. Her body arched instinctively toward the touch, a silent plea she couldn’t contain, as though her very essence was being unraveled, thread by thread.

The touch continued, teasingly slow, unraveling her layer by layer. She needed this. She needed more.

Fingers brushed against the sensitive hollow of her neck, then slid over her chest with an excruciatingly deliberate pressure, making her pulse race. Her breath hitched again as those phantom hands caressed the curve of her ribs, the flat of her stomach, and lower still, each motion measured and exact, designed to push her further into the haze of sensation.

She whimpered softly, the sound foreign and obscene to her own ears. It escaped her lips unbidden, raw and unrestrained, a reflection of the turmoil building inside her. The hands on her body, so light and teasing moments before, suddenly grasped her tight and held her still. A warm body pressed against her back, firm and solid, anchoring her to the darkness as her mind spiraled into chaos.

Her breath hitched as fingers slid over her heat, sending waves of pleasure through her body. She was so close, her need nearly overwhelming her.

But she was still missing something.

She wanted to speak, to demand an answer, but no words came. Her lips parted, but all she could manage was a strangled gasp. Instead, a voice whispered from the darkness, low and commanding, each word striking like a match in the thick blackness.

“ That’s it, Agatha, ” it said, smooth and intoxicating, wrapping around her like a velvet chain. “Let go for me.”

Her pulse quickened, the voice curling through her like smoke, intoxicating and relentless, leaving her trembling and confused. The timbre of it brushed against a memory she couldn’t quite grasp, familiar yet elusive, achingly so. The sensation of closeness, of being held, consumed her completely, and yet she felt an ache, a hollowness, as though something vital was missing.

Her lips parted, desperate to call out, to ask, to beg—but the voice dissolved into silence, and the hands that held her vanished into the void. The warmth faded, replaced by an aching coldness that left her breathless and yearning.

Before she could make sense of the longing and fear tangled in her chest, her eyes snapped open. She bolted upright in bed, her heart pounding so violently it felt as though it might shatter. Her room was bathed in pale moonlight, serene and indifferent, a stark contrast to the lingering heat and shadow that still clung to her skin like a phantom. Her head throbbed, and she realized she was still in her clothes from last night, the tight black sleeves clinging to her skin, her feet bare now, though she didn’t remember taking off her boots.

The memories of the bar hit her in scattered bursts. Stephen’s warm hands guiding hers as they lined up a shot, the soft clink of pool balls breaking apart, the dim glow of neon lights casting shadows across his face. And then Rio. 

Always Rio. 

Her intense gaze had cut through the haze of alcohol like a blade, sharp and unrelenting. Agatha remembered the way Rio had watched her, how her eyes seemed to see right through her. And then the sound. The unmistakable sound of Rio’s voice in the bathroom, smooth and low, echoing faintly through her mind. Her stomach twisted.

Agatha ran a hand through her hair, her breathing uneven as she tried to shake off the weight pressing down on her chest. The dream… was it just her mind playing cruel tricks? Or was it something more?

She told herself it was nothing. A dream, a vivid one, yes, but nothing more. Yet her body betrayed her. She felt it—deep in her chest, a warmth that began to spread outward, seeping into her limbs and pooling low in her stomach. It wasn’t the alcohol. It wasn’t the creeping tendrils of a hangover. It was something else, something unnameable, something that left her weak and unsteady.

Her fingers gripped the edge of the bed as the heat grew stronger, making her feel feverish, dizzy, as though the room itself were tilting under her. Her heart raced, every beat reverberating through her body like a drum. What the hell was happening? She closed her eyes, desperate to steady herself, but the sensation only deepened. It was as if the dream hadn’t ended, as if the voice and the touch had followed her into waking life, leaving her raw and exposed, her nerves alight with sensations she couldn’t explain.

“Get a grip,” she muttered to herself, her voice barely audible, but even as she spoke, it wavered, betraying the confusion and unease roiling within her. She hated this feeling—this vulnerability, this sense that she wasn’t in control.

Agatha swung her legs over the side of the bed, trying to ground herself, but even the coolness of the floor against her bare feet couldn’t shake the lingering heat coiling inside her. She pressed her palms against her face, desperate for clarity, for something tangible to hold onto. But her thoughts were as slippery as water, her emotions a chaotic tangle of fear, frustration, and something darker, something she wasn’t ready to name.

The question loomed, heavy and unavoidable: was this about Stephen? The way his hand had lingered on hers, the way he’d smiled at her, the easy warmth in his touch? Or was it something deeper, something she didn’t want to confront?

With a frustrated sigh, Agatha stripped off her clothes, tossing them carelessly onto the floor. The sleek black outfit she’d worn to the bar—the one that had made her feel bold, confident, untouchable—now felt like a prison. The fabric clung to her damp skin like a second skin, suffocating her. She needed to shed it, to get it off, as if doing so could strip away the confusion that clung to her. She grabbed an old pair of pajamas from the back of her drawer, soft and familiar, the kind that sagged in all the right places. It wasn’t much, but it was a small comfort, a fragile shield against the storm raging inside her.

She climbed back into bed, pulling the covers tightly around herself like armor. She willed herself to forget—to erase the dream, the heat, the way her body still thrummed with a pulse she couldn’t seem to control. But no matter how tightly she shut her eyes, she couldn’t escape the nagging sensation between her legs, the unmistakable dampness that refused to be ignored.

Her cheeks burned with shame as she buried her face in her hands, her teeth sinking into her bottom lip to stifle the noise that threatened to escape. She clenched her fists, angry at herself, angry at her body for betraying her like this.

This was ridiculous. It had to be the alcohol. That sharp whiskey buzz, the haze of too many cocktails, the way everything at the bar had felt heightened and surreal—it had to be that. And maybe… maybe Stephen’s lingering touches had stirred something in her. The way his fingers had lingered on hers, the casual intimacy of his hand brushing her lower back—it wasn’t much, but it was enough to remind her of a time when touch had been familiar, comforting, even thrilling. It had been over a year since she’d had sex, after all, and more than that since she’d felt truly desired by someone. 

That had to be it. 

She was just touch-deprived, that’s all. A body starved of physical contact would respond to anything, wouldn’t it?. It was biological, she told herself. A purely physical response to human contact. She was overthinking it, letting her mind spiral. Touch was primal, instinctive. Her body didn’t care about context or reason; it was just reacting. It was natural to feel this way, wasn’t it? Even if it left her feeling raw and vulnerable, exposed in a way she hated. 

But even as she clung to that explanation, trying to calm the racing of her heart, a small, stubborn voice in the back of her mind whispered that this wasn’t about Stephen, at least not entirely. Stephen’s touch had been warm, yes, but it hadn’t lit her up from the inside. It hadn’t left her trembling, her skin still humming hours later, or made her wake up drenched in heat and desire. Whatever had caused this feeling went beyond a simple lack of contact. It was something darker, something that refused to be rationalized away.

This was about the dream. The voice. The hands that had touched her so intimately, so expertly, like they knew her body better than she did. The way her name had rolled off lips she couldn’t see but could still feel.

Her breath hitched, her chest tightening as the memory of it flooded back in vivid detail. It wasn’t just a dream. It couldn’t be. But if it wasn’t… What was it?

Agatha sat up abruptly, rubbing her temples as if she could scrub the intrusive thoughts from her mind. Her skin felt clammy, a faint sheen of sweat clinging to her despite the early morning chill creeping through the room. She needed to cool down. To clear her head. To make herself stop feeling like this.

With a frustrated groan, she swung her legs over the side of the bed and padded to the bathroom, her bare feet silent against the hardwood. The light above the mirror buzzed faintly as it flickered on, its harsh brightness stinging her eyes, but she didn’t care. Her movements were automatic as she turned the shower knobs, setting the water to as cold as it would go.

The spray hit the porcelain tiles with a sharp hiss, already fogging up the mirror. Without hesitation, Agatha stepped under the icy deluge. The shock was instant. She gasped, her body jolting as the freezing water hit her skin like tiny needles. Her breath came in sharp bursts, and for a moment, all she could do was stand there, bracing herself against the chill. It was unbearable—and exactly what she needed.

As the cold water poured over her, it stripped away the lingering haze of sleep and heat, forcing her to focus on the present. Her fingers curled against the tile wall for balance, the slick surface cool beneath her touch. The shock of the water should have numbed her, dulled everything, but instead, it brought the ache into sharper relief.

She hadn’t been touched—really touched—in over a year. She’d told herself it didn’t matter, that she didn’t care. But the truth hit her now, stark and undeniable, as the ache of loneliness pressed against her chest like a weight she couldn’t lift. Her body, traitorous and desperate, remembered things she’d tried to forget.

Her forehead fell against the cold tiles, her wet hair plastered to her cheeks. It wasn’t like she missed Daniel. God, no. He was a mistake she’d spent years unraveling, a wound she’d sewn shut and refused to reopen. But tonight’s fleeting brushes of contact, the alcohol, the dream—they’d messed with her head, stirred something she hadn’t even realized was dormant. She was vulnerable, touch-deprived, and her subconscious had latched onto something, anything. It wasn’t real. 

It couldn’t be real.

The idea of the dream being something deeper sent a jolt of fear through her. No. She wouldn’t let herself spiral. Couldn’t. Whatever had happened in that dream was a fluke, nothing more than a symptom of exhaustion and isolation.

And yet, as she stood under the relentless cascade of icy water, the denial she clung to felt fragile, like a sheet of glass cracking under the weight of something far too heavy to hold. The cold couldn’t extinguish the heat coiling deep within her, or the unsettling sensation that the dream had been more than just her mind playing tricks.

—---------

Agatha woke with a start, her head pounding viciously, the kind of pain that felt as though her skull might split in two. Her mouth was dry as sandpaper, her stomach churning with nausea that threatened to send her running to the bathroom. She groaned, attempting to shift, but even the simple motion of lifting her head sent waves of dizziness rolling through her.

“Mama!” Nicky’s excited voice pierced through the haze, his small hands shaking her shoulder insistently. “It’s Pancake Saturday!”

She barely opened her eyes, squinting against the bright light filtering through the curtains. It was too much—everything was too much. Agatha buried her face into the pillow, groaning. “Nicky,” she rasped, her voice dry and gravelly, “Mama’s not feeling well today. How about… pancakes tomorrow?”

“But it’s Saturday,” he insisted, his tone teetering on the edge of a whine. “Pancake Saturday, Mama!”

Agatha forced herself to sit up, her movements slow and stiff. The pounding in her head only intensified as she squinted at her son’s hopeful face. Guilt twisted her gut, but she could barely keep herself upright, let alone think about flipping pancakes.

“I know, buddy,” she managed weakly, pressing a hand to her temple. “But Mama’s really sick today. How about cereal instead? You love cereal, right?”

Nicky pouted, crossing his arms over his chest. “But you promised,” he muttered before stomping out of the room in frustration.

Agatha flopped back onto the bed, groaning again. She wanted to disappear into the mattress, to let it swallow her whole. Every part of her ached, and the nausea wasn’t easing. After a few moments, she forced herself up, her feet dragging as she shuffled toward the bathroom. Splashing cold water on her face, she took a deep breath, willing the sickness to subside. It didn’t.

Where was her phone?

The thought jolted her slightly more awake. She reached for the nightstand, but it was empty. Frowning, she checked her pockets—still nothing. Panic began to creep in. What if she’d lost it at the bar? What if someone else had it? A million embarrassing possibilities raced through her foggy mind.

She stumbled back into the bedroom, her bleary eyes scanning every corner, but the phone was nowhere to be found. Her brain felt like a muddled mess, struggling to reconstruct the events of the night before. She remembered playing pool with Stephen, laughing at his jokes, his warm hand guiding hers as she lined up shots. The bathroom incident lingered. But after that? Nothing.

She remembered the dream, the shower later, the water scalding her skin as she tried to scrub away thoughts she didn’t want to admit were lingering. 

Her stomach growled angrily, reminding her of Nicky downstairs, likely sulking by now. Guilt tugged at her, and despite her shaky legs, she dragged herself toward the kitchen.

When she walked in, she froze. There, sitting on the counter by the coffee machine, was her phone. Relief flooded her for a moment—until she picked it up and unlocked the screen.

15 New Messages

Her stomach sank as she scrolled through them. Most were from Mariah.

“Girl, you left with the hottie?!”
“I saw you two playing pool, you’re welcome, by the way ”
“Hope you had fun... in more ways than one ;)”

Agatha groaned aloud, her face heating with embarrassment. “Oh, God, no,” she muttered to herself.

Mariah’s teasing continued relentlessly as she scrolled.

“You better tell me everything. Don’t leave me hanging!”
“You owe me details. ALL the details.”

Then the final message, sent early that morning: “Hope you got lucky, babe. You deserve it.”

Agatha wanted to throw her phone across the room. Her mind raced, trying to piece together what had happened after she left the bar. Did she even say goodbye to her friends? She vaguely remembered Stephen walking her out, his hand on her back, steadying her tipsy steps. But after that? Nothing.

The thought made her stomach flip, not from nausea this time, but from sheer mortification. She had no idea what Mariah thought she knew—or worse, what Stephen might have said to them before they left. God has he even talked to them? All memories were blurry in her mind. And then there was the dream. The heat. The touch. It lingered, unsettling and too vivid, like an unwelcome shadow following her into waking life.

Agatha closed her eyes for a moment, her head pounding and her stomach twisting in protest at the thought of standing in the kitchen. The idea of making pancakes felt impossible. She could barely manage to get herself out of bed, let alone stand over a hot stove. The smell of food made her stomach churn, and the thought of flipping pancakes—especially with Nicky's eager little face watching her—seemed too much to handle in her current state.

With a heavy sigh, she made a decision. “Alright, Nicky,” she called out, wiping a hand over her face. “How about we go out for pancakes today? I think Mama needs a break.” The moment the words left her mouth, she instantly regretted them. But Nicky’s face lit up like a Christmas tree, his excitement palpable. It was worth it, she told herself. One more day of powering through. After all, she was his mom, and she had to get up, even when she felt like death warmed over.

“Pancakes at a café! Yay!” Nicky shouted, already rushing to get his shoes on. Agatha sighed again, feeling guilty that she didn’t have the energy to make this Saturday special in the way she'd promised, but at least a café would give them both a change of pace. Plus, if she sat still long enough, maybe the warmth of the place would ease the throbbing in her skull.

Agatha barely managed to drag herself through the motions of getting ready. Her head pounded relentlessly, and every step felt like wading through molasses. Food was the last thing on her mind, but Nicky’s persistence was relentless. After a lukewarm shower that did little to ease her lingering nausea, she threw on a pair of sunglasses to shield her bloodshot eyes and pulled her hair into a haphazard bun. Nicky, bundled up in his favorite jacket and scarf, tugged at her hand impatiently.

The café they ended up at was quaint, its interior filled with the soft hum of morning conversations and the comforting scent of fresh coffee and pancakes. Large bay windows framed a picturesque view of the ice-skating rink outside, where a handful of skaters twirled and glided under the crisp winter sunlight.

Nicky’s eyes lit up the moment they stepped inside. “Mama, look!” he exclaimed, pointing toward the rink. His face was pressed against the glass before they even made it to their table, leaving a foggy smudge on the pristine surface. Agatha sighed, her stomach sinking with regret. Of course, this was where she decided to bring him.

Inside, the warmth of the café was a welcome relief from the biting cold outside. Nicky immediately dove into the menu, his excitement growing by the second. He ordered chocolate chip pancakes with extra whipped cream, practically bouncing in his seat as he waited. Agatha, on the other hand, stuck to coffee, cradling the warm mug between her hands like it was the only thing tethering her to reality.

As Nicky chattered away about school, superheroes, and everything in between, his gaze kept drifting back to the rink. Between bites of pancake, he suddenly stopped mid-sentence and pressed his face to the glass again, watching as a young woman executed a graceful spin.

“Mama,” he said, turning back to her with wide, hopeful eyes, “can we do that for my birthday? Please? I want to skate!”

Agatha sighed, leaning back in her chair. “Nicky, you can’t just hop on the ice like that. It’s not something you learn overnight.”

“Then I want lessons!” he insisted, bouncing in his seat again. “Like Disney on Ice! Wanda said it was cool”

Agatha groaned, shooting a glare into her coffee. Of course Wanda said that. “I’m going to kill Wanda,” she muttered under her breath. “This is her fault.”

Once breakfast was over, Nicky could hardly contain his excitement. As they stepped outside, he bolted toward the ice rink, his little legs carrying him faster than Agatha could manage, especially with her throbbing headache.

 “Nicky!” Agatha called, panic flaring as she hurried after him, her voice strained and raspy from the pounding in her skull. The cold air didn’t help, and every step felt like a small victory against the dizzying wave of discomfort.

A young rink attendant, likely in his early twenties, stepped forward to intercept the boy before he could get too close to the ice.

“Whoa there, buddy,” he said with a soft laugh. “You can’t just jump on the ice without skates or a grown-up. You’ve gotta be careful, alright?”

Nicky, already bouncing on his heels, pouted but didn’t give up. “I wanna skate like Disney on Ice!” he declared, eyes wide with hope.

The worker chuckled, glancing up at Agatha, who was just catching up to them, breathing a little heavier than she would’ve liked. “He’s got big dreams,” the worker said, his grin widening.

Agatha, still holding onto Nicky’s hand as she knelt down beside him, sighed in a dry, tired tone. “You have no idea,” she said, trying to catch her breath. “Come on, kiddo. Let’s leave the rink to the professionals today.”

“But, Mama—” Nicky began, but she cut him off gently, giving his hand a reassuring squeeze.

“Another time,” she said firmly, leading him away from the rink. Her head was pounding more intensely now, and all she wanted was to get back to the warmth of the car and disappear into the silence of their home.

As she guided Nicky back toward the exit, the young worker stood up, brushing a light layer of frost from his gloves. “Actually,” he called after them, “we run skating lessons here every weekend. I teach the beginners myself. If your little guy’s interested, you should check it out sometime. He’d love it.”

Agatha paused for a moment, forcing a polite smile as she steered Nicky closer. “Thanks,” she said, her voice tight, “I’ll think about it.” She didn’t have the energy to commit to anything right now.

But Nicky wasn’t ready to give up. “Mama! Please? I wanna skate like them!” He pointed at a pair of skaters effortlessly gliding across the ice in perfect harmony.

Agatha’s smile was tight. “We’ll see,” she said, her tone final, signaling that the conversation was over for now.

The worker smiled warmly at Nicky’s enthusiasm and gave him a friendly wave. “I’m here every Saturday morning if you change your mind. Have a good day, buddy.”

Nicky waved back eagerly, still buzzing with excitement, as Agatha, feeling the weight of the moment, steered him away. The entire walk back to the car was filled with his relentless pleading, making Agatha regret ever stepping foot near the rink in the first place.

Once they got home, Agatha practically collapsed onto the couch, her body heavy with exhaustion. Her head throbbed relentlessly, the aftermath of the hangover clinging to her like a fog. Nicky, oblivious to her misery, busied himself with his toys, while Agatha dragged herself to her laptop, hoping to get a few things done.

Opening her email, her eyes landed on the subject line: Trauma Certification: Required Follow-Ups – Dr. Calderu . Her stomach twisted in an instant.

"Oh, for fuck’s sake," she muttered under her breath, clicking it open.

The email contained a detailed outline of additional readings, research, and a joint assignment she was supposed to complete with Rio in order to plan the trauma certification. Attached were case studies they were required to review together with the first year residents. Agatha stared at the screen, her stomach sinking. Of all the things she wanted to deal with today, this was right at the bottom of the list. She groaned.

Nicky ran up to her holding a drawing, but Agatha barely registered it. Her mind was already spiraling. Not only would she have to face Rio again soon, but now they’d be working closely together—again.

With a sigh, Agatha closed her laptop, rubbing her temples in a futile attempt to ease the headache. “This day just keeps getting better,” she said to no one in particular, feeling the weight of everything pressing down on her.

The rest of the day blurred into a haze of forced productivity. Desperate to distract herself, Agatha threw herself into preparing for Nicky’s upcoming birthday party. She gathered construction paper, markers, and stickers, crafting simple but cheerful invitations for Nicky’s daycare friends. Every moment she spent on the invites felt like a small victory, a break from the chaos in her head.

She carefully wrote each name in bold letters, pausing occasionally to sip the coffee she’d made, the bitterness cutting through the fog in her mind. She didn’t know how other parents made this look so effortless. Every part of it felt like a struggle.

Midway through, Nicky wandered into the room, curious. “Mama, what are you doing?” he asked, peering over her shoulder.

Agatha quickly slid the half-finished invitations out of sight, her smile forced but warm. “It’s a surprise,” she said, ruffling his hair. “Birthday stuff.”

Nicky’s eyes lit up. “For me?”

“Who else?” she teased, tapping him on the nose.

He grinned, his enthusiasm a brief balm for her frazzled nerves. Then, after a beat, his face grew thoughtful. “Are Tommy and Billy coming?”

Agatha froze for a second, the question hanging in the air. She plastered on another smile. “I’m working on that, bud.”

Her stomach sank as she realized she’d have to call Wanda. The memory of Mariah’s teasing at the bar the night before was bad enough, and now she’d have to face Wanda’s inevitable questions. The idea made her want to crawl under the table and never come out. But an invitation was an invitation, and Nicky clearly wanted his friends there.

With a resigned sigh, she steeled herself. “Why don’t you go play? I’ll handle it,” she told Nicky, ushering him out of the room before retreating to her bedroom.

She closed the door behind her and sat on the edge of the bed, staring at her phone like it might bite her. Her thumb hovered over Wanda’s number for a moment before she finally dialed, letting out a deep breath as the line rang.

Wanda picked up on the second ring, her voice lilting with amusement. “Agatha,” she said, drawing out the name in a way that already made Agatha regret this decision. “I was wondering when I’d hear from you.”

Agatha closed her eyes, grimacing. “Wanda, hi. I, uh, wanted to talk to you about something.”

“Oh, sure,” Wanda replied, her tone far too innocent. “But first… how’s the hangover?”

Agatha groaned, flopping back onto the bed. “I’m going to kill Mariah.”

Wanda laughed, the kind of laugh that made it clear she was enjoying this far too much. “Oh, you’ll have to get in line. She called me first thing this morning and gave me all the juicy details. Said you were the life of the party last night.”

“I was not,” Agatha grumbled, already dreading what Mariah might have shared.

“Oh, but you were,” Wanda insisted, her voice positively gleeful. “Mariah said you were holding court at the bar like some kind of queen, tossing back whiskey like it was water. And then, of course, there was the mysterious man…”

“There was no-” Agatha started, but Wanda steamrolled over her, clearly enjoying herself.

“And I heard,” Wanda continued, her voice rich with mock scandal, “that you practically had the bartender begging for mercy. Something about ‘I know your game, sweetie,’ and then leaving with this so-called ‘mystery man.’ Care to elaborate on that? ”

Agatha groaned, pressing the heels of her hands to her eyes. “There was no ‘mystery man.’ Mariah is a pathological exaggerator.”

“Oh, sure, Agatha,” Wanda teased. “That’s why she said you were sneaking out, stumbling out like a teenager sneaking home after curfews with this mysterious man,.”

Agatha sat up, glaring at the ceiling as if Wanda could feel the weight of her annoyance through the phone. “I didn’t sneak out. I left like a responsible adult.”

“With a random guy in tow?” Wanda quipped, and Agatha could hear the grin in her voice. “Who was it? A dashing stranger? A rugged sailor? Some poor soul who didn’t know what he was getting into?”

“I didn’t leave with anyone!” Agatha snapped, though there was no real heat in her voice. “Mariah is just trying to make me miserable.”

Wanda hummed, clearly unconvinced. “Uh-huh. Well, if you change your story, let me know. I’ve got popcorn at the ready.”

Agatha pinched the bridge of her nose, wishing the earth would swallow her whole. “I’m never drinking again.”

“Sure, sure,” Wanda teased, laughing. “I’ve heard that one before. Honestly, Agatha, I don’t know why you don’t go out more often if you’re going to give us this much material every time.”

“Can we focus?” Agatha snapped, though her tone was more exasperated than angry. She could hear Wanda’s stifled laughter on the other end, and she took a deep breath to steady herself.

“Fine,” Wanda said, though her voice was still tinged with amusement. “What’s up?”

Agatha exhaled, trying to compose herself. “Nicky’s birthday is next Saturday. I’m throwing him a party, and he specifically asked if Tommy and Billy could come. I thought I’d extend the invitation to you and Vision, too.”

There was a pause, followed by an excited gasp from Wanda. “That’s so sweet! Of course we’ll come! The boys will be thrilled.”

Agatha let out a small sigh of relief, though Wanda’s enthusiasm was almost overwhelming. “Great. I’ll send you the details.”

“Anything I can bring?” Wanda asked, her voice practically bubbling with energy. “Snacks? Drinks? Oh, I can make one of those Pinterest cakes—”

“Wanda, it’s fine,” Agatha interrupted, smiling despite herself. “Just bring yourselves.”

“Alright,” Wanda agreed, and Agatha could almost hear the grin in her voice. “And Agatha?”

“What?”

“I’m glad you got lucky yesterday. You deserve it.”

Agatha sputtered, her face instantly heating. “I didn’t—Wanda, for the love of—”

“Relax,” Wanda interrupted, clearly savoring Agatha’s discomfort. “I’m just saying, if anyone needs a little fun in their life, it’s you. Whether it’s a man or even just yourself…” She trailed off with a mischievous chuckle.

“Goodbye, Wanda,” Agatha said sharply, her tone a mix of mortification and amusement as she hung up the phone.

For a moment, she just sat there, staring at the device in her hand, a bemused smile tugging at her lips despite herself. Wanda always had a way of getting under her skin, but maybe that was why Agatha couldn’t bring herself to stay mad at her for long.

As she set the phone down, the faint smile lingered. One thing down, a thousand more to go. At least the party was one step closer to being organized. Now, she just had to survive the rest of the week—and maybe figure out how to ignore the way Wanda’s teasing made her stomach twist, just a little, in ways she didn’t want to dwell on too deeply.

Agatha sank onto the couch, her head falling back against the cushions as she tried to focus on her breathing. The room felt too still, too quiet, yet her mind wouldn’t stop spinning. Rio’s voice from the bathroom still echoed faintly in her ears, stirring emotions she didn’t want to name. And then there was Stephen—his steady presence, his kindness—a maddening contrast to the chaos Rio always left in her wake.

It all felt tangled, like a knot she couldn’t unravel, no matter how hard she tried. Agatha pressed her palms to her eyes, desperate for clarity, but the more she tried to make sense of it all, the less grounded she felt. Everything blurred together—Stephen’s calm gaze, Rio’s infuriating smirk, the sharp ache of loss and the overwhelming fatigue of trying to hold it all together.

She exhaled slowly, hoping for some quiet, but her thoughts filled the silence, relentless and loud, leaving her more exhausted than before. 

Memories hovered at the edges of her mind, threatening to drag her back to the humiliating reality of what she’d done and how…

No. 

She refused to let herself go there. 

Not tonight. 

Not ever.

Chapter 14

Agatha’s dreams were vivid again, too real, too close. She could feel phantom hands trailing over her body, their touch insistent and warm. Her breath hitched as the sensation grew more defined—lips brushing against her shoulder, a smirk pressed to her back, lingering kisses that ignited a heat beneath her skin. She couldn’t see the person, but their presence was overwhelming, inescapable. A familiar feeling, a longing buried deep within her, rose to the surface, and she felt herself leaning into it, unable to pull away.

Then, with a jolt, she was awake, gasping for air. Her heart thudded violently in her chest, drowning out all other sounds. The room was dark and eerily silent, but the weight of the dream clung to her, thick and suffocating, as if the phantom touch still lingered in the air. She sat up slowly, disoriented, blinking at the darkness around her. It took a few moments to steady her breathing, to convince herself that it was just a dream. But the residue of it—the heat, the sensation, the phantom presence—refused to fade.

What was happening to her? This was the second night in a row she’d had a dream like that, and it was starting to unnerve her.

Agatha’s cheeks burned, not just from the residual heat of the dream, but from the overwhelming flood of guilt that followed. Her mind spiraled as she tried to make sense of it.

Why now? Why this? 

It had been years since she’d felt anything like that, and her body was betraying her, reacting to something she couldn’t even name. She felt a twinge of panic—what was she becoming? She clenched her fists, frustration bubbling up to the surface, threatening to overflow.

But then she felt it. A dampness between her thighs, unmistakable, undeniable. She squeezed her eyes shut in mortification, wishing she could erase the sensation from her body, from her mind. For fuck’s sake , she thought. The surge of arousal—of need—made her skin burn with shame. She wasn’t supposed to feel this way. She clenched her fists, fighting the urge to scream into the quiet of the room. 

Her frustration boiled over, a whirlwind of emotions she couldn’t quite pin down—anger, confusion, shame—and before she could stop herself, her hand slid beneath the waistband of her pajama pants. But the moment her fingers brushed against the undeniable evidence of her arousal, she froze. A sick, twisting feeling churned in her stomach, and she yanked her hand away as if she’d been burned. Her breath came in shallow, frantic bursts, her chest tight with a mix of guilt and disgust. No. I’m not doing this, she thought, pressing her palms flat against the mattress to anchor herself.

She sat there for a moment, her heart racing and her thoughts spiraling out of control. The idea of giving in to whatever this was felt... wrong. She hadn’t done this in years—not since she was much younger, experimenting out of a vague curiosity or trying to soothe the ache of loneliness. But even then, it had never felt like a release. Masturbating had always been more of an empty gesture, one that left her feeling hollow rather than satisfied. It wasn’t something she enjoyed or even thought about often.

The shame settled deep in her bones now, made worse by how out of control she felt. Was this what she had been reduced to? Letting her body betray her over things she couldn’t understand or explain? She shook her head fiercely, trying to banish the thoughts clawing at the edges of her mind. She swung her legs over the side of the bed, her resolve hardening. There was no way she could stay there, trapped in the suffocating aftermath of her emotions. She needed to move, to do something—anything—to pull herself out of this spiral.

Cold shower. Now.

She could still feel the heat rising in her body, the tension coiling in her gut, but the guilt came crashing down harder, suffocating every ounce of desire. Her chest tightened as she stood, stomping toward the bathroom, the cold tile a harsh contrast to the warmth of her skin. The urge to just let go, to give in, was powerful, but she refused. She couldn’t—she wouldn't—let herself fall into this trap again.

She turned the shower on, the cold water pouring down in a rush, forcing her body to react. The chill was a sharp contrast to the heat that still simmered beneath her skin, but it was the only way to break the spell. 

As the water cascaded over her, Agatha stood there for a long moment, letting the coldness numb her, trying to wash away the remnants of the dream, the guilt, the shame. It was just a dream. It didn’t mean anything. She had control over her body. She had control over this. But deep down, something lingered, a quiet ache that she couldn’t quiet, a reminder that some desires, some feelings, couldn't be so easily ignored.

The icy spray was a shock to her system, but she welcomed it. Anything to drown out the lingering sensations from the dream and the confusing mess of emotions swirling in her chest. Anger at herself for losing control. Confusion about why she was having these dreams in the first place. Guilt for the rush of pleasure she’d felt in the moment, even though she’d had no idea who—or what—her mind had conjured.

As the cold water dripped down her back, the memory of Friday night hovered at the edges of her mind, threatening to drag her back to the humiliating reality of what she’d done.

She clenched her fists, shaking her head sharply.  But even as she tried to avoid it her thoughts betrayed her, circling back again and again to the hushed, intimate sounds she couldn’t unhear. Each time, she shoved them aside, burying them deeper, but they lingered, a persistent echo in the quiet of her house.Her mind betrayed her, taking her to the sounds that had rooted her to the spot, about the man with Rio. 

And how she had stayed there. 

Listening.

For far longer than she should have. 

Just the thought of it made her stomach churn, the shame of her own actions twisting alongside the anger still simmering in her chest.

Agatha froze, the reality of the situation slamming into her like a freight train. Her heart thudded painfully in her chest as she strained to hear more, her breath shallow and uneven. The sounds filtering through the thin wall of the bathroom weren’t just unmistakable—they were intimate, raw, and unapologetic.

She could feel every word like a dagger aimed right at her chest. The last thing she had expected was to find herself here, eavesdropping on something that felt far too personal. 

 

Rio was still here, in the bar, and from the sound of it, she wasn’t alone.

"Fuck," Rio’s voice murmured, low and breathless, each word laced with something that made Agatha’s stomach churn.

She stood paralyzed for a moment, torn between walking out and pretending she hadn’t heard anything, and confronting the situation head-on. The last thing she expected tonight was to hear that. The anger in her stomach twisted as she fought against the temptation to turn around and leave, to retreat into the darkness of the bar where she didn’t have to confront this. But it was too late. She had already heard enough.

As she took a breath to steady herself, Rio moaned again, the sound unmistakable. It was louder now.

The fucking nerve. Her nails dug into her palm as her thoughts spiraled. She didn’t know what she’d expected—Rio’s audacity knew no bounds—but this? The way her chest burned with anger and something she didn’t want to name made her want to punch something.

Agatha’s hand trembled on the doorknob, but she couldn’t bring herself to open it. She was caught in the surreal pull of the moment, the shocking realization that Rio, of all people, was fucking in a bars bathroom.

The urge to escape the bathroom—to leave the situation behind and walk away—was strong. But instead, she took another step back, deciding to listen longer. She couldn’t stop herself from feeling... something.

She knew the sound of Rio’s voice,it had haunted her for the past two weeks, and tried to escape it every single time. 

And yet, here she was, eavesdropping like some kind of voyeur, unable to tear herself away.

She stayed frozen, waiting, listening.

“Right there,” Rio’s voice whispered, softer this time, though it still managed to sting, like a taunt meant just for her.

The seconds after the realization blurred into a haze, the sound of Rio’s voice still echoing in her head. Agatha’s thoughts were fragmented, broken up by flashes of frustration and disbelief. The anger bubbled up, but it was wrapped in confusion, and she could barely process what had just happened. What had she just heard? 

The images, the sounds, felt like they were searing into her mind, but all she could do was stand frozen, her body a mixture of shock and guilt. Why was it so hard to breathe?

She didn’t know how long she stood there, but at some point, her feet began to move, and without thinking, she slipped out of the bathroom, the conversation still echoing in her mind, clouding everything. 

Agatha’s stomach twisted, her anger and frustration coiling tighter and tighter until she felt like she might explode. She clenched her teeth so hard it made her jaw ache.

She bit the inside of her cheek, her fists trembling. It wasn’t just the betrayal she felt—because there was no real betrayal, was there? It wasn’t as though Rio owed her anything. No, what made her blood boil was the fact that she felt anything at all. And that the guilt she felt kept taking her to this memory.

She forced herself to focus on something else, anything else. She thought about Nicky. His bright, eager smile. His excitement at the idea of ice skating. Maybe she’d been too quick to dismiss it earlier.

When she finally stepped out of the shower, her hair dripping and her skin chilled, she felt marginally calmer. Sleep was clearly out of the question, so she grabbed her laptop and settled onto the couch.

She typed “figure skating lessons near me” into the search bar, her fingers hesitant at first. The results popped up quickly, and she scrolled through the options, scanning the descriptions and reviews. Most of the rinks were far, but one caught her eye: a rink just a few blocks from Nicky’s daycare. It offered lessons for kids as young as three and promised a patient and encouraging environment.

Agatha bookmarked the page, feeling a flicker of something close to relief. It wasn’t a bad idea. Maybe it would be fun for him—and for her, a distraction. She made a mental note to check it out with him tomorrow.

For now, she closed her laptop and leaned back, staring at the ceiling. Her body was tired, but her mind refused to quiet down. Whatever was happening to her, she needed to get a handle on it. And fast.

Morning came too soon, as it always did. Agatha groaned, her body stiff and her head still foggy from yet another restless night. Dragging herself out of bed, she padded to Nicky’s room and coaxed him awake with the promise of a fun outing. After much negotiation—bribery in the form of pancakes—they quickly got ready and headed out.

The skating rink buzzed with activity, the crisp morning air blending with the faint smell of coffee from a nearby concession stand. Nicky pressed his hands and face against the glass barrier surrounding the rink, his eyes sparkling with curiosity as he watched the skaters glide effortlessly across the ice.

“Look, Mama!” he exclaimed, his voice filled with awe. “They’re skating so fast!”

Agatha smiled despite herself, tucking her hands into her coat pockets. “Yeah, buddy. They’re pretty good, huh?”

A young woman with a bright smile and a clipboard approached them, her upbeat energy making Agatha feel sluggish by comparison. “Hi there! Welcome to Starlight Rink. First time here?”

Agatha nodded. “Yeah. Me and my son, Nicky, wanted to check it out.”

“Well, you picked a great day! We offer lessons every day with instructors for all age groups. There’s actually a beginner’s class starting in about twenty minutes if you’re interested.”

Nicky gasped, turning to Agatha with wide, pleading eyes. “Mama! Can I? Please, please, please?”

“We’ll see,” Agatha said cautiously, glancing back at the young woman. “Can we just watch for now?”

“Of course! But let me introduce you to the instructor for the next class,” the woman said, gesturing for them to follow. “He’s fantastic with kids.”

Agatha followed her reluctantly, keeping a hand on Nicky as his excitement made him bounce with every step.

The woman led them to the edge of the rink, where a young man with dark hair and a kind smile was lacing up his skates. He looked up as they approached, and his eyes widened with recognition. They've already met. He was at the rink outside the coffee yesterday. 

“William! This is Nicky and his mom—”

“Doctor Agatha Harkness,” he finished, standing up and extending a hand to her. “I know who you are.”

Agatha blinked, caught off guard. The pieces clicked into place, and she groaned inwardly. “Oh. Right. William, isn’t it?”

“William Kaplan,” he confirmed with a laugh, extending his hand again. “Small world, huh? Sorry if I seemed over-eager. I’m a pre-med student, and I teach skating here to help cover tuition. But, honestly, meeting you is kind of surreal.”

Agatha tilted her head, narrowing her eyes slightly. “Surreal?”

“Yeah,” William admitted, rubbing the back of his neck, suddenly looking a little shy. “You’re practically a legend in neonatal surgery. I’ve read your research papers. They’re, uh, pretty intense.” His smile widened. “In a good way. They’re part of why I’m pursuing medicine. It’s kind of incredible to meet you in person. I actually recognized you yesterday but... well, I didn’t want to intrude.” He hesitated, his smile sheepish. “You looked busy, and I figured it wasn’t the best time to, you know, gush about how much I admire your work.”

Agatha’s eyes narrowed slightly, suspicion mixing with discomfort. She wasn’t used to being recognized outside of a hospital setting.

His gaze shifted to Nicky, and his smile softened. “Well, if it isn’t the little buddy that wanted to skate on disney on ice,” 

Nicky flushed shyly and ducked behind Agatha’s leg, peeking out at William with a tiny smile.

William crouched to Nicky’s level, keeping his tone warm and friendly. “So, what do you think? Want to try skating today?”

Nicky hesitated, chewing his lip. “I dunno...”

Agatha hesitated, her protective instincts flaring. “I don’t know...”

William stood, grinning. “Great! Let’s get you some skates, Nicky. Come on, I’ll help you pick the right size.”

As she leaned against the glass, sipping a hot coffee she’d grabbed from the concession stand, she couldn’t help but smile as Nicky listened attentively to William’s instructions.

For a brief moment, the chaos of the past few days faded into the background, replaced by the sight of her son taking his first tentative steps onto the ice. It wasn’t perfect—he wobbled, arms flailing—but William was there, steady and patient, encouraging him every step of the way.

Agatha couldn’t help but smile despite the nagging weight in her chest. This was the first time in a while she’d seen him so engaged in something, so determined. He looked so small on the ice, but there was something undeniably sweet about how he fought to stay upright.

William skated beside him, offering gentle words of encouragement, and it made Agatha’s heart swell with a strange mix of pride and gratitude. Nicky was so brave, and William seemed to have a natural way of connecting with him. The instructor’s presence was reassuring, even though Agatha still couldn’t shake the nervous tension that lingered in her body. There was always a part of her that hovered on high alert, especially with Nicky’s safety. But the way William had handled him so far eased her concerns.

But for Agatha, it was something else entirely.

Alone time.

Her fingers tightened around her arms, as if trying to hold herself together. Between work, Nicky’s needs, and the weight of her own complicated emotions, there had been precious little time for herself. Between the meetings with her colleagues, the constant juggling of Nicky’s care, and the endless pile of responsibilities, she’d been drowning. This could be the key to finding a sliver of peace. If Nicky could have a structured, regular activity, and if she could manage it around her schedule, maybe—just maybe—she could carve out an hour a day where she didn’t have to be mom or Dr Harkness

The thought seemed so simple, yet so impossible. But then again, this skating thing was a perfect fit. It was an hour of physical activity that could give Nicky a break from his usual routine while also giving Agatha time to breathe. His nanny could drop him off, pick him up afterward, and—most importantly—she wouldn’t have to worry about making sure everything at home was in perfect order. Maybe it was because she hadn’t allowed herself this kind of luxury in so long.

The private lessons can really help kids improve quickly since the instructor tailors everything specifically to their needs.”

And considering how chaotic her life had been lately, giving Nicky a consistent activity—and giving herself an hour or two of quiet—was worth every penny.
